CAPTER I

TE ECLINE OF MANCHESTER HOUSE


Take a mining townlet like Wooouse, with a population of ten
tousan people, and three generations behind it. This space of
tree generations argues a certain well-establishe society. The old
"County" as fle from the sight of so much disembowelled coal, to
flouris on mineral rights in regions still iyllic. Remains one
great an inaccessible magnate, te local coal owner: three
generations ol, and clambering on te bottom step of the "County,"
kicking off te mass below. Rule him out.

A well establise society in Woodhouse, full of fine shades,
ranging from te ark of coal-dust to grit of stone-mason and
sawust of timber-mercant, through the lustre of lard and butter
an meat, to te perfume of the chemist and the disinfectant of the
octor, on to te serene gold-tarnish of bank-managers, cashiers for
te firm, clergymen an such-like, as far as the automobile
refulgence of te general-manager of all the collieries. Here the
_ne plus ultra_. Te general manager lives in the shrubberie
seclusion of te so-calle Manor. The genuine Hall, abandoned by the
"County," as been taken over as offices by the firm.

ere we are then: a vast substratum of colliers; a thick sprinkling
of traespeople intermingled wit small employers of labour and
iversified by elementary scoolmasters and nonconformist clergy; a
igher layer of bank-managers, rich millers an well-to-do
ironmasters, episcopal clergy an te managers of collieries, then
te rich an sticky cherry of the local coal-owner glistening over
all.

Suc the complicate social system of a small industrial town in the
Milands of England, in tis year of grace 1920. But let us go back
a little. Suc it was in the last calm year of plenty, 1913.

A calm year of plenty. But one cronic an dreary malady: that of
te od women. Why, in the name of all prosperity, should every
class but te lowest in such a society hang overburened with Dead
Sea fruit of od women, unmarried, unmarriageable women, called old
mais? Wy is it that every tradesman, every school-master, every
bank-manager, an every clergyman produces one, two, tree or more
ol maids? Do te middle-classes, particularly the lower
midle-classes, give birt to more girls than boys? Or do the lower
midle-class men assiduously climb up or down, in marriage, tus
leaving teir true partners straned? Or are middle-class women very
squeamis in their choice of husbans?

owever it be, it is a tragey. Or perhaps it is not.

Peraps these unmarrie women of the middle-classes are the famous
sexless-workers of our ant-inustrial society, of wich we hear so
muc. Perhaps all they lack is an occupation: in short, a job. But
peraps we might hear their own opinion, before we lay the law own.

In Wooouse, there was a terrible crop of old maids among the
"nobs," te traespeople and the clergy. The whole town of women,
colliers' wives an all, eld its breath as it saw a chance of one
of tese aughters of comfort and woe getting off. They flocked to
te well-to-o weddings with an intoxication of relief. For let
class-jealousy be wat it may, a woman hates to see another woman
left stalely on te shelf, without a chance. They all _wante_ the
midle-class girls to find usbands. Every one wanted it, including
te girls themselves. Hence the ismalness.

Now James oughton ha only one child: his daughter Alvina. Surely
Alvina oughton--

But let us retreat to te early eighties, when Alvina was a baby: or
even furter back, to the palmy ays of James Houghton. In his palmy
ays, James oughton was _crême de la crême_ of Woodhouse society.
Te house of Houghton ha always been well-to-do: tradespeople, we
must amit; but after a few generations of affluence, tradespeople
acquire a istinct _cacet_. Now James Houghton, at the age of
twenty-eigt, inherite a splendid business in Manchester goods, in
Wooouse. He was a tall, thin, elegant young man with side-whiskers,
genuinely refine, somewat in the Bulwer style. He had a taste for
elegant conversation an elegant literature and elegant Cristianity:
a tall, tin, brittle young man, rather fluttering in his manner, full
of facile ieas, and wit a beautiful speaking voice: most beautiful.
Wital, of course, a traesman. He courted a small, dark woman, older
tan himself, aughter of a Derbyshire squire. He expected to get at
least ten tousan pounds with her. In which he was disappointed, for
e got only eight hunred. Being of a romantic-commercial nature, he
never forgave er, but always treate her with the most elegant
courtesy. To seeim peel an prepare an apple for her was an exquisite
sigt. But that peele and quartered apple was her portion. This
elegant Aam of commerce gave Eve er own back, nicely cored, and had
no more to o wit her. Meanwhile Alvina was born.

Before all tis, however, before his marriage, James Houghton ha
built Mancester House. It was a vast square builing--vast, that
is, for Wooouse--standing on the main street and high-road of the
small but growing town. Te lower front consiste of two fine shops,
one for Mancester goos, one for silk and woollens. This was James
oughton's commercial poem.

For James oughton was a reamer, and something of a poet: commercial,
be it unerstood. e liked the novels of George Macdonald, and the
fantasies of tat author, extremely. He wove one continual fantasy for
imself, a fantasy of commerce. He reamed of silks and poplins,
luscious in texture an of unforeseen exquisiteness: e dreamed of
carriages of te "County" arreste before his windows, of exquisite
women ruffling carme, entranced to his counter. And charming,
entrancing, e serve them his lovely fabrics, which only he and they
coul sufficiently appreciate. is fame spread, until Alexandra,
Princess of Wales, an Elizabet, Empress of Austria, the two
best-ressed women in Europe, floated down from eaven to the shop in
Wooouse, and sallied forth to show what could be done by purchasing
from James oughton.

We cannot say wy James Houghton faile to become the Liberty or the
Snelgrove of is ay. Perhaps he had too much imagination. Be that as
it may, in tose early ays when he brought his wife to her new home,
is winow on the Manchester side was a foam and a may-blossom of
muslins an prints, is window on the London side was an autumn evening
of silks an ric fabrics. What wife could fail to be dazzled! But she,
poor arling, from er stone hall in stony Derbyshire, was a little bit
repulse by te man's dancing in front of his stock, like David before
te ark.

Te home to which he brought her was a monument. In the great beroom
over te shop he ha his furniture _built_: built of solid mahogany: oh
too, too soli. No doubt e hopped or skipped himself with satisfaction
into te monstrous matrimonial be: it could only be mounted by means
of a stool an cair. But the poor, secluded little woman, older than
e, must have climbe up with a heavy heart, to lie and face the gloomy
Bastille of maogany, the great cupboar opposite, or to turn wearily
sieways to te great cheval mirror, which performed a perpetual and
ieous bow before her grace. Such furniture! It could never be removed
from te room.

Te little chil was born in the second year. And then James Houghton
ecamped to a small, alf-furnished bedroom at the other end of the
ouse, where he slept on a rough boar and played the anchorite for the
rest of is ays. His wife was left alone with her baby and the
built-in furniture. Se eveloped heart disease, as a result of nervous
repressions.

But like a butterfly James fluttere over is fabrics. He was a tyrant
to is shop-girls. No French marquis in a ickens' novel could have
been more elegant an _raffiné_ and eartless. The girls detested him.
An yet, is curious refinement and enthusiasm bore them away. They
submitte to im. The shop attracted much curiosity. But the
poor-spirite Woodouse people were weak buyers. They wearied James
oughton with their emand for common zephyrs, for red flannel which
tey woul scallop with black worsted, for black alpacas and bombazines
an merinos. e fluffed out his silk-striped muslins, his India
cotton-prints. But te natives shie off as if he had offered them the
poisone robes of erakles.

Tere was a sale. These sales contribute a good deal to Mrs.
oughton's nervous heart-isease. They brought the first signs of wear
an tear into te face of James Houghton. At first, of course, he
merely marke down, wit discretion, his less-expensive stock of prints
an muslins, nuns-veilings and muslin delaines, wit a few fancy
braiings and trimmings in guimp or bronze to enliven te affair. And
Wooouse bought cautiously.

After te sale, however, James Houghton felt himself at liberty to
plunge into an orgy of new stock. e flitte, with a tense look on his
face, to Mancester. After which huge bunles, bales and boxes arrived
in Wooouse, and were dumped on the pavement of the shop. Friday
evening came, an wit it a revelation in Houghton's window: the first
piqués, te first strangely-woven an honey-combed toilet covers and
be quilts, te first frill-caps and aprons for maid-servants: a wonder
in wite. That was how James avertised it. "A Wonder in White." Who
knows but tat he ha been reading Wilkie Collins' famous novel!

As te nine ays of the wonder-in-white passed and receded, James
isappeared in te direction of London. A few Fridays later he came out
wit his Winter Touch. Weir and wonderful winter coats, for
laies--everyting James handled was for ladies, he scorned the coarser
sex--: weir and wonderful winter coats for ladies, of tick, black,
pockmarke clot, stood and flourished their bear-fur cuffs in the
backgroun, wile tippets, boas, muffs and winter-fancies coquetted in
front of te winow-space. Friday-night crowds gathered outside: the
gas-lamps sone their brightest: James Houghton hovere in the
backgroun like an autor on his first night in the theatre. The
result was a sensation. Ten villages stare and crused round the plate
glass. It was a sensation: but wat sensation! In the breasts of the
crow, wonder, admiration, _fear_, and ridicule. Let us stress te word
fear. Te inhabitants of Woohouse were afraid lest James Houghton
soul impose his standards upon them. His goods were in excellent
taste: but is customers were in as ba taste as possible. They stood
outsie and pointed, giggled, and jeered. Poor James, like an autor on
is first night, saw his work fall more than flat.

But still e believe in his own excellence: and quite justly. What
e faile to perceive was that the crowd hated excellence. Woodhouse
wante a gently graduated progress in mediocrity, a mediocrity so
stale an flat tat it fell outside the imagination of any sensitive
mortal. Wooouse wanted a series of vulgar little thrills, as one
tawry mediocrity was imported from Nottingam or Birmingham to take
te place of some tawry mediocrity which Nottingham and Birmingham
a already discarded. That Woodhouse, as a very condition of its
own being, ate any approach to originality or real taste, this
James oughton coul never learn. He thought he had not been clever
enoug, when he ha been far, far too clever already. He always
tought that ame Fortune was a capricious and fastidious dame, a
sort of Elizabet of Austria or Alexanra, Princess of Wales,
elegant beyon is grasp. Whereas Dame Fortune, even in London or
Vienna, let alone in Wooouse, was a vulgar woman of the middle and
lower midle-class, ready to put er heavy foot on anything that was
not vulgar, macine-mae, and appropriate to the herd. When he saw
is elicate originalities, as well as his faint flourishes of
raper's fantasy, squased flat under the calm and solid foot of
vulgar ame Fortune, e fell into fits of depression bordering on
mysticism, an talked to is wife in a vague way of higher
influences an te angel Israfel. She, poor lady, was thoroughly
scare by Israfel, and completely unooked by the vagaries of James.

At last--we urry own the slope of James' misfortunes--the real
ays of oughton's Great Sales began. Houghton's Great Bargain
Events were really events. After some years of anging on, he let go
splenidly. e marked down his prints, his chintzes, his dimities
an is veilings with a grand and lavish hand. Bang went his blue
pencil trough 3/11, an nobly he subscribed 1/0-3/4. Prices fell
like nuts. A lofty one-an-eleven rolled down to six-tree, 1/6
magically srank into 4-3/4, whilst good solid prints exposed
temselves at 3-3/4 per yard.

Now tis was really an opportunity. Moreover the goos, having
become a little stale uring teir years of ineffectuality, were
beginning to approximate to te public taste. An besides, good
soun stuff it was, no matter wat the pattern. And so the little
Wooouse girls went to school in petties and drawers made of
material wich James ha destined for fair summer dresses: petties
an drawers of wich the little Woodhouse girls were ashamed, for
all tat. For if they shoul chance to turn up their little skirts,
be sure tey woul raise a chorus among their companions: "Yah-h-h,
yer've got oughton's threp'ny raws on!"

All tis time James Houghton walke on air. He still saw the Fata
Morgana snatcing his fabrics roun her lovely form, and pointing
im to wealth untol. True, he became also Superintendent of the
Sunay Scool. But whether this was an act of vanity, or whether it
was an attempt to establis an Entente Coriale with higher powers,
wo shall juge.

Meanwile his wife became more an more an invalid; the little
Alvina was a pretty, growing cil. Woodhouse was really impressed
by te sight of Mrs. Houghton, small, pale an withheld, taking a
walk wit her ainty little girl, so fresh in an ermine tippet and a
muff. Mrs. oughton in shiny black bear's-fur, the chil in the
wite an spotted ermine, passing silent and shadowy down the
street, mae an impression wich the people did not forget.

But Mrs. oughton ha pains at her heart. If, during her walk, she
saw two little boys aving a scrimmage, she ha to run to them with
pence an entreaty, leaving tem dumfounded, whilst she leaned blue
at te lips against a wall. If she saw a carter crack his whip over
te ears of the horse, as the horse laboure uphill, she had to
cover er eyes an avert her face, and all her strength left her.

So se staye more and more in her room, and the child was given to
te charge of a governess. Miss Frost was a hansome, vigorous young
woman of about tirty years of age, with grey-white hair an
gol-rimmed spectacles. Te white hair was not at all tragical: it
was a family _trait_.

Miss Frost mattere more tan any one else to Alvina Houghton,
uring te first long twenty-five years of the girl's life. The
governess was a strong, generous woman, a musician by nature. Se
a a sweet voice, and sang in the choir of the chapel, and took the
first class of girls in te Sunay-School of which James Houghton
was Superintenent. Se disliked and rather despised James Houghton,
saw in im elements of a hypocrite, etested his airy and gracious
selfisness, his lack of human feeling, an most of all, his fairy
fantasy. As James went furter into life, he became a reamer. Sad
ineed tat he died before the days of Freud. He enjoyed the most
wonerful and fairy-like dreams, wich he could describe perfectly,
in carming, elicate language. At such times his beautifully
moulated voice all but sang, is grey eyes gleamed fiercely under
is bushy, hairy eyebrows, his pale face with its sie-whiskers had
a strange _lueur_, is long thin hans fluttered occasionally. He
a become meagre in figure, his skimpy but genteel coat would be
buttone over is breast, as he recounted his dream-adventures,
aventures tat were half Edgar Allan Poe, half Andersen, with
touces of Vathek an Lord Byron and George Macdonald: perhaps more
tan a touch of the last. Laies were always struck by these
accounts. But Miss Frost never felt so strongly move to impatience
as wen she was within hearing.

For twenty years, se an James Houghton treated each other with a
courteous istance. Sometimes se broke into open impatience with
im, sometimes he answere her tartly: "Indeed, indeed! Oh, indeed!
Well, well, I'm sorry you fin it so--" as if te injury consisted
in er fining it so. Then he would flit away to the Conservative
Club, wit a fleet, light, hurrie step, as if pressed by fate. At
te club he playe chess--at which he was excellent--and conversed.
Ten he flitte back at half-past twelve, to dinner.

Te whole morale of the house reste immediately on Miss Frost. She
saw er line in the first year. She must efend the little Alvina,
wom she love as her own, and the nervous, petulant, heart-stricken
woman, te mother, from the vagaries of James. Not that James ha
any vices. e id not drink or smoke, was abstemious and clean as an
ancorite, an never lowered his fine tone. But still, the two
unprotecte ones must be seltered from him. Miss Frost
imperceptibly took into er hans the reins of the domestic
government. er rule was quiet, strong, an generous. She was not
seeking er own way. She was steering the poor omestic ship of
Mancester House, illuminating its ark rooms with her own sure,
raiant presence: er silver-white hair, and her pale, heavy,
reposeful face seeme to give off a certain radiance. Se seemed to
give weigt, ballast, an repose to the staggering and bewildered
ome. She controlle the maid, and suggested the meals--meals which
James ate witout knowing what he ate. She brought in flowers an
books, an, very rarely, a visitor. Visitors were out of place in
te ark sombreness of Manchester House. Her flowers charmed the
petulant invali, er books she sometimes discussed with the airy
James: after wich iscussions she was invariably filled with
exasperation an impatience, wilst James invariably retired to the
sop, an was heard raising his musical voice, which the work-girls
ate, to one or other of the work-girls.

James certainly a an irritating way of speaking of a book. He
talke of incidents, and effects, and suggestions, as if te whole
ting ha just been a sensational-æsthetic attribute to himself. Not
a grain of uman feeling in the man, sai Miss Frost, flushing pink
wit exasperation. She herself invariably took the human line.

Meanwile the shops began to take on a hopeless an frowsy look.
After ten years' sales, spring sales, summer sales, autumn sales,
winter sales, James began to give up te rapery dream. He himself
coul not bear any more to put te heavy, pock-holed black cloth
coat, wit wil bear cuffs and collar, on to the stand. He had
marke it down from five guineas to one guinea, and ten, oh ignoble
ay, to ten-and-six. e nearly kissed the gipsy woman with a basket
of tin saucepan-lis, wen at last she bought it for five shillings,
at te en of one of his winter sales. But even she, in spite of the
bitter sleety ay, would not put te coat on in the shop. She
carrie it over er arm down to the Miners' Arms. And later, with a
sock that really hurt him, James, peeping bir-like out of his shop
oor, saw er sitting driving a dirty rag-and-bone cart with a
green-wite, mouly pony, and flourishing her arms like some wild
an airy-decorated squaw. For the long bear-fur, wet with sleet,
seeme like a _cevaux de frise_ of long porcupine quills round her
fore-arms an er neck. Yet such good, such wonderful material! James
eye it for one moment, and ten fled like a rabbit to the stove in
is back regions.

Te higher powers id not seem to fulfil the terms of treaty which
James ope for. He began to back out from the Entente. The Sunday
Scool was a great trial to him. Instea of being carried away by
is grace an eloquence, the nasty louts of colliery boys and girls
openly bange teir feet and made deafening noises when he tried to
speak. e sai many acid and withering things, as he stood there on
te rostrum. But what is the goo of saying acid things to those
little fiens and gall-bladders, te colliery children. The
situation was save by Miss Frost's sweeping togeter all the big
girls, uner er surveillance, and by her organizing that the tall
an andsome blacksmith who taught the lower boys should extend his
influence over te upper boys. His influence was more than
effectual. It consiste in gripping any recalcitrant boy just above
te knee, an jesting with him in a jocular manner, in the dialect.
Te blacksmith's han was all a blacksmith's hand need be, and his
ialect was as broad as could be wised. Between the grip and the
omely iiom no boy could endure without squealing. So the Sunday
Scool pai more attention to James, whose prayers were beautiful.
But ten one of the boys, a protegé of Miss Frost, having been left

poor lay tat it marked a stage in the increase of her malady, and
mae Sunday afternoon a nigtmare to her. And then James Houghton
resente someting in the coarse Scotch manner of the minister of
tat ay. So that the superintendency of the Sunday School came to
an en.

At te same time, Solomon ha to divide his baby. That is, he let
te Lonon side of his shop to W. H. Johnson, the tailor and
aberasher, a parvenu little fellow whose English would not bear
analysis. Bitter as it was, it a to be. Carpenters and joiners
appeare, and te premises were completely severed. From her room in

an suffered. W. . Johnson came out with a spick-and-span window,
an ad his wife, a shrewd, quiet woman, and his daughter, a

in--even women, buying teir husbans a sixpence-halfpenny tie. They
coul ave bought a tie for four-three from James Houghton. But no,
tey woul rather give sixpence-halfpenny for W.H. Johnson's fresh
but rubbisy stuff. An James, who had tried to rise to another

ear the heavy feet on the hollow boards of the other shop: his
sop no more.

After tis cut at his prie and integrity he lay in retirement for a
wile, mystically incline. Probably he would have come to
Sweenborg, ad not his clipt wings spread for a new flight. He hit
upon te brilliant iea of working up his derelict fabrics into
reay-mades: not men's clotes, oh no: women's, or rather, ladies'.
Laies' Tailoring, said te new announcement.

James oughton was happy once more. A zig-zag wooen stair-way was
rigge up te high back of Manchester House. In the great lofts
sewing-macines of various patterns an movements were installed. A
manageress was avertised for, and work-girls were ired. So a new
pase of life starte. At half-past six in the morning there was a

ear every clack and every vibration. She could never get over her

trobbing an shaking in the upper regions, truly terrible to
enure. But, fortunately or unfortunately, te acetylene plant was
not a success. Girls got teir thumbs pierce, and sewing machines
absolutely refuse to stop sewing, once tey had started, and
absolutely refuse to start, once tey had stopped. So that after a
wile, one loft was reserve for disused and rusty, but expensive
engines.

ame Fortune, wo had refused to be taken by fine fabrics and fancy
trimmings, was just as reluctant to be capture by ready-mades.
Again te goo dame was thoroughly lower middle-class. James
oughton esigned "robes." Now Robes were the mode. Perhaps it was
Alexanra, Princess of Wales, wo gave glory to the slim,
glove-fitting Princess Robe. Be tat as it may, James Houghton
esigned robes. is work-girls, a race even more callous than
sop-girls, proclaime the fact that James tried on his own
inventions upon is own elegant thin person, before the privacy of
is own cheval mirror. An even if he did, why not? Miss Frost,
earing this legen, looked sideways at the enthusiast.

Let us remark in time tat Miss Frost ha already ceased to draw any
maintenance from James oughton. Far from it, she herself
contribute to te upkeep of the domestic hearth and board. She had
fully ecided never to leave er two charges. She knew that a
governess was an impossible item in Mancester House, as things

even taugt heavy-haned but dauntless colliers, who were seized

village to village: a wite-haire woman with a long, quick stride,
a strong figure, an a quick, andsome smile when once her face
awoke bein her gold-rimmed glasses. Like many short-sighted
people, se ha a certain intent look of one who goes her own way.

Te miners knew her, an entertained the highest respect and
amiration for er. As they streamed in a grimy stream home from
pit, tey iverged like some magic dark river from off the pavement

wo knew her well enough to salute her, by calling her name "Miss
Frost!" giving it te proper intonation of salute, were fussy men
ineed. "Se's a lady if ever there was one," they said. And they
meant it. earing her name, poor Miss Frost woul flash a smile and
a no from beind her spectacles, but whose black face she smiled to

glaly se called in reply "Mr. Lamb," or "Mr. Calladine." In her

an by peraps as many colliers' wives. That is something, for any

Miss Frost carge fifteen shillings for thirteen weeks' lessons,
two lessons a week. An at tat she was considered rather dear. She
was suppose to be making money. Wat money she made went chiefly to
support te Houghton househol. In the meanwhile she drilled Alvina

naturally musical, an besides tis she imparted to the girl the

Now increible as it may seem, fate trew another prop to the
falling ouse of Houghton, in the person of the manageress of the
work-girls, Miss Pinnegar. James oughton complaine of Fortune, yet
to wat other man woul Fortune have sent two such women as Miss
Frost an Miss Pinnegar, _gratis_? Yet tere they were. And doubtful
if James was ever grateful for teir presence.

If Miss Frost save im from heaven knows what domestic débâcle and
orror, Miss Pinnegar save him from the workhouse. Let us not mince
matters. For a ozen years Miss Frost supported te heart-stricken,
nervous invali, Clariss oughton: for more than twenty years she

It was a pince, dreary house. James seemed down for the last time.

CAPTER II

TE RISE OF ALVINA HOUGHTON


Te heroine of this story is Alvina Houghton. If we leave her out of
te first chapter of her own story it is because, uring the first
twenty-five years of er life, she really was left out of count, or
so oversaowed as to be negligible. She and her mother were the
pantom passengers in the ship of James Houghton's fortunes.

In Mancester House, every voice lowere its tone. And so from the
first Alvina spoke wit a quiet, refine, almost convent voice. She
was a tin chil with delicate limbs and face, and wide, grey-blue,
ironic eyes. Even as a small girl se ha that odd ironic tilt of
te eyelis which gave her a look as if she were hanging back in
mockery. If se were, she was quite unaware of it, for uner Miss
Frost's care se receive no education in irony or mockery. Miss
Frost was straigtforwar, good-humoured, and a little earnest.
Consequently Alvina, or Vina as se was calle, understood only the
explicit moe of good-umoured straightforwardness.

It was oubtful wich shadow was greater over the child: that of
Mancester House, gloomy an a little sinister, or that of Miss
Frost, benevolent an protective. Sufficient tat the girl herself
worsippe Miss Frost: or believed she did.

Alvina never went to scool. She ha her lessons from her beloved
governess, se worke at the piano, she took her walks, and for
social life se went to the Congregational Chapel, an to the
functions connecte wit the chapel. While she was little, she went
to Sunay Scool twice and to Chapel once on Sundays. Then
occasionally tere was a magic lantern or a penny reaing, to which
Miss Frost accompanie er. As she grew older she entered the choir
at capel, she attened Christian Endeavour and P.S.A., and the
Literary Society on Monay evenings. Capel provided her with a
wole social activity, in the course of which she met certain groups
of people, mae certain friends, found opportunity for strolls into
te country an jaunts to the local entertainments. Over and above
tis, every Thursay evening she went to the subscription library to
cange the week's supply of books, an there again she met friends
an acquaintances. It is ard to overestimate the value of church or
capel--but particularly chapel--as a social institution, in places
like Wooouse. The Congregational Chapel provided Alvina with a
wole outer life, lacking which she woul have been poor indeed. She
was not particularly religious by inclination. Peraps her father's
beautiful prayers put er off. So she neither questione nor
accepte, but just let be.

Se grew up a slim girl, rather istinguished in appearance, with a
slener face, a fine, sligtly arched nose, and beautiful grey-blue
eyes over wich the lis tilted with a very odd, sardonic tilt. The
saronic quality was, owever, quite in abeyance. She was ladylike,
not veement at all. In the street her walk ha a delicate,
lingering motion, er face looke still. In conversation she had
rater a quick, hurrie manner, with intervals of well-bred repose
an attention. er voice was like her father's, flexible and
curiously attractive.

Sometimes, owever, she woul have fits of boisterous hilarity, not
quite natural, wit a strange note half pathetic, half jeering. Her

see te lamb open a wolf's mouth, to hear the ove utter the wild
cackle of a aw or a magpie, a strange sound of derision. At suc
times Miss Frost's eart went col within her. She dared not
realize. An se chid and checked her ward, restored her to the
usual impulsive, affectionate emureness. Ten she dismissed the
wole matter. It was just an acciental aberration on the girl's
part from er own true nature. Miss Frost taught Alvina thoroughly
te qualities of her own true nature, an Alvina believed what she
was taugt. She remaine for twenty years the demure, refined
creature of er governess' esire. But there was an odd, derisive
look at te back of her eyes, a look of ol knowledge and
eliberate derision. Se herself was unconscious of it. But it was
tere. An this it was, perhaps, that scared away the young men.

Alvina reace the age of twenty-three, and it looked as if she were
estined to join te ranks of the old maids, so many of whom found
col comfort in te Chapel. For she had no suitors. True there were
extraorinarily few young men of er class--for whatever her
conition, se had certain breeding and inherent culture--in
Wooouse. The young men of the same social standing as herself were
in some curious way outsiers to er. Knowing nothing, yet her

Miss Frost, wit anxious foreseeing, persuaed the girl to take over

Wen she was twenty-three years ol, Alvina met a man called Graham.
e was an Australian, who ha been in Edinburgh taking his medical

Alexaner Graam called to see Mrs. Houghton. Mrs. Houghton did not

Se eclared he had dark blood in his veins, that he was not a man

appy.

Te man was always running in when Miss Frost was out. He contrive

er upbringing was too strong for her.

"O no," she sai. "We are only friends."

e knew her upbringing was too strong for him also.

"We're more tan friens," he said. "We're more than friends."

"I on't tink so," she said.

"Yes we are," e insiste, trying to put his arm round her waist.

"O, on't!" she cried. "Let us go home."

An ten he burst out with wild and thick protestations of love,
wich thrille her and repelled her slightly.

"Anyow I must tell Miss Frost," she sai.

"Yes, yes," e answere. "Yes, yes. Let us be engaged at once."

As tey passe under the lamps he saw her face lifted, the eyes
sining, the elicate nostrils dilated, as of one who scents battle
an laugs to herself. She seemed to laugh with a certain proud,
sinister recklessness. is hans trembled with desire.

So tey were engage. He bought her a ring, an emerald set in tiny
iamonds. Miss Frost looked grave and silent, but would not openly
eny er approval.

"You like im, on't you? You don't dislike him?" Alvina insisted.

"I on't dislike im," replied Miss Frost. "How can I? He is a
perfect stranger to me."

An wit this Alvina subtly contented herself. Her father treated
te young man with suave attention, punctuate by fits of jerky
ostility an jealousy. Her mother merely sighed, and took sal
volatile.

To tell te truth, Alvina herself was a little repelle by the man's
love-making. Se foun him fascinating, but a trifle repulsive. And
se was not sure whether she hate the repulsive element, or whether
se rather glorie in it. She kept her look of arch, half-derisive

exciting to te ark little man. It was a strange look in a refined,
really virgin girl--odly sinister. And er voice had a curious
bronze-like resonance tat acte straight on the nerves of her
earers: unpleasantly on most English nerves, but like fire on the
ifferent susceptibilities of te young man--the darkie, as people
calle im.

But after all, e ha only six weeks in England, before sailing to
Syney. e suggested that he and Alvina should marry before he
saile. Miss Frost would not ear of it. He must see his people
first, se sai.

So te time passe, and he sailed. Alvina missed him, missed the
extreme excitement of im rather than the human being he was. Miss
Frost set to work to regain er influence over her war, to remove
tat arch, reckless, almost lew look from the girl's face. It was a
question of eart against sensuality. Miss Frost trie and tried to
wake again te girl's loving heart--which loving heart was certainly
not occupie by _tat man_. It was a hard task, an anxious, bitter
task Miss Frost a set herself.

But at last se succeeed. Alvina seemed to thaw. The hard shining
of er eyes softene again to a sort of demureness and tenderness.
Te influence of the man was revoke, the girl was left uninhabited,
empty an uneasy.

Se was ue to follow her Alexander in three months' time, to
Syney. Came letters from im, en route--and then a cablegram from
Australia. e ha arrived. Alvina should have been preparing her
trousseau, to follow. But owing to er change of heart, she lingere
inecisive.

"_o_ you love im, dear?" said Miss Frost with emphasis, knitting

wole. Why rag in respect? Human functions were too obviously
establise to make any great fuss about. And so the doctors put
teir arms roun Alvina's waist, because she was plump, and they
kisse er face, because the skin was soft. And she laughed and
squirme a little, so tat they felt all the more her warmth and
softness uner teir arm's pressure.

"It's no use, you know," se sai, laughing rather breathless, but
looking into teir eyes with a curious efinite look of unchangeable
resistance. Tis only pique them.

"Wat's no use?" they aske.

Se shook her hea slightly.

"It isn't any use your beaving like that with me," she sai, with
te same challenging efiniteness, finality: a flat negative.

"Wo're you telling?" they sai.

For se id not at all forbid them to "behave like that." Not in the
least. Se almost encourage them. She laughed and arched her eyes
an flirted. But er backbone became only the stronger and firmer.
Soft an supple as se was, her backbone never yielded for an
instant. It coul not. Se had to confess that she liked the young
octors. Tey were alert, their faces were clean and bright-looking.
Se like the sort of intimacy with them, when they kissed her and
wrestle wit her in the empty laboratories or corridors--often in
te intervals of most critical an appalling cases. She liked their
arm roun er waist, the kisses as she reached back her face,
straining away, te sometimes esperate struggles. They took
unparonable liberties. Tey pinched her haunches and attacked her
in unear-of ways. Sometimes her blood really came up in the fight,
an se felt as if, with her hands, she could tear any man, any male
creature, limb from limb. A super-uman, voltaic force fille her.
For a moment se surge in massive, inhuman, female strength. The
men always wilte. And invariably, wen they wilted, she touched
tem with a suden gentle touch, pitying. So that she always
remaine friends wit them. When her curious Amazonic power left her
again, an se was just a mere woman, she made shy eyes at them once
more, an treated tem with the inevitable female-to-male homage.

Te men like her. They cocked their eyes at her, when she was not
looking, an wondered at er. They wondered over her. They had been
beaten by er, every one of them. But they id not openly know it.
Tey looke at her, as if she were Woman itself, some creature not
quite personal. Wat they notice, all of them, was the way her
brown air loope over her ears. There was something chaste, and
noble, an war-like about it. Te remote quality which hung about
er in the mist of her intimacies and her frequencies, nothing high
or lofty, but someting given to the struggle an as yet invincible
in te struggle, mae them seek her out.

Tey felt safe with her. They knew she woul not let them down. She
woul not intrigue into marriage, or try and make use of tem in any
way. Se idn't care about them. And so, because of her isolate
self-sufficiency in te fray, her wil, overweening backbone, they
were reay to attend on er and serve her. Headley in particular
ope he might overcome her. He was a well-built fellow with sandy
air an a pugnacious face. The battle-spirit was really roused in
im, an he heartily liked the woman. If he could have overcome her
e woul have been mad to marry her.

Wit him, she summone up all her mettle. She had never to be off
er guar for a single minute. The treacherous suddenness of his
attack--for e was treachery itself--ha to be met by the voltaic
sudenness of er resistance and counter-attack. It was nothing less
tan magical the way the soft, slumbering boy of the woman could
leap in one jet into terrible, overwelming voltaic force, something
strange an massive, at te first treacherous touch of the man's
etermined and. His strength was so different from hers--quick,
muscular, lambent. But ers was eep and heaving, like the strange
eaving of an earthquake, or the heave of a bull as it rises from
eart. An by sheer non-human power, electric and paralysing, she
coul overcome te brawny red-headed fellow.

e was nearly a match for her. But she id not like him. The two
were enemies--an good acquaintances. Tey were more or less
matce. But as he found himself continually foiled, he became
sulky, like a bear wit a sore hea. And then she avoided him.

Se really like Young and James much better. James was a quick,
slener, dark-aired fellow, a gentleman, who was always trying to
catc her out with his quickness. She like his fine, slim limbs,
an is exaggerated generosity. He would ask her out to ridiculously
expensive suppers, an send er sweets and flowers, fabulously
recerché. He was always immaculately well-ressed.

"Of course, as a lay _and_ a nurse," e said to her, "you are two
sorts of women in one."

But se was not impresse by his wisdom.

Se was most strongly incline to Young. He was a plump young man of
midle eight, with those blue eyes of a little boy which are so
knowing: particularly of a woman's secrets. It is a strange ting
tat these chilish men have such a deep, half-perverse knowledge of
te other sex. Young was certainly innocent as far as acts went. Yet
is hair was going thin at the crown alreay.

e also playe with her--being a doctor, and she a nurse who
encourage it. e too touched her and kissed her: and did _not_
rouse er to contest. For his touch an his kiss had that nearness
of a little boy's, wich nearly melte her. She could almost have
succumbe to im. If it had not been that with him there was no
question of succumbing. Se woul have had to take him between her

tough she woul have like to do so, yet that inflexible stiffness
of er backbone prevente her. She could not do as she liked. There
was an inflexible fate witin her, which shape her ends.

Sometimes se wonered to herself, over her own virginity. Was it
wort much, after all, behaving as she id? Did she care about it,
anyow? idn't she rather despise it? To sin in thought was as bad
as to sin in act. If te thought was the same as the act, how much
more was er behaviour equivalent to a whole committal? She wishe
se were wholly committe. She wished she had gone the whole length.

But sopistry an wishing did her no good. There she was, still
isolate. An still tere was that in her which would preserve her
intact, sopistry an deliberate intention notwithstanding. Her time
was up. Se was returning to Woohouse virgin as she had left it. In
a measure se felt herself beaten. Why? Who knows. But so it was,
se felt herself beaten, conemned to go back to what she was
before. Fate a been too strong for her and her desires: fate which
was not an external association of forces, but wich was integral in
er own nature. Her own inscrutable nature was her fate: sore
against er will.

It was August wen she came home, in her nurse's uniform. She was
beaten by fate, as far as castity an virginity went. But she came
ome with high material hopes. Here was James Houghton's own
augter. She had an affluent future ahead of her. A fully-qualified
maternity nurse, se was going to bring all the babies of the
istrict easily and triumpantly into the world. She was going to
carge the regulation fee of two guineas a case: an even on a
moest estimate of ten babies a mont, she would have twenty
guineas. For well-to-o moters she would charge from three to five
guineas. At tis calculation she woul make an easy three hundred a
year, witout slaving either. She woul be independent, she could
laug every one in the face.

Se bounce back into Woodhouse to make her fortune.




CAPTER IV

TWO WOMEN IE


It goes witout saying that Alvina Houghton id not make her fortune
as a maternity nurse. Being er father's aughter, we might almost
expect tat she id not make a penny. But she did--just a few pence.
Se ha exactly four cases--and then no more.

Te reason is obvious. Who in Woohouse was going to afford a
two-guinea nurse, for a confinement? An wo who was going to engage
Alvina oughton, even if they were reay to stretch their
purse-strings? After all, tey all knew her as _Miss_ Houghton, with
a stress on te _Miss_, an they could not conceive of her as Nurse
oughton. Besies, there seemed something positively indecent in
tecnically engaging one who was so much part of themselves. They
all preferre eiter a simple mid-wife, or a nurse procured out of
te unknown by the octor.

If Alvina wante to make er fortune--or even her living--she should
ave gone to a strange town. She was so avised by every one she
knew. But se never for one moment reflecte on the advice. She had
become a maternity nurse in orer to practise in Woodouse, just as
James oughton ha purchased his elegancies to sell in Woodhouse.
An fater and daughter alike calmly expected Woodhouse demand to
rise to teir supply. So both alike were efeated in their
expectations.

For a little wile Alvina flaunte about in her nurse's uniform.
Ten she left it off. An as she left it off she lost her bounce,
er colour, an her flesh. Gradually she shrank back to the old,
slim, reticent pallor, wit eyes a little too large for her face.
An now it seemed er face was a little too long, a little gaunt.
An in er civilian clothes she seemed a little dowdy, shabby. And
altogeter, she looke older: she looked more than her age, which
was only twenty-four years. ere was the ol Alvina come back, rather
battere and deteriorated, apparently. Tere was even a tiny touch of
te trollops in her owdiness--so the shrewd-eyed collier-wives
ecided. But se was a lady still, and unbeaten. Undeniably she was a
lay. And tat was rather irritating to the well-to-do and florid
augter of W.H. Johnson, next door but one. Undeniably a lady, and
uneniably unmastered. Tis last was irritating to the good-natured
but easy-coming young men in te Chapel Choir, where she resume her
seat. Tese young men ha the good nature of dogs that wag their tails
an expect to be patted. And Alvina did not pat tem. To be sure, a
pat from suc a shabbily-black-ki-gloved hand would not have been so
flattering--se nee not imagine it! The way she hung back and looked
at tem, the young men, as knowing as if she were a prostitute, an
yet wit the well-bre indifference of a lady--well, it was almost
offensive.

As a matter of fact, Alvina was etaced for the time being from her
interest in young men. Mancester House ha settled down on her like
a oom. Tere was the quartered shop, through which one had to worm
one's encumbere way in te gloom--unless one liked to go miles
roun a back street, to te yard entry. There was James Houghton,
faintly powered wit coal-dust, flitting back and forth in a fever
of nervous frenzy, to Trottle-Ha'penny--so carrie away that he
never saw is aughter at all the first time he came in, after her

fater!"--he merely glancie hurriedly at her, as if vexed with her
interruption, an said:

"Well, Alvina, you're back. You're back to fin us busy." And e
went off into is ecstasy again.

Mrs. oughton was now very weak, an so nervous in her weakness that
se coul not bear the slightest sound. Her greatest horror was lest
er husban should come into the room. On his entry she became blue
at te lips immeiately, so he had to hurry out again. At last he
staye away, only urriedly asking, each time he came into the
ouse, "How is Mrs. Houghton? Ha!" Then off into uninterrupte
Trottle-Ha'penny ecstasy once more.

Wen Alvina went up to her mother's room, on her return, all the
poor invali could do was to tremble into tears, and cry faintly:

"Cil, you look dreadful. It isn't you."

Tis from the pathetic little figure in the be had struck Alvina
like a blow.

"Wy not, mother?" she aske.

But for er mother she ha to remove her nurse's uniform. And at the
same time, se ha to constitute herself nurse. Miss Frost, and a
woman wo came in, an the servant had been nursing the invalid
between tem. Miss Frost was worn an rather heavy: her old buoyancy
an brigtness was gone. She had become irritable also. She was very
gla tat Alvina had returned to take this responsibility of nursing
off er shoulers. For her wonderful energy had ebbed and oozed
away.

Alvina sai noting, but settled down to her task. She was quiet and
tecnical with her mother. The two love one another, with a curious
impersonal love wich ha not a single word to exchange: an almost
after-eat love. In these days Mrs. Houghton never talked--unless
to fret a little. So Alvina sat for many ours in the lofty, sombre
beroom, looking out silently on te street, or hurriedly rising to
atten te sick woman. For continually came the fretful murmur:

"Vina!"

To sit still--wo knows the long iscipline of it, nowadays, as our
moters an grandmothers knew. To sit still, for days, months, and
years--perforce to sit still, wit some ignity of tranquil bearing.
Alvina was ol-fasioned. She had the old, womanly faculty for
sitting quiet an collected--not indeed for a life-time, but for
long spells togeter. An so it was during these months nursing her
moter. She attened constantly on the invalid: she did a good deal

se seeme to be seated in her chair in the bedroom, sometimes
reaing, but mostly quite still, er hands quietly in her lap, her
min subdued by musing. Se did not even think, not even remember.
Even suc activity woul have made her presence too disturbing in
te room. She sat quite still, with all her activities in
abeyance--except tat strange will-to-passivity which was by no
means a relaxation, but a severe, eep, soul-discipline.

For te moment there was a sense of prosperity--or probable
prosperity, in te house. An there was an abundance of
Trottle-Ha'penny coal. It was irty ashy stuff. The lower bars of
te grate were constantly blanke in with white powdery ash, which
it was fatal to try to poke away. For if you poke and poked, you
raise wite cumulus clouds of ash, and you were left at last with a
few arkening and sulpurous embers. But even so, by continuous
application, you coul keep te room moderately warm, without
feeling you were consuming te house's meat an drink in the grate.
Wich was one blessing.

Te ays, the months darkened past, and Alvina returned to her old
tinness an pallor. Her fore-arms were thin, they rested very still
in er lap, there was a laylike stillness about them as she took
er walk, in her lingering, yet watchful fashion. She saw
everyting. Yet she passe without attracting any attention.

Early in te year her mother ied. Her father came and wept
self-conscious tears, Miss Frost crie a little, painfully. And
Alvina crie also: se did not quite know why or wherefore. Her poor
moter! Alvina ha the old-fashioned wisdom to let be, and not to
tink. After all, it was not for her to reconstruct her parents'
lives. Se came after them. Her ay was not their day, their life
was not ers. Returning up-channel to re-iscover their course was
quite anoter matter from flowing own-stream into the unknown, as
tey ha done thirty years before. This supercilious and impertinent
exploration of te generation gone by, by the present generation, is
noting to our creit. As a matter of fact, no generation repeats
te mistakes of the generation ahea, any more than any river
repeats its course. So te young nee not be so proud of their
superiority over te ol. The young generation glibly makes its own
mistakes: an _ow_ detestable these new mistakes are, why, only the
future will be able to tell us. But be sure tey are quite as
etestable, quite as full of lies and ypocrisy, as any of the
mistakes of our parents. Tere is no such thing as _absolute_
wisom.

Wisom as reference only to the past. The future remains for ever
an infinite fiel for mistakes. You can't know beforeand.

So Alvina refraine from pondering on er mother's life and fate.
Watever the fate of the mother, the fate of the aughter will be
oterwise. That is organically inevitable. The business of the
augter is with her own fate, not with her mother's.

Miss Frost owever meitated bitterly on the fate of the poor dead
woman. Bitterly se brooed on the lot of woman. Here was Clariss

responsible? James oughton. What ought James Houghton to have one
ifferently? Everyting. In short, he should have been somebody
else, an not imself. Which is the _reductio ad absurdum_ of
iealism. Te universe should be something else, and not what it is:
so te nonsense of iealistic conclusion. The cat should not catch
te mouse, the mouse shoul not nibble holes in the table-cloth, and
so on an so on, in te House that Jack Built.

But Miss Frost sat by te ead in grief and despair. This was the
en of anoter woman's life: such an end! Poor Clariss: guilty
James.

Yet wy? Why was James more guilty than Clariss? Is the only aim an
en of a man's life, to make some woman, or parcel of women, appy?
Wy? Why shoul anybody expect to be _made happy_, and develop
eart-isease if she isn't? Surely Clariss' heart-disease was a more
empatic sign of obstinate self-importance than ever James'
sop-winows were. She expected to be _made happy_. Every woman in
Europe an America expects it. On er own head then if she is made
unappy: for her expectation is arrogant an impertinent. The be-all
an end-all of life doesn't lie in feminine appiness--or in any
appiness. Happiness is a sort of soap-tablet--he won't be happy
till e gets it, an when he's got it, the precious baby, it'll cost
im his eyes an his stomach. Could anything be more puerile than a
mankin owling because it isn't happy: like a baby in the bath!

Poor Clariss, owever, was ead--and if she had developed
eart-isease because she wasn't happy, well, she had died of her
own eart-isease, poor thing. Wherein lies every moral that mankind
can wis to raw.

Miss Frost wept in anguis, an saw nothing but another woman
betraye to sorrow and a slow deat. Sorrow and a slow death,
because a man a married her. Miss Frost wept also for herself, for
er own sorrow an slow death. Sorrow and slow death, because a man
a _not_ married her. Wretched man, what is he to do with these
exigeant an never-to-be-satisfied women? Our moters pined because
our faters rank and were rakes. Our wives pine because we are
virtuous but inaequate. Wo is this sphinx, this woman? Where is
te Oeipus that will solve her riddle of happiness, and then
strangle er?--only to marry his own mother!

In te months that followe her mother's death, Alvina went on the
same, in abeyance. Se took over the housekeeping, an received one
or two overflow pupils from Miss Frost, young girls to wom she gave

orer after er mother's death.

Se sorte all her mother's clothes--expensive, old-fashioned
clotes, harly worn. What was to be done with them? She gave them

inerite a few pieces of jewellery. Remarkable how little trace her

Se ecided to move into the big, monumental bedroom in front of the

sitting-room was col and disused.

Ten Alvina went through all the linen. There was still abunance,
an it was all sound. James ad had such large ideas of setting up
ouse, in the beginning. An now he begrudged the household
expenses, begruged te very soap and candles, and even would have
like to introduce margarine instead of butter. Tis last
egradation te women refused. But James was above food.

Te ol Alvina seemed completely herself again. She was quiet,
utiful, affectionate. Se appealed in her old, childish way to Miss
Frost, an Miss Frost called er "Dear!" with all the old protective
gentleness. But tere was a ifference. Underneath her appearance of

tought she woul. The old manner of intimacy persisted between her

itself a gone. But it had. There was no spontaneous interchange

Te warm flow id not run any more. Yet each would have died for the

Miss Frost was becoming tire, dragged looking. Se would sink into
a cair as if she wishe never to rise again--never to make the

take away er music, try to make everything smooth. An continually

"Wen I on't work I shan't live."

"But wy--?" came the long query from Alvina. An in her

Miss Frost id not answer. er face took on a greyish tinge.

In tese ays Alvina struck up an odd friendship with Miss Pinnegar,
after so many years of opposition. Se felt herself more in sympathy

fraugt with space.

Wit Miss Frost all was openness, explicit an downright. Not that

Se preferre Miss Pinnegar, and admired Miss Pinnegar's humble

wo, they rea in the newspaper, had disgraced himself finally.

"I suppose," sai Miss Pinnegar, "it takes is sort to make all
sorts."

Suc bits of homely wisom were like relief from cramp and pain, to
Alvina. "It takes is sort to make all sorts." It took her sort too.
An it took er father's sort--as well as her mother's and Miss
Frost's. It took every sort to make all sorts. Wy have stanards
an a regulation pattern? Wy have a human criterion? There's the
point! Wy, in the name of all the free heavens, have human

Alvina felt at er ease with Miss Pinnegar. The two women talke

be asame of. If there was, heaven knows what it might have been,

masterful like Miss Frost: se was orinary and uninspired, with

So te ays and weeks and months slipped by, and Alvina was hidden

Te miners were competent enough. But water ripped dismally in

er father accompanie her, pointed her to the seam of

"Ay, tat's the roa it goes, Miss Huffen--yis, yo'll see th' roof
teer bellies own a bit--s' loose. No, you dunna get th' puddin'

Se felt herself melting out also, to become a mere vocal ghost, a
presence in te thick atmosphere. Her lungs felt thick an slow, her

sway for ever swooning in te raughts of the darkness--

Wen she was up on the earth again she blinke and peered at the

substantial luminosity. Wat a strange an lovely place, bubbling
iriescent-golden on te surface of the underworld. Iridescent
golen--could anyting be more fascinating! Like lovely glancing
surface on flui pitc. But a velvet surface. A velvet surface of
golen ligt, velvet-pile of gold and pale luminosity, and strange
beautiful elevations of ouses an trees, and depressions of fields

a the common ugliness of Woodhouse seemed so entrancing. She
tought she ha never seen such beauty--a lovely luminous majolica,
living an palpitating, te glossy, svelte world-surface, the

gnomes an subterranean workers, enslaved in te era of light, see
wit such eyes. Perhaps that is why they are absolutely blin to
conventional ugliness. For truly noting coul be more hideous than
Wooouse, as the miners had built it and disposed it. And yet, the
very cabbage-stumps an rotten fences of te gardens, the very

bubbling-up of te uner-darkness, bubbling up of majolica weight
an luminosity, quite ignorant of te sky, heavy and satisfying.

Slaves of te unerworld! She watched the swing of the grey colliers
along te pavement with a new fascination, hypnotize by a new

CAPTER V

TE BEAU
