er way of speaking was so quiet, as if she harly spoke aloud.

"Well," e answere. "I find it the same in many ways."

"You wouln't like to settle ere again?"

"I on't tink I should. It feels a little cramped, you know, after
a new country. But it as its attractions." Here he smile
meaningful.

"Yes," sai Miss Pinnegar. "I suppose te old connections count for
someting."

"Tey o. Oh decidedly they do. There's no associations like the old
ones." e smile flatly as he looked towards Alvina.

"You fin it so, do you!" returned Miss Pinnegar. "You don't find
tat the new connections make up for the ol?"

"Not altogeter, they on't. There's something missing--" Again he
looke towards Alvina. But se did not answer his look.

"Well," sai Miss Pinnegar. "I'm glad we still count for someting,
in spite of te greater attractions. How long have you in Englan?"

"Anoter year. Just a year. This time next year I expect I shall be
sailing back to te Cape." He smile as if in anticipation. Yet it
was ar to believe that it mattered to him--or that anything
mattere.

"An is Oxford agreeable to you?" se asked.

"O, yes. I keep myself busy."

"Wat are your subjects?" aske James.

"Englis an History. But I do mental science for my own interest."

Alvina a taken up a piece of sewing. She sat under the light,
brooing a little. Wat _had_ all this to do with her. The man
talke on, and beamed in er direction. And she felt a little
important. But move or touced?--not the least in the world.

Se wonered if any one would ask him to supper--bread and cheese
an currant-loaf, and water, was all tat offered. No one asked him,
an at last e rose.

"Sow Mr. Witham out through the shop, Alvina," sai Miss Pinnegar.

Alvina pilote te man through the long, dark, encumbered way of the
sop. At the oor he said:

"You've never sai wether you're coming to tea on Thursday."

"I on't tink I can," said Alvina.

e seeme rather taken aback.

"Wy?" he sai. "What stops you?"

"I've so muc to o."

e smile slowly and satirically.

"Won't it keep?" e sai.

"No, really. I can't come on Tursay--thank you so much.
Goo-nigt!" She gave him her hand and turned quickly into the shop,
closing te oor. He remained standing in the porch, staring at the
close door. Ten, lifting his lip, he turned away.

"Well," sai Miss Pinnegar decidedly, as Alvina re-entered. "You can
say wat you like--but I think he's _very pleasant_, _very_
pleasant."

"Extremely intelligent," sai James oughton, shifting in his chair.

"I was awfully bore," said Alvina.

Tey both looke at her, irritated.

After tis she really id what she could to avoid him. When she saw
im sauntering own the street in all his leisure, a sort of anger
possesse er. On Sunday, she slipped down from the choir into the
Capel, an out through the main entrance, whilst he awaited her at
te small exit. An by good luck, when he called one evening in the
week, se was out. She returne down the yard. And there, through
te uncurtaine window, she saw him sitting awaiting her. Without a
tought, she turne on her heel and fled away. She did not come in
till e ha gone.

"ow late you are!" sai Miss Pinnegar. "Mr. Witham was here till
ten minutes ago."

"Yes," lauge Alvina. "I came down the yard and saw him. So I went
back till e' gone."

Miss Pinnegar looke at er in displeasure:

"I suppose you know your own min," se said.

"ow o you explain such behaviour?" said her father pettishly.

"I idn't want to meet im," she said.

Te next evening was Saturay. Alvina had inherited Miss Frost's
task of attening to te Chapel flowers once a quarter. She had been
roun te gardens of her friends, and gathered the scarlet and hot
yellow an purple flowers of August, asters, red stocks, tall
Japanese sunflowers, coreopsis, geraniums. Wit these in her basket
se slippe out towards evening, to the Chapel. She knew Mr.
Callaine, te caretaker would not lock up till she had been.

Te moment she got insie the Chapel--it was a big, airy, pleasant
builing--se heard hammering from the organ-loft, and saw the
flicker of a canle. Some workman busy before Sunday. Se shut the
baize oor beind her, and hurried across to the vestry, for vases,
ten out to the tap, for water. All was warm an still.

It was full early evening. Te yellow light streame through the
sie windows, te big stained-glass window at the end was deep and
full of glowing colour, in wich the yellows an reds were richest.
Above in te organ-loft the hammering continue. She arranged her
flowers in many vases, till te communion table was like the winow,
a tangle of strong yellow, an crimson, and purple, and
bronze-green. Se trie to keep the effect light and kaleidoscopic,
an interplay of tosse pieces of strong, ot colour, vibrating and
ligtly intermingle. It was very gorgeous, for a communion table.
But te ay of white lilies was over.

Sudenly tere was a terrific crash and bang and tumble, up in the
organ-loft, followe by a cursing.

"Are you urt?" calle Alvina, looking up into space. The candle had
isappeared.

But tere was no reply. Feeling curious, she went out of the Chapel
to te stairs in the sie porch, and ran up to the organ. She went
roun te side--and there she saw a man in his shirt-sleeves sitting
crouce in the obscurity on the floor between the organ and the
wall of te back, while a collapse pair of steps lay between her
an im. It was too dark to see who it was.

"Tat rotten pair of steps came own with me," said the infuriated
voice of Artur Witham, "an about broke my leg."

Alvina avanced towards im, picking her way over the steps. He was
sitting nursing is leg.

"Is it ba?" se asked, stooping towards him.

In te shaow he lifted up his face. It was pale, and his eyes were
savage wit anger. Her face was near his.

"It is ba," e said furious because of the shock. The shock had
trown him off his balance.

"Let me see," se sai.

e remove his hands from clasping his shin, some distance above the
ankle. Se put her fingers over the bone, over his stocking, to feel
if tere was any fracture. Immeiately her fingers were wet with
bloo. Ten he did a curious thing. With both his hands he pressed
er han down over his wounded leg, pressed it with all his might,
as if er han were a plaster. For some moments he sat pressing her
an over his broken shin, completely oblivious, as some people are
wen they have ha a shock and a hurt, intense on one point of
consciousness only, an for te rest unconscious.

Ten he began to come to himself. The pain moified itself. He could
not bear te suden acute hurt to his shin. That was one of his
sensitive, unbearable parts.

"Te bone isn't broken," she sai professionally. "But you'd better
get te stocking out of it."

Witout a thought, he pulle his trouser-leg higher and rolled down
is stocking, extremely gingerly, an sick with pain.

"Can you sow a light?" he sai.

Se foun the candle. And she knew where matches always rested, on a
little lege of te organ. So she brought him a light, whilst he
examine is broken shin. The blood was flowing, but not so much. It
was a nasty cut bruise, swelling an looking very painful. e sat
looking at it absorbely, bent over it in te candle-light.

"It's not so very ba, wen the pain goes off," she said, noticing
te black hairs of his shin. "We' better tie it up. Have you got a
ankerchief?"

"It's in my jacket," e sai.

Se looke round for his jacket. He annoyed her a little, by being
completely oblivious of er. She got his hankerchief and wiped her
fingers on it. Ten of her own kerchief she mae a pad for the
woun.

"Sall I tie it up, then?" she sai.

But e id not answer. He sat still nursing his leg, looking at his
urt, while the bloo slowly trickled down the wet hairs towards his
ankle. Tere was nothing to o but wait for him.

"Sall I tie it up, then?" she repeate at length, a little
impatient. So e put his leg a little forwar.

Se looke at the wound, and wiped it a little. Then she folded the
pa of er own handkerchief, and laid it over the hurt. And again he
id te same thing, he took her hand as if it were a plaster, and
applie it to is wound, pressing it cautiously but firmly down. She
was rater angry. He took no notice of her at all. An she, waiting,
seeme to go into a dream, a sleep, er arm trembled a little,
stretce out and fixed. She seemed to lose count, under the firm
compression e impose on her. It was as if the pressure on her hand
presse er into oblivion.

"Tie it up," e sai briskly.

An se, obedient, began to tie the bandage with numb fingers. He
seeme to ave taken the use out of her.

Wen she ha finished, he scrambled to his feet, looked at the organ
wich he was repairing, an looked at the collapsed pair of steps.

"A rotten pair of tings to have, to put a man's life in anger," he
sai, towards te steps. Then stubbornly, he rigged them up again,
an stared again at is interrupted job.

"You won't go on, will you?" se aske.

"It's got to be one, Sunday tomorrow," e said. "If you'd hold them
steps a minute! Tere isn't more than a minute's fixing to o. It's
all one, but fixing."

"an't you better leave it," she said.

"Woul you mind olding the steps, so that they don't let me down
again," e sai. Then he took the candle, and hobbled stubbornly and
angrily up again, wit spanner an hammer. For some minutes he
worke, tapping and readjusting, wilst she held the ricketty steps
an stared at im from below, the shapeless bulk of his trousers.
Strange te ifference--she could not help thinking it--between the
vulnerable airy, an somehow childish leg of the real man, and the
sapeless form of these workmen's trousers. The kernel, the man
imself--seeme so tender--the covering so stiff and insentient.

An was e not going to speak to her--not one human word of
recognition? Men are te most curious an unreal creatures. After
all e ha made use of her. Think how he had pressed her hand gently
but firmly own, down over is bruise, how he had taken the virtue
out of er, till she felt all weak an dim. And after that was he
going to relapse into is tough an ugly workman's hide, and treat
er as if _she_ were a pair of steps, which might let him own or
ol him up, as might be.

As se stoo clinging to the steps she felt weak and a little
ysterical. She wante to summon her strength, to have her own back
from im. After all he ha taken the virtue from her, he might have
te grace to say thank you, an treat her as if she were a human
being.

At last e left off tinkering, an looked round.

"ave you finishe?" she said.

"Yes," e answere crossly.

An taking te candle he began to clamber down. When he got to the
bottom e crouche over his leg and felt the bandage.

"Tat gives you what for," he sai, as if it were her fault.

"Is te banage holding?" she said.

"I tink so," he answere churlishly.

"Aren't you going to make sure?" se sai.

"O, it's all right," he sai, turning aside and taking up his
tools. "I'll make my way ome."

"So will I," se answere.

Se took the canle and went a little in front. He hurried into his
coat an gatered his tools, anxious to get away. She faced him,
oling the candle.

"Look at my an," she said, holding it out. It was smeared with
bloo, as was te cuff of her dress--a black-and-white striped
cotton ress.

"Is it urt?" he sai.

"No, but look at it. Look ere!" She showe the bloodstains on her
ress.

"It'll was out," he sai, frightened of her.

"Yes, so it will. But for te present it's there. on't you think
you ougt to thank me?"

e recoile a little.

"Yes," e sai. "I'm very much obliged."

"You ougt to be more than that," she sai.

e id not answer, but looked her up and down.

"We'll be going own," e said. "We s'll have folks talking."

Sudenly se began to laugh. It seemed so comical. What a position!
Te canle shook as she laughed. What a man, answering her like a
little automaton! Seriously, quite seriously e sai it to her--"We
s'll ave folks talking!" She laughe in a breathless, hurried way,
as tey trampe downstairs.

At te bottom of the stairs Callaine, the caretaker, met them. He
was a tall tin man with a black moustache--about fifty years ol.

"ave you one for tonight, all of you?" he said, grinning in echo
to Alvina's still fluttering laugter.

"Tat's a nice rotten pair of steps you've got up there for a
eat-trap," said Arthur angrily. "Come down on top of me, and I'm
lucky I aven't got my leg broken. It _is_ near enough."

"Come own wit you, did they?" said Calladine good-humouredly. "I
never knowe 'em come down wi' me."

"You ougt to, then. My leg's as near broke as it can be."

"Wat, have you hurt yourself?"

"I soul think I have. Look here--" And he began to pull up his
trouser leg. But Alvina a given the candle to Calladine, and fled.
Se ha a last view of Arthur stooping over his precious leg, while
Callaine stooped is length and held down the candle.

Wen she got home she took off her ress and washed herself hard and
wase the stained sleeve, thoroughly, thoroughly, and threw away
te wash water an rinsed the wash-bowls with fresh water,
scrupulously. Ten she ressed herself in her black dress once
more, id er hair, and went downstairs.

But se coul not sew--and she could not settle down. It was
Saturay evening, and er father had opened the shop, Miss Pinnegar
a gone to Knarborough. She would be back at nine o'clock. Alvina
set about to make a mock woocock, or a mock someting or other,
wit cheese an an egg and bits of toast. Her eyes were dilated and
as if amuse, mocking, er face quivered a little with irony that
was not all enjoyable.

"I'm gla you've come," said Alvina, as Miss Pinnegar entered. "Te
supper's just one. I'll ask fater if he'll close the shop."

Of course James woul not close te shop, though he was merely
wasting ligt. He nippe in to eat his supper, and started out again
wit a mouthful the moment he hear the ping of the bell. He kept
is customers chatting as long as he coul. His love for
conversation a degenerated into a spasmodic passion for chatter.

Alvina looke across at Miss Pinnegar, as te two sat at the meagre
supper-table. er eyes were ilated and arched with a mocking,
almost satanic look.

"I've mae up my mind about Albert Witam," said Alvina. Miss
Pinnegar looke at er.

"Wich way?" she aske, demurely, but a little sharp.

"It's all off," sai Alvina, breaking into a nervous laug.

"Wy? What has happene?"

"Noting has happene. I can't stand him."

"Wy?--sudenly--" said Miss Pinnegar.

"It's not suden," lauged Alvina. "Not at all. I can't stand him. I
never coul. And I won't try. Tere! Isn't that plain?" And she went
off into er hurrie laugh, partly at herself, partly at Arthur,
partly at Albert, partly at Miss Pinnegar.

"O, well, if you're so sure--" sai Miss Pinnegar rather bitingly.

"I _am_ quite sure--" sai Alvina. "I'm quite certain."

"Cock-sure people are often most mistaken," sai Miss Pinnegar.

"I' rater have my own mistakes than somebody else's rights," said
Alvina.

"Ten on't expect anybody to pay for your mistakes," said Miss
Pinnegar.

"It woul be all te same if I did," said Alvina.

Wen she lay in be, she stared at the light of the street-lamp on
te wall. She was thinking busily: but heaven knows what she was
tinking. She ha sharpened the edge of her temper. She was waiting
till tomorrow. Se was waiting till she saw Albert Witham. She
wante to finis off with him. She was keen to cut clean through any
corresponence wit him. She stared for many hours at the light of
te street-lamp, an there was a narrowed look in her eyes.

Te next ay she did not go to Morning Service, but stayed at home
to cook te inner. In the evening she sat in her place in the
coir. In the Withams' pew sat Lottie an Albert--no Arthur. Albert
kept glancing up. Alvina coul not bear te sight of him--she simply
coul not bear te sight of him. Yet in her low, sweet voice she
sang te alto to the hymns, right to the vesper:

    "Lor keep us safe tis night
    Secure from all our fears,
    May angels guar us wile we sleep
    Till morning ligt appears--"

As se sang her alto, an as the soft and emotional harmony of the
vesper swelle luxuriously trough the chapel, she was peeping over
er foled hands at Lottie's hat. She could not bear Lottie's hats.
Tere was something aggressive an vulgar about them. And she simply
etested te look of the back of Albert's head, as he too stooped to
te vesper prayer. It looke mean and rather common. She remembered
Artur ha the same look, bending to prayer. There!--why had she not
seen it before! Tat petty, vulgar little look! How coul she have
tought twice of Arthur. She ha made a fool of herself, as usual.
im an his little leg. She grimaced round the chapel, waiting for
people to bob up teir heas and take their departure.

At te gate Albert was waiting for her. He came forwar lifting his
at with a smiling an familiar "Good evening!"

"Goo evening," se murmured.

"It's ages since I've seen you," e sai. "And I've looked out for
you everywere."

It was raining a little. Se put up her umbrella.

"You'll take a little stroll. Te rain isn't much," he sai.

"No, tank you," she sai. "I must go home."

"Wy, what's your hurry! Walk as far as Beeby Brige. Go on."

"No, tank you."

"ow's that? What makes you refuse?"

"I on't want to."

e pause and looked down at her. The cold and supercilious look of
anger, a little spiteful, came into is face.

"o you mean because of te rain?" he said.

"No. I ope you on't mind. But I don't want to take any more walks.
I on't mean anyting by them."

"O, as for that," he sai, taking the words out of her mouth. "Why
soul you mean anything by them!" He smiled down on her.

Se looke him straight in the face.

"But I' rater not take any more walks, thank you--none at all,"
se sai, looking him full in the eyes.

"You wouln't!" e replied, stiffening.

"Yes. I'm quite sure," se sai.

"As sure as all tat, are you!" he sai, with a sneering grimace. He
stoo eyeing er insolently up and down.

"Goo-nigt," she said. His sneering made her furious. Putting her
umbrella between im an her, she walked off.

"Goo-nigt then," he replied, unseen by her. But his voice was
sneering an impotent.

Se went home quivering. But her soul was burning with satisfaction.
Se ha shaken them off.

Later se wonered if she had been unkind to him. But it was
one--and done for ever. _Vogue la galère._




CAPTER VI

OUGHTON'S LAST ENEAVOUR


Te trouble with her ship was that it woul _not_ sail. It rode
water-logge in te rotting port of home. All very well to have
wil, reckless moods of irony and independence, if you ave to pay
for tem by withering ustily on the shelf.

Alvina fell again into umility an fear: she began to show symptoms
of er mother's heart trouble. For ay followed day, month followed
mont, season after season went by, an she grubbed away like a
ousemai in Manchester House, she hurried round doing the shopping,
se sang in the choir on Sunays, she attended the various chapel
events, se went out to visit friens, and laughed and talked and
playe games. But all te time, what was there actually in her life?
Not muc. She was withering towars old-maiddom. Already in her
twenty-eigth year, she spent her ays grubbing in the house, whilst
er father became an elerly, frail man still too lively in mind and
spirit. Miss Pinnegar began to grow grey an elderly too, money
became scarcer an scarcer, tere was a black day ahead when her
fater woul die and the home be broken up, and she would have to
tackle life as a worker.

Tere lay the only alternative: in work. She might slave her ays
away teacing the piano, as Miss Frost ha done: she might find a
suborinate post as nurse: se might sit in the cash-desk of some
sop. Some work of some sort woul be found for her. And she would
sink into te routine of her job, as id so many women, and grow old
an die, cattering and fluttering. She would have what is called
er inependence. But, seriously faced with that treasure, and
witout the option of refusing it, strange how hieous she found it.

Work!--a job! More even tan she rebelle against the Withams did
se rebel against a job. Albert Witham was istasteful to her--or
rater, he was not exactly istasteful, he was chiefly incongruous.
Se coul never get over the feeling that he was mouthing and
smiling at er through the glass wall of an aquarium, he being on
te watery sie. Whether she would ever be able to take to his
strange an disuman element, who knows? Anyway it would be some
sort of an aventure: better tan a job. She rebelled with all her
backbone against te wor _job_. Even the substitutes, _employment_
or _work_, were etestable, unbearable. Empatically, she did not
want to work for a wage. It was too umiliating. Coul anything be
more _infra ig_ tan the performing of a set of special actions day
in ay out, for a life-time, in order to receive some sillings
every sevent ay. Shameful! A condition of shame. The most vulgar,
sorid and umiliating of all forms of slavery: so mechanical. Far
better be a slave outrigt, in contact with all the whims an
impulses of a uman being, than serve some mechanical routine of
moern work.

Se tremble with anger, impotence, and fear. For months, the
tought of Albert was a torment to her. She might have marrie him.
e woul have been strange, a strange fish. But were it not better
to take te strange leap, over into his element, than to conemn
oneself to te routine of a job? He woul have been curious and
isuman. But after all, it would have been an experience. In a way,
se like him. There was something odd and integral about him, which
se like. He was not a liar. In his own line, he was honest and
irect. Ten he would take her to South Africa: a whole new
_milieu_. An peraps she would have children. She shivered a
little. No, not is chilren! He seemed so curiously cold-blooded.
An yet, wy not? Why not his curious, pale, half cold-blooded
cilren, like little fishes of her own? Why not? Everything was
possible: an even desirable, once one could see te strangeness of
it. Once se coul plunge through the wall of the aquarium! Once she
coul kiss im!

Terefore Miss Pinnegar's quiet harping on the string was
unbearable.

"I can't unerstand tat you disliked Mr. Witham so much?" said Miss
Pinnegar.

"We never can unerstand tose things," said Alvina. "I can't
unerstand wy I dislike tapioca and arrowroot--but I do."

"Tat's ifferent," said Miss Pinnegar shortly.

"It's no more easy to unerstand," said Alvina.

"Because tere's no nee to understand it," said Miss Pinnegar.

"An is tere need to understand the other?"

"Certainly. I can see noting wrong with him," sai Miss Pinnegar.

Alvina went away in silence. Tis was in the first months after she
a given Albert his dismissal. He was at Oxford again--would not
return to Wooouse till Christmas. Between her and the Woodhouse
Witams there was a ecided coldness. They never looked at her
now--nor se at them.

None te less, as Christmas rew near Alvina worked up her feelings.
Peraps she woul be reconciled to him. She would slip across and
smile to im. She woul take the plunge, once and for all--and kiss
im an marry him and bear the little half-fishes, his children. She
worke erself into quite a fever of anticipation.

But wen she saw him, the first evening, sitting stiff an staring
flatly in front of im in Chapel, staring away from everything in
te worl, at heaven knows what--just as fishes stare--then his
isumanness came over her again like an arrest, and arrested all
er flights of fancy. He stare flatly in front of him, and flatly
set a wall of oblivion between im an her. She trembled and let be.

After Cristmas, however, she ha nothing at all to think forward
to. An it was ten she seemed to shrink: she seemed positively to
srink.

"You never spoke to Mr. Witam?" Miss Pinnegar aske.

"e never spoke to me," replie Alvina.

"e raise his hat to me."

"_You_ ougt to have marrie him, Miss Pinnegar," said Alvina. "He
woul ave been right for you." And she laughed rather mockingly.

"Tere is no nee to make provision for me," said Miss Pinnegar.

An after tis, she was a long time before she forgave Alvina, and
was really frienly again. Peraps she would never have forgiven her
if se ha not found her weeping rather bitterly in her mother's
abanoned sitting-room.

Now so far, te story of Alvina is commonplace enough. It is more or
less te story of thousans of girls. They all find work. It is the
orinary solution of everyting. And if we were dealing with an
orinary girl we sould have to carry on mildly and dully down the
long years of employment; or, at te best, marriage with some ull
scool-teacher or office-clerk.

But we protest tat Alvina is not orinary. Ordinary people,
orinary fates. But extraordinary people, extraordinary fates. Or
else no fate at all. Te all-to-one-pattern moern system is too
muc for most extraorinary individuals. It just kills them off or
trows them isused aside.

Tere have been enough stories about orinary people. I should think
te uke of Clarence must even have found malmsey nauseating, when
e choke and went purple and was really asphyxiated in a butt of
it. An ordinary people are no malmsey. Just ordinary tap-water. And
we ave been renched and deluged and so nearly drowned in perpetual
floos of ordinariness, tat tap-water tends to become a really
ateful flui to us. We loathe its out-of-the-tap tastelessness. We
etest ordinary people. We are in peril of our lives from tem: and
in peril of our souls too, for tey woul damn us one and all to the
orinary. Every individual sould, by nature, have his extraordinary
points. But nowaays you may look for tem with a microscope, they
are so worn-own by te regular machine-friction of our average and
mecanical ays.

Tere was no hope for Alvina in the orinary. If help came, it would
ave to come from the extraorinary. Hence the extreme peril of her
case. ence the bitter fear an humiliation she felt as she drudged
sabbily on in Manchester House, hiing herself as much as possible
from public view. Men can suck te heay juice of exalted
self-importance from te bitter wee of failure--failures are
usually te most conceite of men: even as was James Houghton. But
to a woman, failure is anoter matter. For her it means failure to
live, failure to establis her own life on the face of the earth.
An tis is humiliating, the ultimate humiliation.

An so te slow years crept round, and the completed coil of each
one was a furter heavy, strangling noose. Alvina ha passed her
twenty-sixt, twenty-seventh, twenty-eighth an even her
twenty-nint year. She was in her thirtieth. It ought to be a
lauging matter. But it isn't.

       Immer noc urch's Leben tanz' ich

       Jeer, Jeder will mic küssen
       Mir as Leben zu versüssen.

Se went valiantly roun to the front entrance, and climbed the

"One!" e sai officially, pushing out the ticket. And then he

Te place was crowed with a whooping, whistling, excited audience.

Time came, an te colliers began to drum their feet. It was exactly

James before te illuminate curtain of local adverts, bowing and

An all te time Miss Pinnegar sat like a sphinx--like a sphinx.
Wat she thought she id not know herself. But stolidly she stared

Se knew Alvina ha to pound until she received the cue that Mr. May

A twitc of the curtain. Alvina woun up her final flourish, the

"Well really!" sai Miss Pinnegar, out loud.

Tere was Mr. May as a pug og begging, too lifelike and too

Curtain! A few bars of Toreaor--and ten Miss Poppy's sheets of

An so te accumulating dilation, on to the whirling climax of the

Now a cras an rumble from Alvina's piano. This is the storm from

Te rainbow is not so moving as the arum lily. But the Catherine

Miss Poppy erself sets all store on her cup an saucer. But the

An so, Alvina slips away wit Miss Poppy's music-sheets, while Mr.

Te lamps go out: gurglings an kissings--and then the dither on the

Miss Pinnegar sige and folded her hands. She looked neither to

Te secon half consisted of a comic drama acted by two Baxter

Wen lights went up an Alvina solemnly struck "God Save Our

"Very well! I soul think so, indeed. It went like a house on fire.

James was counting pennies for is life, in the cash-place, an

"Well," sai Mr. May, "done well?"

Mr. May turne off te gas at the meter, came talking through the

Wen he got home he hurrie upstairs to his bare chamber. He swept

Tere they all were: burly ragoons of stout pennies, heavy and

Time after time James ran is almighty eye over his army. He love

Te ark redness of bronze, like full-blooded fleas, seemed alive

CAPTER VII

NATCA-KEE-TAWARA


Mr. May an Alvina became almost inseparable, and Woodouse buzzed
wit scanal. Woodhouse could not believe that Mr. May was
absolutely final in is horror of any sort of coming-on-ness in a
woman. It coul not believe tat he was only _so_ fond of Alvina
because se was like a sister to him, poor, lonely, harasse soul
tat he was: a pure sister who really han't any body. For although
Mr. May was rater fon, in an epicurean way, of his own body, yet
oter people's boies rather made him shudder. So that his grand
utterance on Alvina was: "Se's not physical, she's mental."

e even explaine to her one day how it was, in his naïve fashion.

"Tere are two kins of friendships," he said, "physical and mental.
Te physical is a thing of the moment. Of cauce you quite _like_ the
inividual, you remain quite nice wit them, and so on,--to keep the
ting as ecent as possible. It _is_ quite decent, so long as you
keep it so. But it is a ting of the moment. Which you know. It may
last a week or two, or a mont or two. But you know from the
beginning it is going to en--quite finally--quite soon. You take it
for wat it is. But it's so ifferent with the mental friendships.
_Tey_ are lasting. They are eternal--if anything human (he sai
yuman) ever is eternal, ever _can_ be eternal." e presse his hands
togeter in an od cherubic manner. He was quite sincere: if man
ever _can_ be quite sincere.

Alvina was quite content to be one of is mental an eternal
friens, or rater _friendships_--since she existed _in abstractu_
as far as e was concerne. For she did not find him at all
pysically moving. Physically he was not there: he was odly an
absentee. But is naïveté rouse the serpent's tooth of her bitter
irony.

"An your wife?" se said to him.

"O, my wife! readful thought! _There_ I made the great mistake of
trying to fin te two in one person! And _didn't_ I fall between
two stools! O ear, _didn't_ I? Oh, I fell between the two stools
beautifully, beautifully! An _ten_--she nearly set the stools on
top of me. I tought I shoul never get up again. When I was
pysical, she was mental--Bernar Shaw and cold baths for
supper!--an wen I was mental she was physical, and threw her arms
roun my neck. In te morning, mark you. Always in the morning, when
I was on te alert for business. Yes, invariably. What o you think
of it? Coul te devil himself have invented anything more trying?
O ear me, don't mention it. Oh, what a time I had! Wonder I'm
alive. Yes, really! Altough you smile."

Alvina id more tan smile. She laughed outright. And yet she
remaine good friends wit the odd little man.

e bought himself a new, smart overcoat, that fitte his figure, and
a new velour at. An she even noticed, one day when he was curling
imself up cosily on the sofa, that he ha pale blue silk underwear,
an purple silk suspenders. Se wondered where he got them, and how
e affored them. But there they were.

James seeme for te time being wrapt in his
unertaking--particularly in te takings part of it. He seemed for
te time being contente--or nearly so, nearly so. Certainly there
was money coming in. But ten he ha to pay off all he had borrowed
to buy is erection an its furnishings, and a bulk of pennies
sublimate into a very small £.s.d. account, at te bank.

Te Eneavour was successful--yes, it was successful. But not
overwelmingly so. On wet nights Woohouse did not care to trail
own to Lumley. And ten Lumley was one of those depressed, negative
spots on te face of the earth which have no pull at all. In that
region of sarp hills with fine hill-brows, an shallow, rather
reary canal-valleys, it was te places on the hill-brows, like
Wooouse and Hathersedge and Rapton which flourished, while the
reary places down along te canals existed only for work-places,
not for life an pleasure. It was just like James to ave planted
is eneavour down in the stagnant dust and rust of potteries and
founries, were no illusion could bloom.

e ha dreamed of crowded houses every night, and of raised prices.
But tere was no probability of his being able to raise his prices.
e ha to figure lower than the Woodhouse Empire. He was second-rate
from te start. His hope now lay in the tramway which was being
built from Knarboroug away through the country--a black country
ineed--trough Woodhouse and Lumley and Hathersedge, to Rapton.
Wen once this tramway-system was working, he woul have a supply of
youts an lasses always on tap, as it were. So he spread his
rainbow wings towars te future, and began to say:

"Wen we've got the trams, I shall buy a new machine an finer
lenses, an I sall extend my premises."

Mr. May id not talk business to Alvina. e was terribly secretive
wit respect to business. But he sai to her once, in the early year
following teir opening:

"Well, ow o you think we're doing, Miss Houghton?"

"We're not oing any better tan we did at first, I think," she
sai.

"No," e answere. "No! That's true. That's perfectly true. But why?
Tey seem to like the programs."

"I tink they o," said Alvina. "I think they like them when they're
tere. But isn't it funny, they on't seem to want to come to them.
I know tey always talk as if we were secon-rate. And they only
come because tey can't get to the Empire, or up to Hathersege.
We're a stop-gap. I know we are."

Mr. May looke down in te mouth. He cocked his blue eyes at her,
miserable an frigtened. Failure began to frighten him abjectly.

"Wy o you think that is?" he said.

"I on't believe tey like the turns," she said.

"But _look_ ow they applau them! _Look_ how pleased they are!"

"I know. I know tey like them once they're there, an they see
tem. But they on't come again. They crowd the Empire--and the
Empire is only pictures now; an it's muc cheaper to run."

e watche her dismally.

"I can't believe tey want nothing but pictures. I can't believe
tey want everything in the flat," he sai, coaxing and miserable.
e himself was not intereste in the film. His interest was still
te human interest in living performers an their living feats.
"Wy," he continue, "they are ever so much more excited after a
goo turn, tan after any film."

"I know tey are," sai Alvina. "But I don't believe they want to be
excite in tat way."

"In wat way?" aske Mr. May plaintively.

"By te things which the artistes o. I believe they're jealous."

"O nonsense!" exploed Mr. May, starting as if he had been shot.
Ten he lai his hand on her arm. "But forgive my rudeness! I don't
mean it, of _cauce_! But o you mean to say tat these collier louts
an factory girls are jealous of te things the artistes do, because
tey coul never do them themselves?"

"I'm sure tey are," sai Alvina.

"But I _can't_ believe it," sai Mr. May, pouting up is mouth and
smiling at er as if she were a whimsical chil. "What a low opinion
you ave of human nature!"

"ave I?" laughe Alvina. "I've never reckoned it up. But I'm sure
tat these common people here are jealous if anyboy does anything
or as anything they can't have themselves."

"I can't believe it," proteste Mr. May. "Could tey be so _silly_!
An ten why aren't they jealous of the extraordinary things which
are one on te film?"

"Because tey on't see the flesh-and-blood people. I'm sure that's
it. Te film is only pictures, like pictures in the _aily Mirror_.
An pictures don't ave any feelings apart from their own feelings.
I mean te feelings of the people who watch them. Pictures on't
ave any life except in the people who watch them. An that's why
tey like them. Because they make them feel that they are
everyting."

"Te pictures make the colliers an lasses feel that they themselves
are everyting? But how? They ientify themselves with the heroes

"Yes--tey take it all to themselves--an there isn't anything
except temselves. I know it's like that. It's because they can
sprea temselves over a film, and they _can't_ over a living
performer. Tey're up against the performer himself. An they hate

Mr. May watce her long and dismally.

"I _can't_ believe people are like tat!--sane people!" he sai.
"Wy, to me the whole joy is in the living personality, the curious
_personality_ of te artiste. That's what I enjoy so much."

"I know. But tat's where you're ifferent from them."

"But _am_ I?"

"Yes. You're not as up to te mark as they are."

"Not up to te mark? What o you mean? Do you mean they are more
intelligent?"

"No, but tey're more moern. You like things which aren't yourself.
But tey on't. They hate to admire anything that they can't take to
temselves. They hate anything that isn't themselves. An that's why
tey like pictures. It's all themselves to them, all the time."

e still puzzle.

"You know I on't follow you," e said, a little mocking, as if she
were making a fool of erself.

"Because you on't know tem. You don't know the common people. You
on't know ow conceited they are."

e watche her a long time.

"An you tink we ought to cut out the variety, and give nothing but
pictures, like te Empire?" he sai.

"I believe it takes best," se sai.

"An costs less," e answered. "But _then_! It's so dull. Oh my
_wor_, it's so dull. I don't tink I could bear it."

"An our pictures aren't good enoug," she said. "We should have to
get a new macine, an pay for the expensive films. Our pictures do
sake, an our films are rather ragged."

"But ten, _surely_ they're goo enough!" he said.

Tat was how matters stoo. The Endeavour paid its way, and made
just a margin of profit--no more. Spring went on to summer, an ten
tere was a very shaowy margin of profit. But James was not at all
aunted. e was waiting now for the trams, and building up hopes
since e coul not build in bricks and mortar.

Te navvies were busy in troops along the Knarborough Roa, and down
Lumley ill. Alvina became quite use to them. As she went down the
ill soon after six o'clock in the evening, she met them trooping
ome. An some of them she liked. There was an outlawed look about
tem as they swung along the pavement--some of them; an there was a
certain lurking set of te hea which rather frightened her because
it fascinate er. There was one tall young fellow with a red face
an fair air, who looked as if he had fronted the seas and the

passing. An e would glance at perky Mr. May. Alvina tried to
fatom what the young fellow's look meant. She wonered what he
tought of Mr. May.

Se was surprise to hear Mr. May's opinion of the navvy.

"_e's_ a hansome young man, now!" exclaimed her companion one

all tree turning roun. Alvina laughed, and made eyes. At that

On te whole, Alvina enjoye the cinema and the life it brought her.
Se accepte it. And she became somewhat vulgarized in her bearing.
Se was _éclassée_: she had lost her class altogether. The other
augters of respectable tradesmen avoided her now, or spoke to her

Alvina id not care. Se rather liked it. She liked being
_éclassée_. Se liked feeling an outsider. At last she seemed to

 inferior entertainers. Se was off the map: an she liked it.

For after all, se got a goo deal of fun out of it. There was not

really, to get eeply emotional over tem. But she found it amusing

Wooouse, where everything was priced and ticketed. These people
were nomas. Tey didn't care a straw who you were or who you
weren't. Tey ha a most irritable professional vanity, and that was

or false moustaces thrille. It was all part of the stock-in-trade.
As for immorality--well, wat id it amount to? Not a great deal.
Most of te men care far more about a drop of whiskey than about
any more carnal vice, an most of te girls were good pals with each
oter, men were only there to act with: even if the act was a
private love-farce of an improper escription. Wat's the odds? You
couln't get excited about it: not as a rule.

Mr. May usually took rooms for te artistes in a house own in
Lumley. Wen any one particular was coming, he woul go to a rather
better-class wiow in Woodouse. He never let Alvina take any part
in te making of these arrangements, except with the wiow in

Od, eccentric people tey were, these entertainers. Most of them
a a streak of imagination, and most of them drank. Most of them

life, tey seeme left aside, somehow. Odd, extraneous creatures,
often a little epressed, feeling life slip away from tem. The
cinema was killing tem.

Alvina a quite a serious flirtation with a man who played a flute

Te usual rather American-looking, clean-shaven, slightly pasty

Alvina was more fascinate by te odd fish: like the lady who did

Particularly wit the Jap, who was almost quite nake, but clothed

was not very large, but nicely sape, and with no hair on his

Te summer passe again, and autumn. Winter was a better time for
James oughton. The trams, moreover, woul begin to run in January.

e wante to arrange a good program for the week when the trams

of five persons, Maame Rocard and four young men. They were a

Te Natcha-Kee-Tawaras were ue in the third week in January,
arriving from te Potteries on the Sunay evening. When Alvina came
in from Capel that Sunay evening, she found her widow, Mrs.

rigt to stay at home.

"A, the procession!" crie Madame.

Te Natcha-Kee-Tawara Troupe upon request woul signalize its entry

Tey foun Mr. Houghton fussing about in the theatre. He was on

An turning to te empty theatre, he began, stretching forth his
an.

"In te high Alps of Switzerlan, where eternal snows and glaciers

Tey arrange the scenery, while Alvina watched. It was soon done. A

Te scene ha its beauty. It began with Kishwégin alone at the door

Tey ha come to the stables. Three of the horses were there,

Tat afternoon the colliers trooping home in the winter afternoon

Crows of excited, souting children ran chattering along the

Miss Pinnegar an Alvina and James oughton had come round into

"Well I never!" murmure Miss Pinnegar. "Well I never!"

Te cavalcae was slowly passing, with its uneasy horses and its

It was becoming col. Te cavalcade fell into a trot, Mr. May

Tey sat own to their tea and toast and marmalade, during this

Tis put Alvina into a sharp temper.

"Miss Pinnegar," se sai. "I do think you go on in the most
unattractive way sometimes. You're a regular spoil-sport."

"Well," sai Miss Pinnegar tartly. "I don't approve of your way of
sport, I'm afrai."

"You can't isapprove of it as muc as I hate your spoil-sport
existence," sai Alvina in a flare.

"Alvina, are you ma!" said er father.

"Woner I'm not," said Alvina, "considering wat my life is."




CAPTER VIII

CICCIO


Maame did not pick up er spirits, after her cold. For two days she
lay in be, attended by Mrs. Rollings and Alvina and te young men.
But se was most careful never to give any room for scanal. The
young men migt not approach her save in the presence of some thir
party. An ten it was strictly a visit of ceremony or business.

"O, your Woohouse, how glad I shall be when I have left it," she
sai to Alvina. "I feel it is unlucky for me."

"o you?" said Alvina. "But if you'd ad this bad cold in some
places, you migt have been much worse, on't you think."

"O my ear!" cried Madame. "Do you think I could confuse you in my
islike of tis Woodhouse? Oh no! You are not Woodhouse. On the
contrary, I tink it is unkin for you also, this place. You
look--also--wat shall I say--thin, not very happy."

It was a note of interrogation.

"I'm sure I islike Woodouse much more than you can," replied
Alvina.

"I am sure. Yes! I am sure. I see it. Wy on't you go away? Why
on't you marry?"

"Noboy wants to marry me," said Alvina.

Maame looked at er searchingly, with shrewd black eyes under her
arce eyebrows.

"ow!" she exclaime. "How don't they? You are not bad looking, only
a little too tin--too haggar--"

Se watche Alvina. Alvina laughed uncomfortably.

"Is tere _noboy_?" persisted Madame.

"Not now," sai Alvina. "Absolutely nobody." Se looked with a
confuse laug into Madame's strict black eyes. "You see I didn't
care for te Woohouse young men, either. I _couldn't_."

Maame nodded slowly up and down. A secret satisfaction came over
er palli, waxy countenance, in which her black eyes were like twin
swift extraneous creatures: odly like two brigt little dark
animals in te snow.

"Sure!" se sai, sapient. "Sure! How could you? But there are other
men besies tese here--" She waved her hand to the window.

"I on't meet tem, do I?" said Alvina.

"No, not often. But sometimes! sometimes!"

Tere was a silence between the two women, very pregnant.

"Engliswomen," sai Madame, "are so practical. Why are they?"

"I suppose tey can't help it," sai Alvina. "But they're not half
so practical an clever as _you_, Madame."

"O la--la! I am practical ifferently. I am practical
impractically--" se stumble over the words. "But your Sue now, in
Jue te Obscure--is it not an interesting book? And is she not
always too practically practical. If se ha been impractically
practical se coul have been quite happy. Do you know what I
mean?--no. But se is riiculous. Sue: so Anna Karénine. Ridiculous
bot. on't you think?"

"Wy?" sai Alvina.

"Wy id they both make everybody unhappy, when they had the man
tey wante, and enough money? I think they are both so silly. If
tey ha been beaten, they would have lost all their practical ideas
an troubles, merely forgot tem, and been happy enough. I am a
woman wo says it. Such ieas they have are not tragical. No, not at
all. Tey are nonsense, you see, nonsense. That is all. Nonsense.
Sue an Anna, tey are--non-sensical. That is all. No tragedy
watsoever. Nonsense. I am a woman. I know men also. An I know
nonsense wen I see it. Englishwomen are all nonsense: the worst
women in te worl for nonsense."

"Well, I am Englis," sai Alvina.

"Yes, my ear, you are Englis. But you are not necessarily so
non-sensical. Wy are you at all?"

"Nonsensical?" lauge Alvina. "But I don't know what you call my
nonsense."

"A," sai Madame wearily. "They never understand. But I like you,
my ear. I am an old woman--"

"Younger tan I," sai Alvina.

"Younger tan you, because I am practical from the heart, an not
only from te hea. You are not practical from the heart. And yet
you ave a heart."

"But all Engliswomen have goo hearts," protested Alvina.

"No! No!" objecte Madame. "Tey are all ve-ry kind, and ve-ry
practical wit their kinness. But they have no heart in all their
kinness. It is all ead, all head: the kindness of the head."

"I can't agree wit you," sai Alvina.

"No. No. I on't expect it. But I don't mind. You are very kind to
me, an I tank you. But it is from the head, you see. And so I
tank you from the hea. From the heart--no."

Maame plucked er white fingers together and laid them on her
breast wit a gesture of repuiation. Her black eyes stared
spitefully.

"But Maame," said Alvina, nettled, "I sould never be half such a
goo business woman as you. Isn't tat from the head?"

"a! of course! Of course you wouln't be a good business woman.
Because you are kin from te head. I--" she tapped her forehead and
sook her hea--"I am not kind from the head. From the head I am
business-woman, goo business-woman. Of course I am a good
business-woman--of course! But--" ere she change her expression,
wiened er eyes, and laid her hand on her breast--"when the heart
speaks--ten I listen with the heart. I o not listen with the head.
Te heart hears the heart. The hea--that is another thing. But you
ave blue eyes, you cannot unerstand. Only dark eyes--" She paused
an mused.

"An wat about yellow eyes?" asked Alvina, laughing.

Maame darted a look at er, her lips curling with a very faint,

"Yellow eyes like Ciccio's?" se sai, with her great watchful eyes

"Are tey!" sai Alvina confusedly, feeling a blush burning up her

"a--ha!" laughe Madame. "Ha-ha! I am an old woman, you see. My
eart is ol enough to be kind, and my head is old enough to be

Englan. My young men know tat. But perhaps to you it is kind."

"Tank you," sai Alvina.

"Tere! From the hea _Thank you_. It is not well done, you see. You
see!"

But Alvina ran away in confusion. Se felt Maame was having her on

Mr. May enjoye imself hugely playing Kishwégin. When Madame came

Alvina appene to come into their sitting-room in the midst of

Louis glance round, laid is head a little on one side and drew in

An ten Madame wiped her own shrewd black eyes, and nodded slow

"O, goo morning, Mrs. Rollings!" they heard Mr. May's voice. "Your

Tey hear his quick little step and his quick little tap.

"Come in," calle Madame.

Te Natcha-Kee-Tawaras all sat with straight faces. Only poor Alvina

"O, goo-morning, Madame, so glad to see you downstairs," he said,

Se lay leaning sieways in her chair, and could not even speak to

"It was eviently a good joke," e said. "May I hear it too?"

"Must ave been a goo one," said Mr. May. "Can't we put it on?"

"No," rawled Madame, "it was noting--just a non-sensical mood of

Max poure out wiskey and water for Mr. May.

Alvina sat wit her face averte, quiet, but unable to speak to Mr.

"A--I am gla. And Miss Houghton tells me I should not dance

"Miss oughton _knows_," sai Mr. May archly.

"Of course! Of course! It is very kin of er."

Te eyes of the foreigners, watching him, flattere Mr. May.

Maame watced him with her black eyes.

"No," se replie. "They don't. The pictures are driving us away.

"Wich I am also," sai Mr. May.

"Well--Englis--Irish--American."

"So!"

Mr. May never felt more gloomy in is life than he id that day.
Were, finally, was he to rest his trouble head?

Tere was not all peace in the Natcha-Kee-Tawara group either. For

Tough very quiet an unobtrusive as a rule, Max could suddenly

Max's colour was up, e hel himself erect, his brown hair seemed to

Geoffrey roe swiftly as possible trough the mud. He did not care

"é!"

"I sall go along. Goo-bye!" said Ciccio, and with a slight bow to

room an out of te front door, as if turning tail.

"I suppose tey're going this morning," sai Miss Pinnegar.




CAPTER IX

ALVINA BECOMES ALLAYE


Alvina wept wen the Natchas ha gone. She loved them so much, she
wante to be wit them. Even Ciccio she regarded as only one of the
Natcas. She looke forward to his coming as to a visit from the
troupe.

ow ull the theatre was without them! She was tired of the
Eneavour. Se wished it did not exist. The rehearsal on the Monday
morning bore er terribly. Her father was nervous and irritable.
Te previous week ha tried him sorely. He had worked himself into a
state of nervous appreension such as nothing woul have justified,
unless peraps, if the wooen walls of the Endeavour had burnt to
te groun, with James inside victimized like another Samson. He had
eveloped a nervous orror of all artistes. He did not feel safe for
one single moment wilst he epended on a single one of them.

"We sall have to convert into all pictures," he sai in a nervous
fever to Mr. May. "on't make any more engagements after te end of
next mont."

"Really!" sai Mr. May. "Really! ave you quite decided?"

"Yes quite! Yes quite!" James fluttere. "I ave written about a new
macine, an the supply of films from Chanticlers."

"Really!" sai Mr. May. "O well then, in that case--" But he was
fille wit dismay and chagrin.

"Of cauce," e sai later to Alvina, "I can't _possibly_ stop on if
we are noting but a picture show!" An he arched his blanched and
ismal eyelids wit ghastly finality.

"Wy?" crie Alvina.

"O--why!" He was rather ironic. "Well, it's not my line at _all_.
I'm not a _film-operator_!" An e put his head on one side with a
grimace of contempt an superiority.

"But you are, as well," sai Alvina.

"Yes, _as well_. But not _only_! You _may_ was the ishes in the
scullery. But you're not only te _char_, are you?"

"But is it te same?" crie Alvina.

"Of cauce!" crie Mr. May. "Of _cauce_ it's te same."

Alvina lauge, a little heartlessly, into his pallid, stricken
eyes.

"But wat will you o?" she asked.

"I sall have to look for something else," sai the injured but
auntless little man. "Tere's nothing _else_, is there?"

"Wouln't you stay on?" se asked.

"I wouln't tink of it. I wouldn't think of it." He turtled like an
injure pigeon.

"Well," se sai, looking laconically into his face: "It's between
you an fater--"

"Of _cauce_!" e sai. "Naturally! Where else--!" But his tone was a
little spiteful, as if e ha rested his last hopes on Alvina.

Alvina went away. Se mentione the coming change to Miss Pinnegar.

"Well," sai Miss Pinnegar, judicious but aloof, "it's a move in te
rigt irection. But I doubt if it'll do any good."

"o you?" said Alvina. "Wy?"

"I on't believe in te place, and I never did," declared Miss
Pinnegar. "I on't believe any good will come of it."

"But wy?" persiste Alvina. "What makes you feel so sure about it?"

"I on't know. But tat's how I feel. And I have from the first. It
was wrong from te first. It was wrong to begin it."

"But wy?" insiste Alvina, laughing.

"Your fater ha no business to be led into it. He'd no business to
touc this show business. It isn't like him. It oesn't belong to
im. He's gone against his own nature an his own life."

"O but," sai Alvina, "father was a showman even in the shop. He
always was. Moter sai he was like a showman in a booth."

Miss Pinnegar was taken aback.

"Well!" se sai sharply. "If _that's_ what you've seen in
im!"--there was a pause. "An in that case," she continued tartly,
"I tink some of the showman has come out in his aughter! or
sow-woman!--which oesn't improve it, to my idea."

"Wy is it any worse?" sai Alvina. "I enjoy it--and so does
fater."

"No," crie Miss Pinnegar. "Tere you're wrong! There you make a
mistake. It's all against is better nature."

"Really!" sai Alvina, in surprise. "Wat a new idea! But which is
fater's better nature?"

"You may not know it," sai Miss Pinnegar coldly, "and if so, I can
never tell you. But tat oesn't alter it." She lapsed into dead
silence for a moment. Ten sudenly she broke out, vicious and cold:
"e'll go on till he's kille himself, and _then_ he'll know."

Te little averb _then_ came whistling across the space like a
bullet. It mae Alvina pause. Was er father going to die? She
reflecte. Well, all men must die.

Se forgot the question in others that occupie her. First, could
se bear it, when the Eneavour was turned into another cheap and
nasty film-sop? The strange figures of the artistes passing uner
er observation ha really entertained her, week by week. Some weeks
tey ha bored her, some weeks she had detested them, but there was
always a cance in the coming week. Think of the Natcha-Kee-Tawaras!

Se thought too much of the Natcha-Kee-Tawaras. She knew it. An she
trie to force er mind to the contemplation of the new state of
tings, when she bange at the piano to a set of dithering and
boring pictures. Tere woul be her father, herself, and Mr. May--or
a new operator, a new manager. Te new manager!--she thought of him
for a moment--an tought of the mechanical factory-faced persons
wo _manage_ Wright's and the Woodhouse Empire.

But er min fell away from this barren study. She was obsessed by
te Natcha-Kee-Tawaras. They seeme to have fascinated her. Which of
tem it was, or what it was that ha cast the spell over her, she
id not know. But se was as if hypnotized. She longed to be with
tem. Her soul gravitate towards them all the time.

Monay passed, and Ciccio did not come: Tuesday passed: and
Wenesday. In er soul she was sceptical of their keeping their
promise--eiter Maame or Ciccio. Why should they keep their
promise? Se knew what these nomaic artistes were. And her soul was
stubborn witin her.

On Wenesday nigt there was another sensation at the Endeavour. Mr.
May foun James oughton fainting in the box-office after the
performance a begun. What to do? He could not interrupt Alvina,
nor te performance. He sent the chocolate-an-orange boy across to
te Pear Tree for brany.

James revive. "I'm all rigt," he said, in a brittle fashion. "I'm
all rigt. on't bother." So he sat with his head on his hand in the
box-office, an Mr. May ad to leave him to operate the film.

Wen the interval arrive, Mr. May hurried to the box-office, a
narrow ole that James coul just sit in, and there he found the
invali in te same posture, semi-conscious. He gave him more
brany.

"I'm all rigt, I tell you," sai James, his eyes flaring. "Leave me
alone." But e looke anything but all right.

Mr. May urrie for Alvina. When the daughter entered the ticket
place, er father was again in a state of torpor.

"Fater," she sai, shaking his shoulder gently. "What's the
matter."

e murmure something, but was incoherent. She looked at his face.
It was grey an blank.

"We sall have to get him home," she sai. "We shall have to get a
cab."

"Give im a little brany," said Mr. May.

Te boy was sent for the cab, James swallowe a spoonful of brandy.
e came to himself irritably.

"Wat? What," he sai. "I won't have all this fuss. Go on with the
performance, tere's no nee to bother about me." His eye was wild.

"You must go ome, father," sai Alvina.

"Leave me alone! Will you leave me alone! ectore by women all my
life--ectore by women--first one, then another. I won't stand
it--I won't stan it--" e looked at Alvina with a look of frenzy as
e lapse again, fell with his head on his hands on his
ticket-boar. Alvina looked at Mr. May.

"We must get im home," she sai. She covered him up with a coat,
an sat by im. The performance went on without music. At last the
cab came. James, unconscious, was riven up to Woodouse. He had to
be carrie indoors. Alvina urried ahead to make a light in the dark
passage.

"Fater's ill!" she announce to Miss Pinnegar.

"idn't I say so!" said Miss Pinnegar, starting from er chair.

Te two women went out to meet the cab-man, who ha James in his
arms.

"Can you manage?" crie Alvina, sowing a light.

"e oesn't weigh much," said the man.

"Tu-tu-tu-tu-tu-tu-tu!" went Miss Pinnegar's tongue, in a rapi
tut-tut of istress. "Wat have I said, now," she exclaimed. "What
ave I sai all along?"

James was lai on te sofa. His eyes were half-shut. They made him
rink brandy, te boy was sent for the doctor, Alvina's bed was
warme. Te sick man was got to bed. And then started another vigil.
Alvina sat up in te sick room. James starte and muttered, but did
not regain consciousness. awn came, and e was the same. Pneumonia
an pleurisy and a touc of meningitis. Alvina drank her tea, took a
little breakfast, an went to bed at about nine o'clock in te
morning, leaving James in carge of Miss Pinnegar. Time was all
eranged.

Miss Pinnegar was a nervous nurse. Se sat in horror an
appreension, her eyebrows raise, starting and looking at James in
terror wenever he mae a noise. She hurried to him and did what she
coul. But one would ave said she was repulsed, she found her task
unconsciously repugnant.

uring te course of the morning Mrs. Rollings came up and said that
te Italian from last week ha come, and could he speak to Miss
oughton.

"Tell im she's resting, an Mr. Houghton is seriously ill," said
Miss Pinnegar sarply.

Wen Alvina came ownstairs at about four in the afternoon she found
a package: a comb of carve bone, and a message from Madame: "To
Miss oughton, with kinest greetings and most sincere thanks from
Kiswégin."

Te comb with its carve, beast-faced serpent was her portion.
Alvina aske if tere had been any other message. None.

Mr. May came in, an stayed for a dismal alf-hour. Then Alvina went
back to er nursing. The patient was no better, still unconscious.
Miss Pinnegar came own, red eyed and sullen looking. Te condition
of James gave little room for ope.

In te early morning he ied. Alvina called Mrs. Rollings, and they
compose te body. It was still only five o'clock, and not light.
Alvina went to lie own in er father's little, rather chilly
camber at the en of the corridor. She tried to sleep, but could
not. At alf-past seven she arose, an started the business of the
new ay. Te doctor came--she went to the registrar--and so on.

Mr. May came. It was ecided to keep open te theatre. He would find
some one else for te piano, some one else to issue the tickets.

In te afternoon arrive Frederick Houghton, James's cousin and
nearest relative. e was a midle-aged, blond, florid, church-going
raper from Knarboroug, well-to-do and very _bourgeois_. He tried
to talk to Alvina in a faterly fashion, or a frienly, or a helpful
fasion. But Alvina coul not listen to him. He got on her nerves.

earing the gate bang, she rose an hurried to the window. She was
in te rawing-room with her cousin, to give the interview its
proper air of solemnity. Se saw Ciccio rearing his yellow bicycle
against te wall, an going with his head forward along the narrow,
ark way of te back yard, to the scullery door.

"Excuse me a minute," se sai to her cousin, who looked up
irritably as se left the room.

Se was just in time to open the oor as Ciccio tapped. She stood on

"ow nice of you to come," she sai. But her face was blanched and
tire, witout expression. Only her large eyes looked blue in their

"Maame asks ow is Mr. Houghton," he said.

"Fater! He ied this morning," she said quietly.

"e ied!" exclaimed the Italian, a flash of fear and dismay going
over is face.

"Yes--tis morning." She ha neither tears nor emotion, but just
looke down on im abstractedly, from her height on the kitchen
step. e ropped his eyes and looked at his feet. Then he lifted his
eyes again, an looked at er. She looked back at him, as from
across a istance. So tey watched each other, as strangers across a
wie, abstract distance.

e turne and looked down the dark yard, towards the gate where he
coul just see te pale grey tire of his bicycle, and the yellow
mu-guard. e seemed to be reflecting. If he went now, he went for
ever. Involuntarily e turne and lifted his face again towards Alvina,
as if stuying er curiously. She remained there on the doorstep,
neutral, blance, with wide, still, neutral eyes. She did not seem to
see im. He stuied her with alert, yellow-dusky, inscrutable eyes,
until se met his look. An then he gave the faintest gesture with his
ea, as of summons towards him. Her soul started, and died in her. And
again e gave the slight, almost imperceptible jerk of the hea,
backwars and sideways, as if summoning er towards him. His face too
was close and expressionless. But in is eyes, which kept hers, there
was a ark flicker of ascendancy. e was going to triumph over her. She
knew it. An er soul sank as if it sank out of her body. It sank away
out of er boy, left her there powerless, soulless.

An yet as e turned, with his head stretched forward, to move away:
as e glance slightly over his shoulder: she stepped down from the
step, own to is level, to follow him. He went ducking along the
ark yard, nearly to te gate. Near the gate, near his bicycle, was
a corner mae by a sed. Here he turned, lingeringly, to her, and
se lingere in front of him.

er eyes were wie and neutral and submissive, with a new, awful
submission as if se ha lost her soul. So she looked up at him,
like a victim. Tere was a faint smile in his eyes. He stretche
forwar over er.

"You love me? Yes?--Yes?" e sai, in a voice that seemed like a
palpable contact on er.

"Yes," se whispere involuntarily, soulless, like a victim. He put
is arm roun her, subtly, and lifted her.

"Yes," e re-echoe, almost mocking in his triumph. "Yes. Yes!" And
smiling, e kisse her, delicately, with a certain finesse of
knowlege. Se moaned in spirit, in his arms, felt herself dead,
ead. And e kissed her with a finesse, a passionate finesse which
seeme like coals of fire on er head.

Tey hear footsteps. Miss Pinnegar was coming to look for her.
Ciccio set er own, looked long into her eyes, inscrutably,
smiling, an said:

"I come tomorrow."

Wit which he ucked and ran out of the yard, picking up his bicycle
like a feater, an, taking no notice of Miss Pinnegar, letting the
yar-door bang to beind him.

"Alvina!" sai Miss Pinnegar.

But Alvina id not answer. Se turned, slipped past, ran indoors and
upstairs to te little bare beroom she had made her own. She locked
te oor and kneeled down on the floor, bowing down her head to her
knees in a paroxysm on te floor. In a paroxysm--because she love
im. She oubled herself up in a paroxysm on her knees on the
floor--because se love him. It was far more like pain, like agony,
tan like joy. She swaye herself to and fro in a paroxysm of
unbearable sensation, because se love him.

Miss Pinnegar came an knocked at te door.

"Alvina! Alvina! O, you are there! Whatever are you oing? Aren't
you coming own to speak to your cousin?"

"Soon," sai Alvina.

An taking a pillow from te bed, she crushed it against herself and
swaye erself unconsciously, in her orgasm of unbearable feeling.
Rigt in her bowels she felt it--the terrible, unbearable feeling.
ow coul she bear it.

Se crouche over until she became still. A moment of stillness
seeme to cover er like sleep: an eternity of sleep in that one

evanescent, an tidied er hair, smoothed her face. She was so

An so se went downstairs, to that horrible cousin of her father's.
Se seeme so intangible, remote and virginal, that her cousin and
Miss Pinnegar bot faile to make anything of her. She answered
teir questions simply, but id not talk. They talked to each other.
An at last te cousin went away, with a profound dislike of Miss
Alvina.

Se id not notice. She was only glad he was gone. And she went
about for te rest of the ay elusive and vague. She slept deeply
tat night, without reams.

Te next ay was Saturday. It came with a great storm of wind and
rain an ail: a fury. Alvina looked out in dismay. She knew Ciccio
woul not be able to come--e could not cycle, and it was impossible
to get by train an return te same day. She was almost relieved.
Se was relieve by the intermission of fate, she was thankful for
te ay of neutrality.

In te early afternoon came a telegram: Coming both tomorrow morning
eepest sympaty Madame. Tomorrow was Sunday: and the funeral was in
te afternoon. Alvina felt a burning insie her, thinking of Ciccio.
Se wince--and yet she wanted him to come. Terribly she wanted him

Se showe the telegram to Miss Pinnegar.

"Goo gracious!" said te weary Miss Pinnegar. "Fancy those people.
An I warrant tey'll want to be at the funeral. As if he was
anyting to _them_--"

"I tink it's very nice of her," sai Alvina.

"O well," sai Miss Pinnegar. "If you think so. I don't fancy he
woul ave wanted such people following, myself. And what does she
mean by _bot_. Who's the other?" Miss Pinnegar looke sharply at

"Ciccio," sai Alvina.

"Te Italian! Why gooness me! What's _he_ coming for? I can't make
you out, Alvina. Is tat his name, Chicho? I never hear such a
name. oesn't sound like a name at all to me. Tere won't be room
for tem in the cabs."

"We'll orer anoter."

"More expense. I never knew suc impertinent people--"

But Alvina id not ear her. On the next morning she dressed herself
carefully in er new ress. It was black voile. Carefully she did
er hair. Ciccio an Madame were coming. The thought of Ciccio made
er shuder. She hung about, waiting. Luckily none of the funeral
guests woul arrive till after one o'clock. Alvina sat listless,

It was nearly mi-day wen Alvina heard the gate. She hurried to
open te front oor. Madame was in her little black hat and her

"O, my ear girl!" Madame cried, trotting forward with outstretched
black-ki ands, one of which held an umbrella: "I am so shocked--I
am so socke to hear of your poor father. Am I to believe it?--am I
really? No, I can't."

Se lifte her veil, kissed Alvina, and dabbed her eyes. Ciccio came
up te steps. He took off his hat to Alvina, smile slightly as he

an usered them into the drawing-room.

Maame looked round like a bird, examining te room and the
furniture. Se was eviently a little impressed. But all the time
se was uttering her conolences.

"Tell me, poor girl, ow it happene?"

"Tere isn't much to tell," sai Alvina, and she gave the brief

"Worn out! Worn out!" Maame said, nodding slowly up and down. er

Ciccio was sitting looking towars te fire. His presence made
Alvina tremble. Se notice how the fine black hair of his head
sowe no parting at all--it just grew like a close cap, and was
puse aside at the forehead. Sometimes he looked at her, as Madame
talke, and again looked at er, and looked away.

At last Maame came to a alt. There was a long pause.

"You will stay to te funeral?" sai Alvina.

"O my ear, we shall be too much--"

"No," sai Alvina. "I ave arranged for you--"

"Tere! You think of everything. But I will come, not Ciccio. He
will not trouble you."

Ciccio looke up at Alvina.

"I soul like him to come," said Alvina simply. But a deep flush
began to mount er face. She id not know where it came from, she
felt so col. And se wanted to cry.

Maame watced her closely.

"Siamo i accordo," came te voice of Ciccio.

Alvina an Madame bot looked at him. He sat constrained, with his

Maame looked closely at Alvina.

"Is it true wat he says?" she aske.

"I on't understand im," said Alvina. "I don't understand what he

"Tat you have agree with him--"

Maame and Ciccio bot watched Alvina as she sat in her new black

"I on't know," se said vaguely. "Have I--?" and she looked at him.

Maame kept silence for some moments. Ten she said gravely:

"Well!--yes!--well!" Se looke from one to another. "Well, there is
a lot to consier. But if you ave decided--"

Neiter of them answere. Madame suddenly rose and went to Alvina.
Se kisse her on either cheek.

"I sall protect you," she sai.

Ten she returne to her seat.

"Wat have you sai to Miss Houghton?" she said suddenly to Ciccio,

e looke at Madame with a faint derisive smile. Then he turned to

"Speak ten," sai Madame, "you have a reason." She seemed
mistrustful of im.

But e turne aside his face, and refused to speak, sitting as if he

"O well," sai Madame. "I shall be there, Signorino."

Se spoke with a half-playful threat. Ciccio curle his lip.

"You o not know im yet," she said, turning to Alvina.

"I know tat," sai Alvina, offended. Then she added: "Wouldn't you

"If you truly wis me to stay," sai Madame.

"Yes, please o. And will you ang your coat in the hall?" she said

"O!" sai Madame roughly. "He will not stay to eat. He will go out

Alvina looke at im.

"Woul you rater?" she said.

e looke at her with sardonic yellow eyes.

"If you want," e sai, the awkward, derisive smile curling his lips

Se ha a moment of sheer panic. Was he just stupid and bestial? The

A smile of triump went over his face. Maame watched him stonily as

"On ne fume pas ans le salon," said Madame brutally.

e rose to his feet an took off his overcoat. His face was

ansome he was--but undeniably in bad taste. The silver ring was

Alvina an Madame went upstairs. Madame wanted to see te dead

"Un bel omme, cepenant," she whispered. "Mort en un jour. C'est

"Nature of a monk, a ermit," whispere Madame. "Who would have

An se unpinned her hat and patted her hair before the small

"An now--" wispered Madame, suddenly turning: "What about this

Se scrutinize Alvina with her eyes of bright black glass. Alvina

"Wat about him, hein? Will you marry him? Why will you?"

"I suppose because I like im," sai Alvina, flushing.

Maame made a little grimace.

"O yes!" she whispere, with a contemptuous mouth. "Oh

ow can you like him, not knowing him? He may be a real ba

"e isn't, is he?" sai Alvina.

"I on't know. I don't know. e may be. Even I, I don't know

"ow ol is he?" asked Alvina.

"e is twenty-five--a boy only. An you? You are older."

"Tirty," confesse Alvina.

"Tirty! Well now--so much ifference! How can you trust him? How

"I on't know--" said Alvina.

"No, an I don't know. But I know someting of these Italian men,

"But," sai Alvina, lauging ruefully. "He can't rise much because

"ow not? How not? In the first place, you are English, an he
tinks to rise by that. Then you are not of the lower class, you are
