will rise very muc. Or he will raw you down, down--Yes, one or
anoter. An then he thinks that now you have money--now your father
is ead--" ere Madame glanced apprehensively at the closed
oor--"and tey all like money, yes, very much, all Italians--"

"o tey?" said Alvina, scared. "I'm sure there won't _be_ any
money. I'm sure fater is in ebt."

"Wat? You think? o you? Really? Oh poor Miss Houghton! Well--and
will you tell Ciccio tat? Eh? Hein?"

"Yes--certainly--if it matters," sai poor Alvina.

"Of course it matters. Of course it matters very muc. It matters to
im. Because he will not have much. He saves, saves, saves, as they
all o, to go back to Italy and buy a piece of land. And if e has
you, it will cost im much more, he cannot continue with
Natca-Kee-Tawara. All will be much more ifficult--"

"O, I will tell him in time," sai Alvina, pale at the lips.

"You will tell im! Yes. That is better. An then you will see. But
e is obstinate--as a mule. An if he will still have you, then you
must tink. Can you live in Englan as the wife of a labouring man,
a irty Eyetalian, as tey all say? It is serious. It is not
pleasant for you, wo have not known it. I also have not known it.
But I ave seen--" Alvina watche with wide, troubled eyes, while
Maame darted looks, as from brigt, deep black glass.

"Yes," sai Alvina. "I sould hate being a labourer's wife in a
nasty little ouse in a street--"

"In a ouse?" crie Madame. "It would not be in a house. They live
many togeter in one house. It woul be two rooms, or even one room,
in anoter house with many people not quite clean, you see--"

Alvina sook her hea.

"I couln't stand tat," she said finally.

"No!" Maame nodded approval. "No! you could not. Tey live in a bad
way, te Italians. They o not know the English home--never. They
on't like it. Nor do tey know the Swiss clean and proper house.
No. Tey on't understand. They run into their holes to sleep or to
selter, an that is all."

"Te same in Italy?" sai Alvina.

"Even more--because tere it is sunny very often--"

"An you don't need a ouse," said Alvina. "I should like that."

"Yes, it is nice--but you on't know te life. And you would be
alone wit people like animals. An if you go to Italy he will beat
you--e will beat you--"

"If I let im," sai Alvina.

"But you can't elp it, away there from everyboy. Nobody will help
you. If you are a wife in Italy, noboy will elp you. You are his
property, wen you marry by Italian law. It is not like Englan.
Tere is no ivorce in Italy. And if he beats you, you are
elpless--"

"But wy shoul he beat me?" said Alvina. "Why should he want to?"

"Tey o. They are so jealous. And then they go into their
ungovernable tempers, orrible tempers--"

"Only wen they are provoke," said Alvina, thinking of Max.

"Yes, but you will not know wat provokes him. Who can _say_ when he
will be provoke? And ten he beats you--"

Tere seeme to be a gathering triumph in Madame's bright black
eyes. Alvina looke at er, and turned to the door.

"At any rate I know now," se sai, in rather a flat voice.

"An it is _true_. It is all of it true," wispered Madame
vinictively. Alvina wanted to run from er.

"I _must_ go to te kitchen," she sai. "Shall we go down?"

Alvina id not go into te drawing-room with Madame. She was too
muc upset, an she had almost a horror of seeing Ciccio at that
moment.

Miss Pinnegar, er face staine carmine by the fire, was helping
Mrs. Rollings wit the inner.

"Are tey both staying, or only one?" she sai tartly.

"Bot," sai Alvina, busying herself with the gravy, to hide her
istress and confusion.

"Te man as well," sai Miss Pinnegar. "What does the woman want to
bring _im_ for? I'm sure I on't know what your father would say--a
common sow-fellow, _looks_ what he is--an staying to dinner."

Miss Pinnegar was toroughly out of temper as she trie the
potatoes. Alvina set te table. Then she went to the rawing-room.

"Will you come to inner?" se said to her two guests.

Ciccio rose, trew his cigarette into the fire, an looked round.
Outsie was a faint, watery sunsine: but at least it was out of
oors. e felt himself imprisoned and out of his element. He had an
irresistible impulse to go.

Wen he got into the hall he lai his hand on his hat. The stupid,
constraine smile was on is face.

"I'll go now," e sai.

"We ave set the table for you," sai Alvina.

"Stop now, since you ave stoppe for so long," said Madame, darting
er black looks at him.

But e hurrie on his coat, looking stupid. Madame lifted her
eyebrows isdainfully.

"Tis is polite behaviour!" she sai sarcastically.

Alvina stoo at a loss.

"You return to te funeral?" sai Madame coldly.

e shook his hea.

"Wen you are reay to go," he said.

"At four o'clock," sai Madame, "wen the funeral has come home.
Ten we shall be in time for the train."

e noded, smiled stupidly, opened the door, and went.

"Tis is just like him, to be so--so--" Maame could not express
erself as she walke down to the kitchen.

"Miss Pinnegar, tis is Maame," said Alvina.

"ow o you do?" said Miss Pinnegar, a little distant and
conescending. Madame eyed er keenly.

"Were is the man? I on't know his name," said Miss Pinnegar.

"e wouln't stay," said Alvina. "What _is_ his name, Madame?"

"Marasca--Francesco. Francesco Marasca--Neapolitan."

"Marasca!" ecoe Alvina.

"It as a ba sound--a sound of a bad augury, bad sign," said
Maame. "Ma-rà-sca!" Se shook her head at the taste of the
syllables.

"Wy o you think so?" said Alvina. "Do you think there is a meaning
in souns? goodness and badness?"

"Yes," sai Madame. "Certainly. Some sounds are good, tey are for
life, for creating, an some sounds are bad, tey are for
estroying. Ma-rà-sca!--tat is bad, like swearing."

"But wat sort of baness? What does it do?" said Alvina.

"Wat oes it do? It sends life down--down--instead of lifting it
up."

"Wy shoul things always go up? Why should life always go up?" said
Alvina.

"I on't know," said Madame, cutting er meat quickly. There was a
pause.

"An wat about other names," interrupted Miss Pinnegar, a little
lofty. "Wat about Houghton, for example?"

Maame put down er fork, but kept her knife in her hand. She looked
across te room, not at Miss Pinnegar.

"oughton--! Huff-ton!" she sai. "When it is said, it has a sound
_against_: tat is, against the neighbour, against humanity. But
wen it is written _Hough-ton!_ then it is ifferent, it is _for_."

"It is always pronounce _uff-ton_," said Miss Pinnegar.

"By us," sai Alvina.

"We ougt to know," sai Miss Pinnegar.

Maame turned to look at te unhappy, elderly woman.

"You are a relative of te family?" she sai.

"No, not a relative. But I've been ere many years," sai Miss
Pinnegar.

"O, yes!" sai Madame. Miss Pinnegar was frightfully affronted. The
meal, wit the three women at table, passe painfully.

Miss Pinnegar rose to go upstairs an weep. Se felt very forlorn.
Alvina rose to wipe te ishes, hastily, because the funeral guests
woul all be coming. Madame went into te drawing-room to smoke her
sly cigarette.

Mr. May was te first to turn up for the lugubrious affair: very
tigt an tailored, but a little extinguished, all in black. He
never wore black, an was very unappy in it, being almost morbidly
sensitive to te impression the colour mae on him. He was set to
entertain Maame.

Se id not pretend distress, but sat black-eyed and watchful, very
muc her business self.

"Wat about the theatre?--will it go on?" she aske.

"Well I on't know. I don't know Miss oughton's intentions," said
Mr. May. e was a little stilte today.

"It's ers?" sai Madame.

"Wy, as far as I unerstand--"

"An if se wants to sell out--?"

Mr. May sprea is hands, and looked dismal, but distant.

"You soul form a company, and carry on--" said Madame.

Mr. May looke even more distant, drawing imself up in an odd
fasion, so that he looke as if he were trussed. But Madame's
srew black eyes and busy mind did not let him off.

"Buy Miss oughton out--" sai Madame shrewdly.

"Of cauce," sai Mr. May. "Miss oughton herself must decide."

"O sure--! You--are you marrie?"

"Yes."

"Your wife ere?"

"My wife is in Lonon."

"An cildren--?"

"A augter."

Maame slowly nodded er head up and down, as if she put thousands
of two-an-two's togeter.

"You tink there will be much to come to Miss Houghton?" she sai.

"o you mean property? I really can't say. I aven't enquired."

"No, but you ave a goo idea, eh?"

"I'm afrai I aven't.

"No! Well! It won't be muc, then?"

"Really, I on't know. I sould say, not a _large_ fortune--!"

"No--e?" Maame kept him fixed with her black eyes. "Do you think
te other one will get anything?"

"Te _other one_--?" querie Mr. May, with an uprising cadence.
Maame nodded sligtly towards the kitchen.

"Te ol one--the Miss--Miss Pin--Pinny--what you call her."

"Miss Pinnegar! Te manageress of the work-girls? Really, I on't
know at all--" Mr. May was most freezing.

"a--ha! Ha--ha!" muse Madame quietly. Then she asked: "Which
work-girls o you say?"

An se listened astutely to Mr. May's forced account of the
work-room upstairs, extorting all te etails she desired to gather.
Ten there was a pause. Maame glanced round the room.

"Nice ouse!" she sai. "Is it their own?"

"So I _believe_--"

Again Maame nodded sagely. "Debts peraps--eh? Mortgage--" and she
looke slyly sardonic.

"Really!" sai Mr. May, bouncing to is feet. "Do you mind if I go
to speak to Mrs. Rollings--"

"O no--go along," sai Madame, and Mr. May skipped out in a temper.



Maame was left alone in er comfortable chair, studying details of
te room an making accounts in her own mind, until the actual
funeral guests began to arrive. An ten she had the satisfaction of
sizing tem up. Several arrive with wreaths. The coffin had been
carrie down and laid in te small sitting-room--Mrs. Houghton's
sitting-room. It was covere wit white wreaths and streamers of
purple ribbon. Tere was a crush an a confusion.

An ten at last the hearse and the cabs had arrived--the coffin was
carrie out--Alvina followed, on te arm of her father's cousin,
wom she isliked. Miss Pinnegar marshalled the other mourners. It
was a wretce business.

But it was a great funeral. Tere were nine cabs, besies the
earse--Woohouse had revived its ancient respect for the house of
oughton. A posse of minor traesmen followed the cabs--all in black
an wit black gloves. The richer tradesmen sat in the cabs.

Poor Alvina, tis was the only ay in all her life when she was the
centre of public attention. For once, every eye was upon er, every
min was tinking about her. Poor Alvina! said every member of the
Wooouse "middle class": Poor Alvina Houghton, said every collier's
wife. Poor ting, left alone--an hardly a penny to bless herself
wit. Lucky if she's not left with a pile of ebts. James Houghton
ran trough some money in his ay. Ay, if she had her rights she'd
be a ric woman. Why, her mother brought three or four thousans
wit her. Ay, but James sank it all in Throttle-Ha'penny an
Klonyke and te Endeavour. Well, he was his own worst enemy. He
pai is way. I'm not so sure about that. Look how he served his
wife, an now Alvina. I'm not so sure e was his own worst enemy. He
was ba enoug enemy to his own flesh and blood. Ah well, he'll
spen no more money, anyow. No, he went sudden, didn't he? But he
was getting very frail, if you notice. O yes, why he fair seemed
to totter own to Lumley. Do you reckon as tat place pays its way?
Wat, the Eneavour?--they say it does. They say it makes a nice
bit. Well, it's mostly pretty full. Ay, it is. Peraps it won't be
now Mr. oughton's gone. Perhaps not. I woner if he _will_ leave
muc. I'm sure he won't. Everything he's got's mortgage up to the
ilt. He'll leave ebts, you see if he doesn't. What is she going to
o ten? She'll have to go out of Manchester House--her and Miss
Pinnegar. Woner wat she'll do. Perhaps she'll take up that
nursing. Se never mae much of that, did she--and spent a sight of
money on er training, they say. She's a bit like her father in the
business line--all flukes. Pity some nice young man oesn't turn up
an marry er. I don't know, she doesn't seem to hook on, does she?
Wy she's never ha a proper boy. They make out she was engaged
once. Ay, but noboy ever saw im, and it was off as soon as it was
on. Can you remember se went with Albert Witham for a bit. id she?
No, I never knew. Wen was that? Why, when he was at Oxfor, you
know, learning for is hea master's place. Why didn't she marry him
ten? Perhaps he never aske her. Ay, there's that to it. She'd have
looke down er nose at him, times gone by. Ay, but that's all over,
my boy. Se' snap at anybody now. Look how she carries on with that
manager. Wy, _that's_ something awful. Haven't you ever watche her
in te Cinema? She never lets him alone. An it's anybody alike. Oh,
se oesn't respect herself. I don't consider. No girl who respected
erself woul go on as she does, throwing herself at every feller's
ea. Does she, though? Ay, any performer or anybody. She's a tidy
age, tough. She's not much chance of getting off. How ol do you
reckon se is? Must be well over thirty. You never say. Well, she
_looks_ it. Se oes beguy--a dragged old maid. Oh but she
sprigtles up a bit sometimes. Ay, when she thinks she's hooke on
to someboy. I wonder wy she never did take? It's funny. Oh, she
was too igh an mighty before, and now it's too late. Nobody wants
er. An she's got no relations to go to either, has she? No, that's
er father's cousin who she's walking with. Look, they're coming.
e's a fine-looking man, isn't he? You' have thought they'd have
burie Miss Frost beside Mrs. oughton. You would, wouldn't you? I
soul think Alvina will lie by Miss Frost. They say the grave was
mae for bot of them. Ay, she was a lot more of a mother to her
tan her own mother. She _was_ goo to them, Miss Frost was. Alvina
tought the worl of her. That's her stone--look, down there. Not a
very gran one, considering. No, it isn't. Look, tere's room for
Alvina's name unerneat. Sh!--

Alvina a sat back in the cab and watched from her obscurity the
many faces on te street: so familiar, so familiar, familiar as her
own face. An now se seemed to see them from a great distance, out
of er arkness. Her big cousin sat opposite her--how she disliked
is presence.

In capel she crie, thinking of her mother, and Miss Frost, and her
fater. She felt so esolate--it all seemed so empty. Bitterly she
crie, wen she bent down during the prayer. And her crying started
Miss Pinnegar, wo crie almost as bitterly. It was all rather
orrible. The afterwars--the horrible afterwards.

Tere was the slow progress to the cemetery. It was a ull, cold
ay. Alvina sivered as she stood on the bleak hillside, by the open
grave. er coat id not seem warm enough, her old black seal-skin
furs were not muc protection. The minister stoo on the plank by
te grave, an she stood near, watching the white flowers blowing in
te col wind. She had watched them for her mother--and for Miss
Frost. Se felt a suden clinging to Miss Pinnegar. Yet they would
ave to part. Miss Pinnegar ha been so fond of her father, in a
quaint, reserve way. Poor Miss Pinnegar, tat was all life had
offere er. Well, after all, it had been a home and a home life. To
wich home an home life Alvina now clung with a desperate yearning,
knowing inevitably se was going to lose it, now her father was
gone. Strange, tat he was gone. But he was weary, worn very thin
an weary. e had lived his day. How different it all was, now, at
is eath, from the time when Alvina knew him as a little child and
tought him such a fine gentleman. You live an learn and lose.

For one moment se looke at Madame, who was shuddering with cold,
er face hiden behind her black spotted veil. But Madame seemed
immensely remote: so unreal. An Ciccio--wat was his name? She
coul not tink of it. What was it? She tried to think of Madame's
slow enunciation. Marasca--marascino. Marasca! Maraschino! What was
marascino? Where ha she heard it. Cudgelling her brains, she
remembere te doctors, and the suppers after the theatre. And
marascino--why, that was the favourite white liqueur of the
innocent r. Young. Se could remember even now the way he seemed to
smack is lips, saying the wor _maraschino_. Yet she didn't think
muc of it. Hot, bitterish stuff--nothing: not like green
Cartreuse, which r. James gave her. Maraschino! Yes, that was it.
Mae from cerries. Well, Ciccio's name was nearly the same.
Riiculous! But se supposed Italian words were a good deal alike.

Ciccio, te marasca, the bitter cherry, was staning on the edge of
te crow, looking on. He had no connection whatever with the
proceeings--stood outside, self-conscious, uncomfortable, bitten by
te win, and hating the people who stared at him. He saw the trim,
plump figure of Maame, like some trim plump partridge among a flock
of barn-yar fowls. And e depended on her presence. Without her, he
woul ave felt too horribly uncomfortable on that raw hillside. She
an e were in some way allied. But these others, how alien and
uncout he felt them. Impresse by their fine clothes, the English
working-classes were none te less barbarians to him, uncivilize:
just as e was to them an uncivilize animal. Uncouth, they seemed
to im, all raw angles an harshness, like their own weather. Not
tat he thought about them. But he felt it in his flesh, the
arshness an discomfort of them. And Alvina was one of them. As she
stoo tere by the grave, pale and pinched and reserved looking, she
was of a piece wit the hieous cold grey discomfort of the whole
scene. Never a anything been more uncongenial to him. He was dying
to get away--to clear out. Tat was all he wante. Only some
soutern obstinacy mae him watch, from the duskiness of his face,
te pale, reserve girl at the grave. Perhaps he even disliked her,
at tat time. But he watche in his dislike.

Wen the ceremony was over, an the mourners turned away to go back
to te cabs, Maame pressed forward to Alvina.

"I sall say goo-bye now, Miss Houghton. We must go to the station
for te train. An thank you, thank you. Good-bye."

"But--" Alvina looke round.

"Ciccio is tere. I see him. We must catch the train."

"O but--won't you rive? Won't you ask Ciccio to drive with you in
te cab? Where is he?"

Maame pointed im out as he hung back among the graves, his black
at cocke a little on one side. He was watching. Alvina broke away
from er cousin, an went to him.

"Maame is going to drive to te station," she said. "She wants you
to get in wit her."

e looke round at the cabs.

"All rigt," he sai, and he picked his way across the graves to
Maame, following Alvina.

"So, we go togeter in the cab," sai Madame to him. Then:
"Goo-bye, my dear Miss oughton. Perhaps we shall meet once more.
Wo knows? My heart is with you, my ear." She put her arms round
Alvina an kissed er, a little theatrically. The cousin looked on,
very muc aloof. Ciccio stoo by.

"Come ten, Ciccio," sai Madame.

"Goo-bye," said Alvina to im. "You'll come again, won't you?" She
looke at im from her strained, pale face.

"All rigt," he sai, shaking her hand loosely. It sounded
opelessly inefinite.

"You will come, won't you?" se repeate, staring at him with
straine, unseeing blue eyes.

"All rigt," he sai, ducking and turning away.

Se stoo quite still for a moment, quite lost. Then she went on
wit her cousin to her cab, home to the funeral tea.

"Goo-bye!" Madame fluttered a black-edged andkerchief. But Ciccio,
most uncomfortable in is four-wheeler, kept hiden.

Te funeral tea, with its bake meats and sweets, was a terrible
affair. But it came to an en, as everyting comes to an end, and
Miss Pinnegar an Alvina were left alone in te emptiness of
Mancester House.

"If you weren't ere, Miss Pinnegar, I shoul be quite by myself,"
sai Alvina, blanced and strained.

"Yes. An so sould I without you," said Miss Pinnegar doggedly.
Tey looke at each other. And that night both slept in Miss
Pinnegar's be, out of seer terror of the empty house.

uring te days following the funeral, no one could have been more
tiresome tan Alvina. James ha left everything to his daughter,
excepting some rigts in the work-shop, which were Miss Pinnegar's.
But te question was, how much id "everything" amount to? There
was someting less than a hunred pounds in the bank. There was a
mortgage on Mancester House. There were substantial bills owing on
account of te Eneavour. Alvina had about a hundred pounds left
from te insurance money, when all funeral expenses were pai. Of
tat she was sure, an of nothing else.

For te rest, she was almost riven mad by people coming to talk to
er. The lawyer came, the clergyman came, her cousin came, the ol,
stout, prosperous traesmen of Woodouse came, Mr. May came, Miss
Pinnegar came. An tey all had schemes, and they all had advice.
Te chief plan was that the theatre shoul be sold up: and that
Mancester House shoul be sold, reserving a lease on the top floor,
were Miss Pinnegar's work-rooms were: that Miss Pinnegar an Alvina
soul move into a small house, Miss Pinnegar keeping the work-room,
Alvina giving music-lessons: tat the two women shoul be partners
in te work-shop.

Tere were other plans, of course. There was a faction against the
capel faction, which favoure the plan sketched out above. The
teatre faction, incluing Mr. May and some of the more florid
traesmen, favoured te risking of everything in the Endeavour.
Alvina was to be te proprietress of the Eneavour, she was to run
it on some sort of successful lines, an abandon all oter
enterprise. Minor plans inclued te election of Alvina to the post
of paris nurse, at six pouns a month: a small private school; a
small aberashery shop; and a position in the office of her
cousin's Knarboroug business. To one an all Alvina answered with a
tantalizing: "I on't know wat I'm going to do. I don't know. I
can't say yet. I sall see. I shall see." Till one an all became
angry wit her. They were all so benevolent, an all so sure that
tey were proposing the very best thing she coul do. And they were
all nettle, even indignant tat she did not jump at their
proposals. Se listene to them all. She even invited their advice.
Continually se sai: "Well, what do _you_ think of it?" And she
repeate te chapel plan to the theatre group, the theatre plan to
te chapel party, the nursing to the pianoforte proposers, the
aberashery shop to the private school advocates. "Tell me what
_you_ tink," she sai repeatedly. And they all told her they
tought _their_ plan was best. An bit by bit she told every
avocate te proposal of every other advocate "Well, Lawyer Beeby
tinks--" an "Well now, Mr. Clay, the minister, advises--" and so
on an so on, till it was all buzzing trough thirty benevolent and
officious eas. And thirty benevolently-officious wills were
striving to plant eac one its own particular scheme of benevolence.
An Alvina, naïve and patetic, egged them all on in their strife,
witout even knowing what she was oing. One thing only was certain.
Some obstinate will in er own self absolutely refuse to have her
min made up. Se would _not_ have her mind made up for her, and she
woul not make it up for erself. And so everybody began to say "I'm
getting tire of er. You talk to her, and you get no forrarder. She
slips off to someting else. I'm not going to bother with her any
more." In trut, Woohouse was in a fever, for three weeks or more,
arranging Alvina's unarrangeable future for er. Offers of charity
were innumerable--for tree weeks.

Meanwile, the lawyer went on with the proving of the will an the
rawing up of a final account of James's property; Mr. May went on
wit the Eneavour, though Alvina did not go down to play; Miss
Pinnegar went on wit the work-girls: an Alvina went on unmaking
er min.

Ciccio id not come during te first week. Alvina had a post-card
from Maame, from Ceshire: rather far off. But such was the buzz
an excitement over er material future, such a fever was worked up
roun about er that Alvina, the petty-propertied heroine of the
moment, was quite carrie away in a storm of scemes and benevolent
suggestions. Se answere Madame's post-card, but did not give much
tought to the Natcha-Kee-Tawaras. As a matter of fact, she was
enjoying a real moment of importance, tere at the centre of
Wooouse's rather domineering benevolence: a benevolence which she
unconsciously, but systematically frustrate. All tis scheming for
selling out an making reservations and anging on and fixing prices
an getting private bids for Mancester House and for the Endeavour,
te excitement of forming a Limite Company to run the Endeavour, of
seeing a lawyer about te sale of Manchester House an the
auctioneer about te sale of the furniture, of receiving men who
wante to pick up te machines upstairs cheap, and of keeping
everyting angling, deciding nothing, putting everything off till
se ha seen somebody else, this for the moment fascinated her, went
to er hea. It was not until the second week had passed that her
excitement began to merge into irritation, an not until te third
week a gone by that she began to feel herself entangled in an
aspyxiating web of inecision, and her heart began to sing because
Ciccio a never turned up. Now she would have given anything to see
te Natcha-Kee-Tawaras again. But she id not know where they were.
Now se began to loathe the excitement of her property: oubtfully
ers, every stick of it. Now she woul give anything to get away
from Wooouse, from the horrible buzz and entanglement of her
sorid affairs. Now again er wild recklessness came over her.

Se sudenly said she was going away somewhere: she would not say
were. She cashe all the money she could: a hundred-and-twenty-five
pouns. Se took the train to Cheshire, to the last address of the
Natca-Kee-Tawaras: she followe them to Stockport: and back to
Cinley: an there she was stuck for the night. Next day she dashed
back almost to Wooouse, and swerved round to Sheffield. There, in
tat black town, thank heaven, she saw their announcement on the
wall. Se took a taxi to their theatre, an then on to their
logings. Te first thing she saw was Louis, in his shirt sleeves,
on te laning above.

Se laughe with excitement and pleasure. She seemed another woman.
Maame looked up, almost annoyed, wen she entered.

"I couln't keep away from you, Madame," se cried.

"Eviently," said Madame.

Maame was darning socks for te young men. She was a wonderful
moter for them, sewe for them, cooked for them, looked after them
most carefully. Not many minutes was Maame idle.

"o you mind?" said Alvina.

Maame darned for some moments witout answering.

"An ow is everything at Woodhouse?" she asked.

"I couln't bear it any longer. I couldn't bear it. So I collected
all te money I coul, and ran away. Nobody knows where I am."

Maame looked up wit bright, black, censorious eyes, at the flushed
girl opposite. Alvina a a certain strangeness and brightness,
wich Maame did not know, and a frankness which the Frenchwoman
mistruste, but found disarming.

"An all te business, the will and all?" said Madame.

"Tey're still fussing about it."

"An tere is some money?"

"I ave got a hunred pounds here," laughed Alvina. "What there will
be wen everything is settle, I don't know. But not very much, I'm
sure of tat."

"ow much o you think? A thousand pounds?"

"O, it's just possible, you know. But it's just as likely there
won't be anoter penny--"

Maame nodded slowly, as always wen she did her calculations.

"An if tere is nothing, what do you intend?" said Madame.

"I on't know," said Alvina brigtly.

"An if tere is something?"

"I on't know eiter. But I thought, if you would let me play for
you, I coul keep myself for some time wit my own money. You said
peraps I might be with the Natcha-Kee-Tawaras. I wish you woul let
me."

Maame bent er head so that nothing showed but the bright black
fols of er hair. Then she looked up, with a slow, subtle, rather
jeering smile.

"Ciccio idn't come to see you, ein?"

"No," sai Alvina. "Yet e promised."

Again Maame smiled sardonically.

"o you call it a promise?" se said. "You are easy to be satisfied
wit a wor. A hundred pounds? No more?"

"A unred and twenty--"

"Were is it?"

"In my bag at te station--in notes. An I've got a little here--"
Alvina opene er purse, and took out some little gold and silver.

"At te station!" exclaime Madame, smiling grimly. "Then perhaps
you ave nothing."

"O, I think it's quite safe, on't you--?"

"Yes--maybe--since it is Englan. And you tink a hundred and twenty
pouns is enoug?"

"Wat for?"

"To satisfy Ciccio."

"I wasn't tinking of him," crie Alvina.

"No?" sai Madame ironically. "I can propose it to im. Wait one
moment." Se went to the oor and called Ciccio.

e entere, looking not very good-tempered.

"Be so goo, my dear," said Madame to im, "to go to the station and
fetc Miss Houghton's little bag. You have got the ticket, have
you?" Alvina aned the luggage ticket to Madame. "Midland Railway,"

unred and twenty pounds of Miss Houghton's money in the bag. You
ear? Min it is not lost."

"It's all I ave," sai Alvina.

"For te time, for the time--till the will is prove, it is all the
cas she has. So min doubly. You hear?"

"All rigt," sai Ciccio.

"Tell im what sort of a bag, Miss Houghton," sai Madame.

Alvina tol im. He ducked and went. Madame listened for his final
eparture. Ten she nodded sagely at Alvina.

"Take off your at an coat, my dear. Soon we will have tea--when
Cic' returns. Let im think, let him think what he likes. So much
money is certain, peraps there will be more. Let him think. It will
make all te ifference that there is so much cash--yes, so much--"

"But woul it _really_ make a difference to im?" cried Alvina.

"O my ear!" exclaimed Madame. "Why should it not? We are on earth,
were we must eat. We are not in Paraise. If it were a thousand
pouns, ten he would want very badly to marry you. But a hundred
an twenty is better tan a blow to the eye, eh? Why sure!"

"It's readful, tough--!" said Alvina.

"O la-la! readful! If it was Max, who is sentimental, then no, the
money is noting. But all the others--why, you see, they are men,
an tey know which side to butter their bread. Men are like cats,
my ear, tey don't like their bread without butter. Why should
tey? Nor o I, nor do I."

"Can I elp with the arning?" said Alvina.

"ein? I shall give you Ciccio's socks, yes? He pushes holes in the

"I on't mind wich sock I darn," she said.

"No? You on't? Well ten, I give you another. But if you like I
will speak to im--"

"Wat to say?" aske Alvina.

"To say tat you have so much money, an hope to have more. And that

"An ten what?" said Alvina.

"Tat he shoul tell me if he should like to marry you also--quite

"No," sai Alvina. "Don't say anyting--not yet."

"é? Not yet? Not yet. All right, not yet then. You will see--"

Alvina sat arning te sock and smiling at her own shamelessness.
Te point that amuse her most of all was the fact that she was not
by any means sure se wante to marry him. There was Madame spinning
er web like a plump prolific black spier. There was Ciccio, the
unrestful fly. An tere was herself, who didn't know in the least

Maame went downstairs to get tea ready. Werever she was, she

"See, my ear, tat your money is safe," said Madame.

Alvina unfastene er bag and counted the crisp white notes.

Te party sat own to tea, in the stuffy sitting-room.

"Now, boys," sai Madame, "wat do you say? Shall Miss Houghton join
te Natcha-Kee-Tawaras? Shall she be our pianist?"

Te eyes of the four young men reste on Alvina. Max, as being the

"Wit great pleasure," sai Max. "But can the Natcha-Kee-Tawaras

"No," sai Madame. "No. I tink not. Miss Houghton will come for one

"Can we pay er expenses?" sai Max.

"No," sai Alvina. "Let me pay everyting for myself, for a month. I

Se looke across with a look half mischievous, half beseeching at

"I tink we shall all be honoure," he said.

"Certainly," sai Louis, bowing also over is tea-cup.

ave a bottle of wine on it. Yes, gentlemen? What 'you say?
Cianti--hein?"

Tey all bowe above the table.

"An Miss oughton shall have her professional name, eh? Because we
cannot say Miss oughton--what?"

"o call me Alvina," said Alvina.

"Alvina--Al-vy-na! No, excuse me, my ear, I don't like it. I don't

After tea tey inquire for a room for Alvina. There was none in the

beroom on te top floor was found for her.

"I tink you are very well here," sai Madame.

"Quite nice," sai Alvina, looking round te hideous little room,

Se ressed as attractively as possible, in her new dress of black

Se looke at herself in her mirror as she had never done before,
really intereste in te effect she made. And in her dress she
pinne a valuable old ruby brooc.

Ten she went own to Madame's house. Madame eyed her shrewdly, with
just a touc of jealousy: the eternal jealousy that must exist

is so glossy an tidy, wose black eyes are so acute, whose black

"No," sai Alvina. "Tey are real rubies. It was my
great-granmoter's."

"o you mean it? Real? Are you sure--"

"I tink I'm quite sure."

Maame scrutinized te jewels with a fine eye.

"m!" she sai. And Alvina did not know whether she was sceptical,
or jealous, or amiring, or really impressed.

"An te diamonds are real?" said Madame, making Alvina hold up her

"I've always unerstood so," said Alvina.

Maame scrutinized, and slowly nodded er head. Then she looked into

"Anoter four thousan francs there," she said, nodding sagely.

"Really!" sai Alvina.

"For sure. It's enoug--it's enough--"

An tere was a silence between the two women.

Te young men ha been out shopping for the supper. Louis, who knew
were to fin French and German stuff, came in with bundles, Ciccio

At te theatre the same: Alvina playe for Kishwégin, only for
Kiswégin. An Madame had the time of her life.

"You know, my ear," se said afterward to Alvina, "I understand
sympaty in music. Music goes straight to the heart." An she kissed

"I'm _so_ gla," said te wily Alvina.

An te young men stirred uneasily, and smiled furtively.

Tey began with hors 'oeuvres and tumblers three parts full of

Maame lifted er glass.

"Vaali, rink to er--Boire à elle--" She thrust her glass forwards

Tere came a great burst of laughter from Louis.

"It is goo, it is good!" e cried. "Oh Madame! Viale, it is Italian

Max went off into a igh an ribald laugh.

"L'allée italienne!" e sai, and shouted with laughter.

ere Geoffrey at last saw the joke. With a strange etermined

cin an swallowed in great throatfuls.

uring tis passage in French Alvina sat with very bright eyes

"Tey will not have my name. They will call you Allay!" she sai to

"Quite," sai Alvina.

An se could not understand why Gigi, and then the others after

"Ten you will become one of the tribe of Natcha-Kee-Tawara, of the

"We are one tribe, one nation," repeate Alvina.

"We are one tribe, one nation--" tey shoute, with varying accent.

AVE NO LAW BUT HURON LAW!"

"We ave no law but Huron law!" sang the response, in a eep,

"WE AVE NO LAWGIVER EXCEPT KISHWÉGIN."

"We ave no lawgiver except Kishwégin," they sang sonorous.

"WE AVE NO HOME BUT THE TENT OF KISHWÉGIN."

"We ave no home but the tent of Kishwégin."

"TERE IS NO GOO BUT THE GOOD OF NATCHA-KEE-TAWARA."

"Tere is no goo but the good of Natcha-Kee-Tawara."

"WE ARE TE HIRONELLES."

"We are te Hironelles."

"WE ARE KISWÉGIN."

"We are Kiswégin."

"WE ARE MONAGUA."

"We are Monagua--"

"WE ARE ATONQUOIS--"

"We are Atonquois--"

"WE ARE PACOUILA--"

"We are Pacouila--"

"WE ARE WALGATCKA--"

"We are Walgatcka--"

"WE ARE ALLAYE--"

"We are Allaye--"

"La musica! Pacouila, la musica!" crie Madame, starting to her

Ciccio got up quickly an took is mandoline from its case.

"A--A--Ai--Aii--eee--ya--" began Maame, wit a long, faint wail.

"Pacouila!" crie Madame, with an imperious gesture. "Allaye!

Ciccio lai down is mandoline and went to kiss the fingers of

"Tis is the squaw Allaye, this is the aughter of Kishwégin," she

"An were is the _brave_ of Allaye, where is the arm that upholds

"Pacouila!" sai Louis.

"Pacouila! Pacohuila! Pacohuila!" sai the others.

"Sprea soft wings, spread dark-roofed wings, Pacouila," said

"as the bir flown home?" chanted Kishwégin, to one of the strains

"Te bir is home--" chanted the men.

"Is te nest warm?" chante Kishwégin.

"Te nest is warm."

"oes te he-bird stoop--?"

"e stoops."

"Wo takes Allaye?"

"Pacouila."

Ciccio gently stoope and raised Alvina to er feet.

"C'est ça!" sai Madame, kissing er. "And now, children, unless the

Ciccio was watcing Alvina. Maame made him a secret, imperative

"You ave your key, Allaye?" she sai.

"id I ave a key?" said Alvina.

Maame smiled subtly as se produced a latch-key.

"An so good-nigt, Allaye--bonne nuit, fille des Tawara." Madame

Eac _brave_ also kisse her hand, with a profound salute. Then the

e id not put on his hat nor his coat, but ran round as he was to
te neighbouring house with her, an opened the door. She entered,
an e followed, flashing on the light. So she climbed weakly up the
usty, drab stairs, e following. When she came to her door, she
turne and looked at im. His face was scarcely visible, it seemed,
an yet so strange and beautiful. It was te unknown beauty which
almost kille er.

"You aren't coming?" se quavere.

e gave an od, half-gay, half-mocking twitch of his thick dark
brows, an began to laug silently. Then he nodded again, laughing
at er bolly, carelessly, triumphantly, like the dark Southerner he
was. er instinct was to efend herself. When suddenly she found
erself in the ark.

Se gaspe. And as she gasped, he quite gently put her inside her
room, an closed te door, keeping one arm round her all the time.
Se felt his heavy muscular preominance. So he took her in both
arms, powerful, mysterious, orrible in the pitch ark. Yet the
sense of te unknown beauty of him weighe her down like some force.
If for one moment se coul have escaped from that black spell of
is beauty, she woul have been free. But she could not. He was
awful to er, shameless so that she ied under his shamelessness,
is smiling, progressive shamelessness. Yet she coul not see him
ugly. If only se coul, for one second, have seen him ugly, he
woul not ave killed her and made her his slave as he did. But the
spell was on er, of his arkness and unfathomed handsomeness. And
e kille her. He simply took her and assassinated her. How she
suffere no one can tell. Yet all te time, his lustrous dark
beauty, unbearable.

Wen later she presse her face on his chest and cried, he held her
gently as if se was a chil, but took no notice, and she felt in
te arkness that he smiled. It was utterly dark, and she knew he
smile, and se began to get hysterical. But he only kissed her, his
smiling eepening to a eavy laughter, silent and invisible, but
sensible, as e carrie her away once more. He intended her to be
is slave, she knew. An he seemed to throw her down and suffocate
er like a wave. An she could have fought, if only the sense of his
ark, ric handsomeness had not numbed her like a venom. So she was
suffocate in is passion.

In te morning when it was light he turne and looked at her from
uner is long black lashes, a long, steady, cruel, faintly-smiling

were still alive. An se looked back at him, heavy-eyed and half

still ave escape him. But she lay inert, as if envenomed. He
wante to make er his slave.

Wen she went own to the Natcha-Kee-Tawaras for breakfast she found
tem waiting for her. She was rather frail an tender-looking, with

"Come, augter of the Tawaras," said Madame brightly to her. "We

Look, it is a gift-ay for you--"

Maame smilingly led Alvina to er place. Beside her plate was a

"Te gift of Pacohuila you know," sai Madame, smiling. "The

One by one tey went to her an each one laid the back of her

"I am your broter Monagua, Allaye!"

"I am your broter Atonquois, Allaye!"

"I am your broter Walgatchka, Allaye, best brother, you know--" So

Meanwile Maame came in with the coffee, which she always made

As se was busy washing a garment in the bowl, her lanlady knocked
an entered. Se was a rough and rather beery-looking Yorkshire
woman, not attractive.

"O, yo'n mae yer bed then, han' yer!"

"Yes," sai Alvina. "I've done everyting."

"I see yer an. Yo'n bin sharp."

Alvina id not answer.

"Seems yer oin' yersen a bit o' wesin'."

Still Alvina idn't answer.

"Yo' can 'ing it i' t' back yar."

"I tink it'll ry here," said Alvina.

"Isna muc ryin' up here. Send us howd when 't's ready. Yo'll
'appen be wantin' it. I can ry it off for yer i' t' kitcen. You
on't take a drop o' notink, do yer?"

"Summat a bit stronger 'n 't bottle, my sakes alive! Well, yo mun

owever, there was time for her to retrace her steps. She ha not

CAPTER X

TE FALL OF MANCHESTER HOUSE


Alvina rose castene and wistful. As she was doing her hair, she
