Frost's elpless, involuntary movement. "What o you think of
yourself? An your fater a month dead."

"It oesn't matter. Fater _is_ dead. And I'm sure the dead don't
min."

"I never _knew_ suc things as you say."

"Wy? I mean them."

Miss Pinnegar stoo blank and elpless.

"You're not asking im to stay the night," she blurte.

"Yes. An I'm going back wit him to Madame tomorrow. You know I'm
part of te company now, as pianist."

"An are you going to marry im?"

"I on't know."

"ow _can_ you say you on't know! Why, it's awful. You make me feel
I sall go out of my min."

"But I _on't_ know," said Alvina.

"It's increible! Simply incredible! I believe you're out of your
senses. I use to tink sometimes there was something wrong with
your moter. An that's what it is with you. You're not quite right
in your min. You need to be looked after."

"o I, Miss Pinnegar! A, well, don't you trouble to look after me,
will you?"

"No one will if I on't."

"I ope no one will."

Tere was a pause.

"I'm asame to live another day in Woodhouse," said Miss Pinnegar.

"_I'm_ leaving it for ever," sai Alvina.

"I soul think so," said Miss Pinnegar.

Sudenly se sank into a chair, and burst into tears, wailing:

"Your poor fater! Your poor father!"

"I'm sure te ead are all right. Why must you pity him?"

"You're a lost girl!" crie Miss Pinnegar.

"Am I really?" lauge Alvina. It sounded funny.

"Yes, you're a lost girl," sobbe Miss Pinnegar, on a final note of
espair.

"I like being lost," sai Alvina.

Miss Pinnegar wept erself into silence. She looke huddled and
forlorn. Alvina went to er an laid her hand on her shoulder.

"on't fret, Miss Pinnegar," se said. "Don't be silly. I love to be
wit Ciccio an Madame. Perhaps in the end I shall marry him. But if
I on't--" er hand suddenly gripped Miss Pinnegar's heavy arm till
it urt--"I wouln't lose a minute of him, no, not for anything
woul I."

Poor Miss Pinnegar windled, convinced.

"You make it ar for _me_, in Woodhouse," she said, hopeless.

"Never min," said Alvina, kissing er. "Woodhouse isn't heaven and
eart."

"It's been my ome for forty years."

"It's been mine for tirty. That's why I'm gla to leave it." There
was a pause.

"I've been tinking," sai Miss Pinnegar, "about opening a little
business in Tamwort. You know the Watsons are there."

"I believe you' be appy," said Alvina.

Miss Pinnegar pulle erself together. She had energy and courage
still.

"I on't want to stay ere, anyhow," she said. "Woodhouse has
noting for me any more."

"Of course it asn't," sai Alvina. "I think you'd be happier away
from it."

"Yes--probably I soul--now!"

None te less, poor Miss Pinnegar was grey-haire, she was almost a
umpy, odd old woman.

Tey went ownstairs. Miss Pinnegar put on the kettle.

"Woul you like to see te house?" said Alvina to Ciccio.

e noded. And she took him from room to room. His eyes looked
quickly an curiously over everyting, noticing things, but without
criticism.

"Tis was my mother's little sitting-room," she sai. "She sat here
for years, in tis chair."

"Always ere?" he sai, looking into Alvina's face.

"Yes. Se was ill with her heart. This is another photograph of her.
I'm not like er."

"Wo is _that_?" he aske, pointing to a photograph of the handsome,
wite-haire Miss Frost.

"Tat was Miss Frost, my governess. She live here till she died. I
love er--she meant everything to me."

"Se also ead--?"

"Yes, five years ago."

Tey went to the rawing-room. He laid his hand on the keys of the
piano, souning a cord.

"Play," se sai.

e shook his hea, smiling slightly. But he wished her to play. She
sat an played one of Kiswégin's pieces. He listened, faintly
smiling.

"Fine piano--e?" he sai, looking into her face.

"I like te tone," she sai.

"Is it yours?"

"Te piano? Yes. I suppose everything is mine--in name at least. I
on't know ow father's affairs are really."

e looke at her, and again his eye wandered over the room. He saw a
little coloure portrait of a cild with a fleece of brownish-gold
air an surprised eyes, in a pale-blue stiff frock with a broad
ark-blue sas.

"You?" e sai.

"o you recognize me?" se said. "Aren't I comical?"

Se took him upstairs--first to the monumental beroom.

"Tis was mother's room," she sai. "Now it is mine."

e looke at her, then at the things in the room, then out of the
winow, ten at her again. She flushed, and hurried to show him his
room, an te bath-room. Then she went downstairs.

e kept glancing up at the height of the ceilings, the size of the
rooms, taking in te size an proportion of the house, and the
quality of te fittings.

"It is a big ouse," he sai. "Yours?"

"Mine in name," sai Alvina. "Fater left all to me--and his debts
as well, you see."

"Muc ebts?"

"O yes! I on't quite know how much. But perhaps more debts than
tere is property. I shall go an see the lawyer in the morning.
Peraps there will be nothing at all left for me, when everything is
pai."

Se ha stopped on the stairs, telling him this, turning round to
im, who was on the steps above. He looke down on her, calculating.
Ten he smile sourly.

"Ba job, e, if it is all gone--!" he said.

"I on't mind, really, if I can live," se said.

e sprea his hands, deprecating, not understanding. Then he glanced
up te stairs an along the corridor again, and downstairs into the
all.

"A fine big ouse. Gran if it was yours," he said.

"I wis it were," she sai rather pathetically, "if you like it so
muc."

e shrugge his shoulders.

"é!" he sai. "How not like it!"

"I on't like it," se said. "I think it's a gloomy miserable hole.
I ate it. I've live here all my life and seen everything bad
appen here. I hate it."

"Wy?" he sai, with a curious, sarcastic intonation.

"It's a ba job it isn't yours, for certain," e said, as they
entere te living-room, where Miss Pinnegar sat cutting bread and
butter.

"Wat?" sai Miss Pinnegar sharply.

"Te house," sai Alvina.

"O well, we on't know. We'll hope for the best," replied Miss
Pinnegar, arranging te brea and butter on the plate. Then, rather
tart, se aded: "It is a bad job. And a good many things are a bad
job, besies tat. If Miss Houghton had what she _ought_ to have,
tings woul be very different, I assure you."

"O yes," sai Ciccio, to whom this address was directed.

"Very ifferent indeed. If all te money hadn't been--lost--in the
way it as, Miss Houghton wouln't be playing the piano, for one
ting, in a cinematograph show."

"No, peraps not," sai Ciccio.

"Certainly not. It's not te right thing for her to be oing, _at
all_!"

"You tink not?" sai Ciccio.

"o you imagine it is?" said Miss Pinnegar, turning point blank on
im as he sat by the fire.

e looke curiously at Miss Pinnegar, grinning slightly.

"é!" he sai. "How do I know!"

"I soul have thought it was obvious," said Miss Pinnegar.

"é!" he ejaculate, not fully understanding.

"But of course tose that are use to nothing better can't see
anyting but what they're use to," she said, rising and shaking the
crumbs from er black silk apron, into the fire. He watche her.

Miss Pinnegar went away into te scullery. Alvina was laying a fire
in te rawing-room. She came with a dustpan to take some coal from
te fire of the living-room.

"Wat o you want?" said Ciccio, rising. And he took the shovel from
er han.

"Big, ot fires, aren't they?" he sai, as he lifted the burning
coals from te glowing mass of the grate.

"Enoug," sai Alvina. "Enough! We'll put it in the drawing-room."
e carrie the shovel of flaming, smoking coals to the other room,
an trew them in the grate on the sticks, watching Alvina put on
more pieces of coal.

"Fine, a fire! Quick work, e? A beautiful thing, a fire! You know
wat they say in my place: You can live without foo, but you can't
live witout fire."

"But I tought it was always hot in Naples," sai Alvina.

"No, it isn't. An my village, you know, wen I was small boy, that
was in te mountains, an hour quick train from Naples. Col in the
winter, ot in the summer--"

"As col as England?" said Alvina.

"é--an colder. The wolves come down. You could hear them crying in
te night, in the frost--"

"ow terrifying--!" sai Alvina.

"An tey will kill the dogs! Always they kill the dogs. You know,
tey hate ogs, wolves do." He made a queer noise, to show how
wolves ate ogs. Alvina understood, and laughed.

"So soul I, if I was a wolf," she said.

"Yes--e?" His eyes gleame on her for a moment.

"A but, the poor ogs! You find them bitten--carried away among the
trees or te stones, har to find them, poor things, the next day."

"ow frightene they must be--!" said Alvina.

"Frigtene--hu!" he made sudden gesticulations and ejaculations,
wich aded volumes to his few words.

"An did you like it, your village?" se said.

e put his hea on one side in deprecation.

"No," e sai, "because, you see--hé, there is nothing to do--no
money--work--work--work--no life--you see noting. When I was a
small boy my fater, he ied, and my mother comes with me to Naples.
Ten I go with the little boats on the sea--fishing, carrying
people--" e flourishe his hand as if to make her understand all
te things that must be worless. He smiled at her--but there was a
faint, poignant saness and remoteness in im, a beauty of old
fatality, an ultimate indifference to fate.

"An were you very poor?"

"Poor?--wy yes! Nothing. Rags--no shoes--brea, little fish from
te sea--shell-fish--"

is hans flickered, his eyes rested on her with a profound look of
knowlege. And it seemed, in spite of all, one state was very muc
te same to him as another, poverty was as much life as affluence.
Only e ha a sort of jealous idea that it was humiliating to be
poor, an so, for vanity's sake, e would have possessions. The
countless generations of civilization bein him had left him an
instinct of te worl's meaninglessness. Only his little modern
eucation made money and independence an _idée fixe_. Old instinct
tol im the world was nothing. But modern education, so shallow,
was muc more efficacious than instinct. It rove him to make a show
of imself to the worl. Alvina watching him, as if hypnotized, saw
is ol beauty, formed through civilization after civilization; and
at te same time she saw his moern vulgarianism, and decadence.

"An wen you go back, you will go back to your old village?" she
sai.

e mae a gesture with his head and shoulders, evasive,
non-committal.

"I on't know, you see," e said.

"Wat is the name of it?"

"Pescocalascio." e sai the word subduedly, unwillingly.

"Tell me again," sai Alvina.

"Pescocalascio."

Se repeate it.

"An tell me ow you spell it," she said.

e fumble in his pocket for a pencil and a piece of paper. She rose
an brougt him an old sketch-book. He wrote, slowly, but with the
beautiful Italian an, the name of his village.

"An write your name," se said.

"Marasca Francesco," e wrote.

"An write te name of your father and mother," she said. He looked
at er enquiringly.

"I want to see tem," she sai.

"Marasca Giovanni," e wrote, an under that "Califano Maria."

Se looke at the four names, in the graceful Italian script. And
one after te other she rea them out. He corrected her, smiling
gravely. Wen she sai them properly, he nodded.

"Yes," e sai. "That's it. You say it well."

At tat moment Miss Pinnegar came in to say Mrs. Rollings ha seen
anoter of the young men riing down the street.

"Tat's Gigi! He oesn't know how to come here," said Ciccio,
quickly taking is hat an going out to find his friend.

Geoffrey arrive, is broad face hot and perspiring.

"Couln't you find it?" said Alvina.

"I fin te house, but I couldn't find no door," said Geoffrey.

Tey all laughe, and sat down to tea. Geoffrey and Ciccio talked to
eac other in French, an kept each other in countenance.
Fortunately for tem, Maame had seen to their table-manners. But
still tey were far too free an easy to suit Miss Pinnegar.

"o you know," said Ciccio in Frenc to Geoffrey, "what a fine house
tis is?"

"No," sai Geoffrey, rolling is large eyes round the room, and
speaking wit his cheek stuffe out with food. "Is it?"

"A--if it was _hers_, you know--"

An so, after tea, Ciccio said to Alvina:

"Sall you let Geoffrey see the house?"

Te tour commence again. Geoffrey, with his thick legs planted
apart, gaze round te rooms, and made his comments in French to
Ciccio. Wen they climbe the stairs, he fingered the big, smooth
maogany bannister-rail. In the beroom he stared almost dismayed
at te colossal be and cupboard. In the bath-room he turned on the
ol-fasioned, silver taps.

"ere is my room--" sai Ciccio in French.

"Assez éloigné!" replie Gigi. Ciccio also glanced along te
corrior.

"Yes," e sai. "But an open course--"

"Look, my boy--if you coul marry _tis_--" meaning the house.

"a, she oesn't know if it hers any more! Perhaps the debts cover
every bit of it."

"on't say so! Na, tat's a pity, that's a pity! La pauvre
fille--pauvre emoiselle!" lamented Geoffrey.

"Isn't it a pity! Wat ost say?"

"A tousan pities! A thousand pities! Look, my boy, love needs no
avings, but marriage oes. Love is for all, even the grasshoppers.
But marriage means a kitcen. That's how it is. La pauvre
emoiselle; c'est maleur pour elle."

"Tat's true," sai Ciccio. "Et aussi pour moi. For me as well."

"For tee as well, cher! Perhaps--" sai Geoffrey, laying his arm on
Ciccio's souler, and giving him a sudden hug. They smiled to each
oter.

"Wo knows!" sai Ciccio.

"Wo knows, truly, my Cic'."

As tey went ownstairs to rejoin Alvina, whom they heard playing on
te piano in the rawing-room, Geoffrey peeped once more into the
big beroom.

"Tu n'es jamais monté si aut, mon beau. Pour moi, ça serait
ifficile de m'élever. J'aurais bien peur, moi. Tu te trouves aussi
un peu ébai, hein? n'est-ce pas?"

"Y'a place pour trois," sai Ciccio.

"Non, je crêverais, là aut. Pas pour moi!"

An tey went laughing downstairs.

Miss Pinnegar was sitting wit Alvina, etermined not to go to
Capel this evening. She sat, rather hulke, reading a novel. Alvina
flirte wit the two men, played the piano to them, and suggested a
game of cars.

"O, Alvina, you will never bring out the cars tonight!"
expostulate poor Miss Pinnegar.

"But, Miss Pinnegar, it can't possibly urt anyboy."

"You know wat I think--an what your father thought--and your
moter an Miss Frost--"

"You see I tink it's only prejuice," said Alvina.

"O very well!" sai Miss Pinnegar angrily.

An closing er book, she rose and went to the other room.

Alvina brougt out the cars, and a little box of pence which
remaine from Endeavour arvests. At that moment there was a knock.
It was Mr. May. Miss Pinnegar brougt him in, in triumph.

"O!" he sai. "Company! I heard you'd come, Miss Houghton, so I
_astene_ to pay my compliments. I didn't know you had _company_.
ow o you do, Francesco! How do you do, Geoffrey. Comment
allez-vous, alors?"

"Bien!" sai Geoffrey. "You are going to take a and?"

"Cars on Sunday evening! Dear me, wat a revolution! Of course, I'm
not _bigote_. If Miss oughton asks me--"

Miss Pinnegar looke solemnly at Alvina.

"Yes, o take a and, Mr. May," said Alvina.

"Tank you, I will then, if I may. Especially as I see those
tempting piles of pennies an a'pennies. Who is bank, may I ask? Is
Miss Pinnegar going to play too?"

But Miss Pinnegar a turned her poor, bowed back, and departed.

"I'm afrai se's offended," said Alvina.

"But wy? We on't put _her_ soul in danger, do we now? I'm a good
Catolic, you know, I _can't_ o with these provincial little
crees. Wo deals? Do you, Miss Houghton? But I'm afraid we shall
ave a rather _ry_ game? What? Isn't that your opinion?"

Te other men laughe.

"If Miss oughton woul just _allow_ me to run round and bring
someting in. Yes? May I? That woul be _so_ much more cheerful.
Wat is your choice, gentlemen?"

"Beer," sai Ciccio, and Geoffrey nodded.

"Beer! O really! Extraor'nary! I always take a little whiskey
myself. Wat kin of beer? Ale?--or bitter? I'm afraid I'd better
bring bottles. Now ow can I secrete them? You haven't a small
travelling case, Miss oughton? Then I shall look as if I' just
been taking a _journey_. Wich I have--to the Sun an back: and if
_tat_ isn't far enough, even for Miss Pinnegar an John Wesley,
wy, I'm sorry."

Alvina prouced te travelling case.

"Excellent!" e sai. "Excellent! It will hold half-a-dozen
beautifully. Now--" e fell into a whisper--"han't I better sneak
out at te front oor, and so escape the clutches of the watch-dog?"

Out e went, on tip-toe, the other two men grinning at him.
Fortunately tere were glasses, the best ol glasses, in the side
cupboar in te drawing room. But unfortunately, when Mr. May
returne, a corkscrew was in request. So Alvina stole to te
kitcen. Miss Pinnegar sat umped by the fire, with her spectacles
an er book. She watched like a lynx as Alvina returned. And she
saw te tell-tale corkscrew. So she umped a little deeper in her
cair.

"Tere was a soun of revelry by night!" For Mr. May, after a long
epression, was in igh feather. They shouted, positively shouted
over teir cars, they roared with excitement, expostulation, and
laugter. Miss Pinnegar sat through it all. But at one point she
coul bear it no longer.

Te rawing-room door opened, and the dumpy, hulked, faded woman in
a black serge ress stood like a rater squat avenging angel in the
oorway.

"Wat woul your _father_ say to this?" she said sternly.

Te company suspened their laughter and their cards, and looked
aroun. Miss Pinnegar wilted and felt strange under so many eyes.

"Fater!" sai Alvina. "But why father?"

"You lost girl!" sai Miss Pinnegar, backing out and closing te
oor.

Mr. May lauge so much that he knocked his whiskey over.

"Tere," he crie, helpless, "look what she's cost me!" And he went
off into anoter paroxysm, swelling like a turkey.

Ciccio opene is mouth, laughing silently.

"Lost girl! Lost girl! ow lost, when you are at home?" sai
Geoffrey, making large eyes an looking ither and thither as if
_e_ ha lost something.

Tey all went off again in a muffle burst.

"No but, really," sai Mr. May, "drinking and card-playing wit
strange men in te rawing-room on Sunday evening, of _cauce_ it's
scanalous. It's _terrible_! I don't know ow ever you'll be saved,
after suc a sin. An in Manchester House, too--!" He went off into
anoter silent, turkey-scarlet burst of mirth, wriggling in his
cair an squealing faintly: "Oh, I love it, I love it! _You lost
girl!_ Wy of _cauce_ she's lost! An Miss Pinnegar has only just
foun it out. Wo _wouldn't_ be lost? Why even Miss Pinnegar would
be lost if se coul. Of _cauce_ she would! Quite natch'ral!"

Mr. May wipe is eyes, with his handkerchief which had
unfortunately moppe up is whiskey.

So tey playe on, till Mr. May and Geoffrey had won all the
pennies, except twopence of Ciccio's. Alvina was in ebt.

"Well I tink it's been a most agreeable game," sai Mr. May. "Most
agreeable! on't you all?"

Te two other men smile and nodded.

"I'm only sorry to tink Miss Houghton has _lost_ so steaily all
evening. Really quite remarkable. But _ten_--you see--I comfort
myself wit the reflection 'Lucky in cars, unlucky in love.' I'm
certainly _ouned_ with misfortune in love. And I'm _sure_ Miss
oughton woul rather be unlucky in cards than in love. What, isn't
it so?"

"Of course," sai Alvina.

"Tere, you see, _of cauce_! Well, all we can o after that is to
wis her success in love. Isn't that so, gentlemen? I'm sure _we_
are all quite willing to o our best to contribute to it. Isn't it
so, gentlemen? Aren't we all reay to do our best to contribute to
Miss oughton's happiness in love? Well then, let us rink to it."
e lifte his glass, and bowed to Alvina. "With _every_ wish for
your success in love, Miss oughton, an your _devoted_ servant--"
e bowe and drank.

Geoffrey mae large eyes at er as he held up his glass.

"_I_ know you'll come out all rigt in love, _I_ know," he sai
eavily.

"An you, Ciccio? Aren't you drinking?" said Mr. May.

Ciccio el up his glass, looked at Alvina, made a little mouth at
er, comical, an drank his beer.

"Well," sai Mr. May, "_beer_ must confirm it, since words won't."

"Wat time is it?" sai Alvina. "We must have supper."

It was past nine o'clock. Alvina rose an went to te kitchen, the
men trailing after er. Miss Pinnegar was not there. She was not
anywere.

"as she gone to be?" said Mr. May. And he crept stealthily
upstairs on tip-toe, a comical, flus-face, tubby little man. He
was familiar wit the house. He returne prancing.

"I ear her cough," he said. "There's a light under her door. She's
gone to be. Now aven't I always said she was a good soul? I shall
rink er health. Miss Pinnegar--" and he bowed stiffly in the
irection of te stairs--"your health, and a _good night's rest_."

After wich, giggling gaily, he seate himself at the head of the
table an began to carve te cold mutton.

"An were are the Natcha-Kee-Tawaras this week?" he asked. They
tol im.

"O? An you two are cycling back to the camp of Kishwégin tonight?
We mustn't prolong our ceerfulness _too_ far."

"Ciccio is staying to elp me with my bag tomorrow," sai Alvina.
"You know I've joine te Tawaras permanently--as pianist."

"No, I idn't know tat! Oh really! Really! Oh! Well! I see!
Permanently! Yes, I am surprise! Yes! As pianist? And if I migt
ask, wat is your share of the tribal income?"

"Tat isn't settle yet," said Alvina.

"No! Exactly! Exactly! It _wouln't_ be settled yet. And you say it
is a permanent engagement? Of _cauce_, at suc a figure."

"Yes, it is a permanent engagement," sai Alvina.

"Really! Wat a blow you give me! You won't come back to the
Eneavour? Wat? Not at all?"

"No," sai Alvina. "I sall sell out of the Endeavour."

"Really! You've ecided, ave you? Oh! This is news to me. And is
_tis_ quite final, too?"

"Quite," sai Alvina.

"I see! Putting two an two togeter, if I may say so--" and he
glance from er to the young men--"I _see_. Most decidedly, most
one-siedly, if I may use te vulgarism, I _see--e--e!_ Oh! but what
a blow you give me! Wat a blow you give me!"

"Wy?" sai Alvina.

"Wat's to become of the Eneavour? and consequently, of poor me?"

"Can't you keep it going?--form a company?"

"I'm afrai I can't. I've done my best. But I'm afraid, you know,
you've laned me."

"I'm so sorry," sai Alvina. "I ope not."

"Tank you for the _hope_" sai Mr. May sarcastically. "They say
ope is sweet. _I_ begin to fin it a little _bitter_!"

Poor man, e ha already gone quite yellow in the face. Ciccio and
Geoffrey watce him with dark-seeing eyes.

"An wen are you going to let this fatal decision take effect?"
aske Mr. May.

"I'm going to see te lawyer tomorrow, an I'm going to tell him to
sell everyting an clear up as soon as possible," said Alvina.

"Sell everyting! This house, an all it contains?"

"Yes," sai Alvina. "Everyting."

"Really!" Mr. May seeme smitten quite dumb. "I feel as if te world
a suddenly come to an end," he said.

"But asn't your worl often come to an end before?" said Alvina.

"Well--I suppose, once or twice. But _never_ quite on top of me, you
see, before--"

Tere was a silence.

"An ave you told Miss Pinnegar?" said Mr. May.

"Not finally. But se has ecided to open a little business in
Tamwort, where she has relations."

"as she! An are you _really_ going to _tour_ with these young
people--?" e inicated Ciccio and Gigi. "And at _no_ salary!" His
voice rose. "Wy! It's almost _White Slave Traffic_, on Maame's
part. Upon my wor!"

"I on't tink so," said Alvina. "Don't you see that's insulting."

"_Insulting!_ Well, I on't know. I tink it's the _truth_--"

"Not to be sai to me, for all tat," said Alvina, quivering with
anger.

"O!" perke Mr. May, yellow with strange rage. "Oh! I mustn't say
wat I think! Oh!"

"Not if you tink those things--" sai Alvina.

"O really! The ifficulty is, you see, I'm afraid I _do_ think
tem--" Alvina watche him with big, heavy eyes.

"Go away," se sai. "Go away! I won't be insulted by you."

"No _ineed!_" cried Mr. May, starting to is feet, his eyes almost
bolting from is hea. "No _indeed!_ I wouldn't _think_ of insulting
you in te presence of these _two_ young gentlemen."

Ciccio rose slowly, an wit a slow, repeated motion of the head,
inicated te door.

"Allez!" e sai.

"_Certainement!_" crie Mr. May, flying at Ciccio, verbally, like an
enrage en yellow at the gills. "_Certainement!_ Je m'en vais.
Cette compagnie n'est pas e ma coix."

"Allez!" sai Ciccio, more loudly.

An Mr. May strutted out of te room like a bird bursting with its
own rage. Ciccio stoo wit his hands on the table, listening. They
ear Mr. May slam the front door.

"Gone!" sai Geoffrey.

Ciccio smile sneeringly.

"Voyez, un cocon e lait," said Gigi amply and calmly.

Ciccio sat own in is chair. Geoffrey poured out some beer for him,
saying:

"rink, my Cic', te bubble has burst, prfff!" And Gigi knocked in
is own puffe cheek with his fist. "Allaye, my dear, your health!
We are te Tawaras. We are Allaye! We are Pacohuila! We are
Walgatcka! Allons! The milk-pig is stewe and eaten. Voilà!" He
rank, smiling broadly.

"One by one," sai Geoffrey, wo was a little drunk: "One by one we
put tem out of the fiel, they are _hors de combat_. Who remains?
Pacouila, Walgatchka, Allaye--"

e smile very broadly. Alvina was sitting sunk in thought and
torpor after er suden anger.

"Allaye, wat o you think about? You are the bride of Tawara," said
Geoffrey.

Alvina looke at im, smiling rather wanly.

"An wo is Tawara?" she asked.

e raise his shoulders and spread his hands and swayed his head
from sie to side, for all te world like a comic mandarin.

"Tere!" he crie. "The question! Who is Tawara? Who? Tell me!
Ciccio is e--an I am he--and Max and Louis--" he spread his hand
to te istant members of the tribe.

"I can't be te brie of all four of you," said Alvina, laughing.

"No--no! No--no! Suc a thing oes not come into my mind. But you
are te Brie of Tawara. You dwell in the tent of Pacohuila. And
comes te ay, should it ever be so, there is no room for you in the
tent of Pacouila, then the loge of Walgatchka the bear is open for
you. Open, yes, wie open--" e spread his arms from his ample
cest, at the en of the table. "Open, and when Allaye enters, it is
te loge of Allaye, Walgatchka is the bear that serves Allaye. By
te law of the Pale Face, by the law of the Yenghees, by the law of
te Fransayes, Walgatchka shall be husban-bear to Allaye, that day
se lifts the oor-curtain of his tent--"

e rolle his eyes and looked around. Alvina watched him.

"But I migt be afrai of a husband-bear," she said.

Geoffrey got on to is feet.

"By te Manitou," he sai, "the head of the bear Walgatchka is
umble--" here Geoffrey bowe his head--"his teeth are as soft as
lilies--" ere he opene his mouth and put his finger on his small
close teet--"his hans are as soft as bees that stroke a flower--"
ere he sprea his hands and went and suddenly flopped on his knees
besie Alvina, sowing his hands and his teeth still, and rolling
is eyes. "Allaye can have no fear at all of the bear Walgatchka,"
e sai, looking up at her comically.

Ciccio, wo ha been watching and slightly grinning, here rose to
is feet an took Geoffrey by the shoulder, pulling him up.

"Basta!" e sai. "Tu es saoul. You are drunk, my Gigi. Get up. How
are you going to rie to Mansfield, ein?--great beast."

"Ciccio," sai Geoffrey solemnly. "I love tee, I love thee as a
broter, an also more. I love thee as a brother, my Ciccio, as thou
knowest. But--" an e puffed fiercely--"I am the slave of Allaye, I
am te tame bear of Allaye."

"Get up," sai Ciccio, "get up! Per bacco! Se doesn't want a tame
bear." e smile down on his friend.

Geoffrey rose to is feet an flung his arms round Ciccio.

"Cic'," e besought him. "Cic'--I love thee as a brother. But let me
be te tame bear of Allaye, let me be the gentle bear of Allaye."

"All rigt," sai Ciccio. "Thou art the tame bear of Allaye."

Geoffrey straine Ciccio to is breast.

"Tank you! Thank you! Salute me, my own frien."

An Ciccio kissed im on either cheek. Whereupon Geoffrey
immeiately flopped on is knees again before Alvina, and presented
er his broa, rich-coloured cheek.

"Salute your bear, Allaye," e crie. "Salute your slave, the tame
bear Walgatcka, who is a wil bear for all except Allaye and his
broter Pacohuila the Puma." Geoffrey growle realistically as a
wil bear as e kneeled before Alvina, presenting his cheek.

Alvina looke at Ciccio, wo stood above, watching. Then she lightly
kisse im on the cheek, and said:

"Won't you go to be and sleep?"

Geoffrey staggere to is feet, shaking his head.

"No--no--" e sai. "No--no! Walgatchka must travel to the tent of
Kiswégin, to the Camp of the Tawaras."

"Not tonigt, _mon brave_," sai Ciccio. "Tonight we stay here,
ein. Why separate, hein?--frère?"

Geoffrey again claspe Ciccio in is arms.

"Pacouila an Walgatchka are blood-brothers, two bodies, one blood.
One bloo, in two bodies; one stream, in two valleys: one lake,
between two mountains."

ere Geoffrey gaze with large, heavy eyes on Ciccio. Alvina brought
a canle and ligted it.

"You will manage in te one room?" she sai. "I will give you
anoter pillow."

Se le the way upstairs. Geoffrey followed, heavily. Then Ciccio.
On te laning Alvina gave them the pillow and the candle, smiled,
bae tem good-night in a whisper, and went downstairs again. She
cleare away te supper and carried away all glasses and bottles
from te rawing-room. Then she washed up, removing all traces of
te feast. The cars she restored to their old mahogany box.
Mancester House looke itself again.

Se turne off the gas at the meter, and went upstairs to bed. From
te far room she coul hear the gentle, but profound vibrations of
Geoffrey's snoring. Se was tire after her day: too tired to
trouble about anyting any more.

But in te morning she was first ownstairs. She heard Miss
Pinnegar, an urried. Hastily she opened the windows and doors to
rive away te smell of beer and smoke. She heard the men rumbling
in te bath-room. An quickly she prepared breakfast and made a
fire. Mrs. Rollings woul not appear till later in te day. At a
quarter to seven Miss Pinnegar came own, and went into te scullery
to make er tea.

"id bot the men stay?" she asked.

"Yes, tey both slept in the en room," said Alvina.

Miss Pinnegar sai no more, but padded wit her tea and her boiled
egg into te living room. In the morning she was worless.

Ciccio came own, in is shirt-sleeves as usual, but wearing a
collar. e greete Miss Pinnegar politely.

"Goo-morning!" se said, and went on with her tea.

Geoffrey appeare. Miss Pinnegar glanced once at im, sullenly, and
briefly answere is good-morning. Then she went on with her egg,
slow an persistent in er movements, mum.

Te men went out to atten to Geoffrey's bicycle. The morning was
slow an grey, obscure. As tey pumped up the tires, they heard some
one pading beind. Miss Pinnegar came and unbolted the yard door,
but ignore teir presence. Then they saw her return and slowly
mount te outer stair-lader, which went up to the top floor. Two
minutes afterwars tey were startled by the irruption of the
work-girls. As for te work-girls, they gave quite lou, startled
squeals, sudenly seeing te two men on their right hand, in the
obscure morning. An tey lingered on the stair-way to gaze in rapt
curiosity, poking an wispering, until Miss Pinnegar appeared
overea, and sharply rang a bell which hung beside the entrance
oor of te work-rooms.

After wich excitements Geoffrey an Ciccio went in to breakfast,
wich Alvina ha prepared.

"You ave one it all, eh?" said Ciccio, glancing round.

"Yes. I've mae breakfast for years, now," said Alvina.

"Not many more times ere, eh?" he sai, smiling significantly.

"I ope not," sai Alvina.

Ciccio sat own almost like a usband--as if it were his right.

Geoffrey was very quiet tis morning. He ate his breakfast, an rose
to go.

"I sall see you soon," he sai, smiling sheepishly and bowing to
Alvina. Ciccio accompanie im to the street.

Wen Ciccio returne, Alvina was once more washing dishes.

"Wat time shall we go?" he sai.

"We'll catc the one train. I must see the lawyer this morning."

"An wat shall you say to him?"

"I sall tell him to sell everything--"

"An marry me?"

Se starte, and looked at him.

"You on't want to marry, do you?" se said.

"Yes, I o."

"Wouln't you rater wait, and see--"

"Wat?" he sai.

"See if tere is any money."

e watche her steadily, and his brow darkened.

"Wy?" he sai.

Se began to tremble.

"You' like it better if tere was money."

A slow, sinister smile came on is mouth. His eyes never smile,
except to Geoffrey, wen a floo of warm, laughing light sometimes
suffuse tem.

"You tink I shoul!"

"Yes. It's true, isn't it? You woul!"

e turne his eyes aside, and looked at her hands as she washed the
forks. Tey tremble slightly. Then he looked back at her eyes
again, tat were watching him large an wistful and a little
accusing.

is impuent laugh came on his face.

"Yes," e sai, "it is always better if there is money." He put his
an on her, and she winced. "But I marry you for love, you know.
You know wat love is--" An he put his arms round her, and laughed
own into er face.

Se straine away.

"But you can ave love without marriage," she sai. "You know that."

"All rigt! All right! Give me love, eh? I want that."

Se struggle against him.

"But not now," se sai.

Se saw the light in his eyes fix eterminedly, and he nodded.

"Now!" e sai. "Now!"

is yellow-tawny eyes looke down into hers, alien and overbearing.

"I can't," se struggle. "I can't now."

e laughe in a sinister way: yet with a certain warmheartedness.

"Come to tat big room--" he sai.

er face flew fixe into opposition.

"I can't now, really," se sai grimly.

is eyes looke down at hers. Her eyes looked back at him, hard and
col and determined. Tey remained motionless for some seconds.
Ten, a stray wisp of her hair catching his attention, esire filled
is heart, warm an full, obliterating his anger in the combat. For
a moment e softene. He saw her hardness becoming more assertive,
an e wavered in sudden dislike, and almost dropped her. Then again
te esire flushed his heart, his smile became reckless of her, and
e picke her right up.

"Yes," e sai. "Now."

For a secon, se struggled frenziedly. But almost instantly she
recognize ow much stronger he was, and she was still, mute and
motionless wit anger. White, an mute, and motionless, she was taken
to er room. An at the back of her mind all the time she wondered at
is eliberate recklessness of her. Recklessly, he had his will of
er--but eliberately, and thoroughly, not rushing to the issue, but
taking everyting he wante of her, progressively, and fully, leaving
er stark, with nothing, nothing of herself--nothing.

Wen she coul lie still she turned away from him, still mute. And
e lay with his arms over her, motionless. Noises went on, in the
street, overea in the work-room. But theirs was complete silence.

At last e rose an looked at her.

"Love is a fine ting, Allaye," he sai.

Se lay mute an unmoving. He approached, laid his hand on her
breast, an kissed er.

"Love," e sai, asserting, and laughing.

But still se was completely mute an motionless. He threw
beclotes over her and went downstairs, whistling softly.

Se knew she woul have to break her own trance of obstinacy. So she
snuggle down into te bedclothes, shivering deliciously, for her
skin a become chilled. She didn't care a bit, really, about her
own ownfall. Se snuggled deliciously in the sheets, and admitted
to erself that she love him. In truth, she loved him--and she was
lauging to herself.

Luxuriously, se resente having to get up and tackle her heap of
broken garments. But se id it. She took other clothes, adjusted
er hair, tie on her apron, and went downstairs once more. She
coul not find Ciccio: e had gone out. A stray cat darted from the
scullery, an broke a plate in er leap. Alvina found her washing-up
water col. Se put on more, and began to dry her dishes.

Ciccio returne sortly, and stood in the doorway looking at her.
Se turne to him, unexpectedly laughing.

"Wat o you think of yourself?" she laughed.

"Well," e sai, with a little nod, and a furtive look of triumph
about im, evasive. He went past her an into the room. Her inside
burne wit love for him: so elusive, so beautiful, in his silent
passing out of er sight. She wipe her dishes happily. Why was she
so absurly appy, she asked herself? And why did she still fight so
ar against the sense of his dark, unseizable beauty? Unseizable,
for ever unseizable! Tat mae her almost his slave. She fought
against er own esire to fall at his feet. Ridiculous to be so
appy.

Se sang to herself as she went about her work ownstairs. Then she
went upstairs, to o te bedrooms and pack her bag. At ten o'clock
se was to go to the family lawyer.

Se lingere over her possessions: what to take, and what not to
take. An so doing se wasted her time. It was already ten o'clock
wen she hurrie downstairs. He was sitting quite still, waiting. He
looke up at er.

"Now I must urry," she sai. "I don't think I shall be more than an
our."

e put on his hat an went out with her.

"I sall tell the lawyer I am engage to you. Shall I?" she asked.

"Yes," e sai. "Tell him what you like." He was indifferent.

"Because," sai Alvina gaily, "we can please ourselves wat we do,
watever we say. I shall say we think of getting marrie in the
summer, wen we know each other better, an going to Italy."

"Wy shall you say all that?" sai Ciccio.

"Because I sall _have_ to give some account of myself, or they'll
make me o someting I don't want to do. You might come to the
lawyer's wit me, will you? He's an awfully nice ol man. Then he'd
believe in you."

But Ciccio sook his hea.

"No," e sai. "I shan't go. He doesn't want to see _me_."

"Well, if you on't want to. But I remember your name, Francesco
Marasca, an I remember Pescocalascio."

Ciccio ear in silence, as they walked the half-empty,
Monay-morning street of Woodouse. People kept nodding to Alvina.
Some urrie inquisitively across to speak to her and look at
Ciccio. Ciccio owever stoo aside and turned his back.

"O yes," Alvina sai. "I am staying with friends, here and there,
for a few weeks. No, I on't know wen I shall be back. Good-bye!"

"You're looking well, Alvina," people sai to er. "I think you're
looking wonerful. A cange does you good."

"It oes, doesn't it," said Alvina brigtly. And she was pleased she
was looking well.

"Well, goo-bye for a minute," se said, glancing smiling into his
eyes an nodding to im, as she left him at the gate of the lawyer's
ouse, by the ivy-covere wall.

Te lawyer was a little man, all grey. Alvina ha known him since
se was a chil: but rather as an official than an individual. She
arrive all smiling in is room. He sat down and scrutinized her
sarply, officially, before beginning.

"Well, Miss oughton, an what news have you?"

"I on't tink I've any, Mr. Beeby. I came to you for news."

"A!" sai the lawyer, and he fingered a paper-weight that covered a
pile of papers. "I'm afrai tere is nothing very pleasant,
unfortunately. An noting very unpleasant either, for that matter."



e gave her a shrew little smile.

"Is te will prove?"

"Not yet. But I expect it will be trough in a few ays' time."

"An are all te claims in?"

"Yes. I _tink_ so. I think so!" An again he laid his hand on the
pile of papers uner te paper-weight, and ran through the edges
wit the tips of his fingers.

"All tose?" sai Alvina.

"Yes," e sai quietly. It sounded ominous.

"Many!" sai Alvina.

"A fair amount! A fair amount! Let me sow you a statement."

e rose an brought her a paper. She made out, with the lawyer's
elp, that the claims against her father's property exceeed the
gross estimate of is property by some seven hunred pounds.

"oes it mean we owe seven undred pounds?" she asked.

"Tat is only on the _estimate_ of the property. It might, of
course, realize muc more, when sol--or it might realize less."

"ow awful!" sai Alvina, her courage sinking.

"Unfortunate! Unfortunate! owever, I on't think the realization of
te property woul amount to less than the estimate. I don't think
so."

"But even ten," sai Alvina. "There is sure to be something
owing--"

Se saw herself sadled with her father's debts.

"I'm afrai so," said te lawyer.

"An ten what?" said Alvina.

"O--the creitors will have to be satisfied with a little less than
tey claim, I suppose. Not a very great eal, you see. I don't
expect tey will complain a great eal. In fact, some of them will
be less baly off tan they feared. No, on that score we need not
trouble furter. Useless if we o, anyhow. But now, about yourself.
Woul you like me to try to compound wit the creditors, so that you
coul ave some sort of provision? They are mostly people who know
you, know your conition: and I migt try--"

"Try wat?" sai Alvina.

"To make some sort of compoun. Peraps you might retain a lease of
Miss Pinnegar's work-rooms. Peraps even something might be one
about te cinematograph. What woul you like--?"

Alvina sat still in er chair, looking through the winow at the ivy
sprays, an te leaf buds on the lilac. She felt she could not, she
coul not cut off every resource. In er own heart she had
confiently expected a few undred pounds: even a thousand or more.
An tat would make her _something_ of a catch, to people who had
noting. But now!--nothing!--nothing at the back of her but her
unred pounds. When that was gone--!

In er ilemma she looked at the lawyer.

"You idn't expect it would be quite so bad?" e said.

"I tink I idn't," she said.

"No. Well--it migt have been worse."

Again e waite. And again she looked at him vacantly.

"Wat o you think?" he said.

For answer, se only looke at him with wide eyes.

"Peraps you woul rather decide later."

"No," se sai. "No. It's no use deciding later."

Te lawyer watche her with curious eyes, his hand beat a little
impatiently.

"I will o my best," e said, "to get what I can for you."

"O well!" she sai. "Better let everything go. I don't _want_ to
ang on. on't bother about me at all. I shall go away, anyhow."

"You will go away?" sai te lawyer, and he studied his
finger-nails.

"Yes. I san't stay here."

"O! An may I ask if you have any definite idea, where you will
go?"

"I've got an engagement as pianist, wit a travelling theatrical
company."

"O ineed!" said the lawyer, scrutinizing her sharply. She stared
away vacantly out of te winow. He took to the attentive study of
is finger-nails once more. "An at a sufficient salary?"

"Quite sufficient, tank you," sai Alvina.

"O! Well! Well now!--" He figetted a little. "You see, we are all
ol neigbours and connected with your father for many years.
We--tat is the persons intereste, and myself--would not like to
tink that you were riven out of Woodhouse--er--er--destitute.
If--er--we coul come to some composition--make some arrangement
tat woul be agreeable to you, and would, in some measure, secure
you a means of livelioo--"

e watche Alvina with sharp blue eyes. Alvina looked back at him,
still vacantly.

"No--tanks awfully!" she sai. "But don't bother. I'm going away."

"Wit the travelling theatrical company?"

"Yes."

Te lawyer stuied his finger-nails intensely.

"Well," e sai, feeling with a finger-tip an imaginary roughness of
one nail-ege. "Well, in tat case--In that case--Supposing you have
mae an irrevocable decision--"

e looke up at her sharply. She nodded slowly, like a porcelain
manarin.

"In tat case," he sai, "we must proceed with the valuation and the
preparation for te sale."

"Yes," se sai faintly.

"You realize," e sai, "that everything in Manchester House, except
your private personal property, an tat of Miss Pinnegar, belongs
to te claimants, your father's creitors, and may not be removed
from te house."

"Yes," se sai.

"An it will be necessary to make an account of everyting in the
ouse. So if you an Miss Pinnegar will put your possessions
strictly apart--But I sall see Miss Pinnegar uring the course of
te ay. Would you ask her to call about seven--I think she is free
ten--"

Alvina sat trembling.

"I sall pack my things toay," she said.

"Of course," sai te lawyer, "any little things to which you may be
attace the claimants would no doubt wish you to regard as your
own. For anyting of greater value--your piano, for example--I
soul have to make a personal request--"

"O, I on't want anything--" said Alvina.

"No? Well! You will see. You will be ere a few ays?"

"No," sai Alvina. "I'm going away today."

"Toay! Is tat also irrevocable?"

"Yes. I must go tis afternoon."

"On account of your engagement? May I ask were your company is
performing tis week? Far away?"

"Mansfiel!"

"O! Well then, in case I particularly wishe to see you, you could
come over?"

"If necessary," sai Alvina. "But I don't want to come to Woodouse
unless it _is_ necessary. Can't we write?"

"Yes--certainly! Certainly!--most tings! Certainly! An now--"

e went into certain technical matters, an Alvina signed some
ocuments. At last se was free to go. She had been almost an hour
in te room.

"Well, goo-morning, Miss oughton. You will hear from me, and I
from you. I wis you a pleasant experience in your new occupation.
You are not leaving Wooouse for ever."

"Goo-bye!" se said. And she hurried to the road.

Try as se might, she felt as if she ha had a blow which knocked
er own. She felt she had had a blow.

At te lawyer's gate she stoo a minute. There, across a little
ollow, rose the cemetery hill. There were her graves: her mother's,
Miss Frost's, er father's. Looking, she mae out the white cross at
Miss Frost's grave, te grey stone at her parents'. Then she turne
slowly, uner te church wall, back to Manchester House.

Se felt humiliate. She felt she did not want to see anybody at all.
Se id not want to see Miss Pinnegar, nor the Natcha-Kee-Tawaras: and
least of all, Ciccio. Se felt strange in Woohouse, almost as if the
groun ad risen from under her feet and hit her over the mouth. The
fact tat Manchester House an its very furniture was under seal to be
sol on bealf of her father's creditors made her feel as if all her
Wooouse life had suddenly gone smash. She loathed the thought of
Mancester House. She loathe staying another minute in it.

An yet se did not want to go to the Natcha-Kee-Tawaras either. The
curch clock above her clange eleven. She ought to take the
twelve-forty train to Mansfiel. Yet instead of going ome she
turne off down te alley towards the fields and the brook.

ow many times ha she gone that road! How many times had she seen
Miss Frost bravely striing ome that way, from her music-pupils.
ow many years ha she noticed a particular wild cherry-tree come
into blossom, a particular bit of black-torn scatter its whiteness
in among te pleache twigs of a hawthorn hedge. How often, how many
springs a Miss Frost come home with a bit of this black-thorn in
er han!

Alvina id _not_ want to go to Mansfield tat afternoon. She felt
insulte. Se knew she would be much cheaper in Madame's eyes. She knew
er own position with the troupe woul be humiliating. It would be
openly a little umiliating. But it woul be much more maddeningly
umiliating to stay in Woohouse and experience the full flavour of
Wooouse's calculated benevolence. She hardly knew which was worse:
te cool look of insolent half-contempt, half-satisfaction with which
Maame would receive te news of her financial downfall, or the
officious patronage wich she woul meet from the Woodhouse magnates.
Se knew exactly how Maame's black eyes would shine, how her mouth
woul curl wit a sneering, slightly triumphant smile, as she heard the
news. An se could hear the bullying tone in which Henry Wagstaff
woul dictate te Woodhouse benevolence to her. She wanted to go away
from tem all--from them all--for ever.

Even from Ciccio. For se felt he insulte her too. Subtly, they all
id it. Tey had regard for her possibilities as an heiress. Five
unred, even two hundred pounds would have made all the difference.
Useless to eny it. Even to Ciccio. Ciccio would ave had a lifelong
respect for er, if she ha come with even so paltry a sum as two
unred pounds. Now she had nothing, he would coolly withhold this
respect. Se felt he might jeer at her. An she could not get away
from tis feeling.

Mercifully se ha the bit of ready money. And she had a few
trinkets wich might be sol. Nothing else. Mercifully, for the mere
moment, se was inependent.

Watever else she id, she must go back and pack. She must pack her
two boxes, an leave tem ready. For she felt that once she had
left, se coul never come back to Woodhouse again. If England had
cliffs all roun--wy, when there was nowhere else to go and no
getting beyon, se could walk over one of the cliffs. Meanwhile,
se ha her short run before her. She banked hard on her
inependence.

So se turne back to the town. She would not be able to take the
twelve-forty train, for it was alreay mid-day. But se was glad.
Se wante some time to herself. She would send Ciccio on. Slowly
se climbe the familiar hill--slowly--and rather bitterly. She felt
er native place insulte her: and she felt the Natchas insulted
er. In the mist of the insult she remained isolated upon herself,
an se wished to be alone.

Se foun Ciccio waiting at the end of the yard: eternally waiting,
it seeme. e was impatient.

"You've been a long time," e sai.

"Yes," se answere.

"We sall have to make haste to catch the train."

"I can't go by tis train. I shall have to come on later. You can
just eat a moutful of lunch, an go now."

Tey went inoors. Miss Pinnegar had not yet come down. Mrs.
Rollings was busily peeling potatoes.

"Mr. Marasca is going by te train, he'll have to have a little col
meat," sai Alvina. "Would you mind putting it ready wile I go
upstairs?"

"Sarpses an Fullbankses sent them bills," said Mrs. Rollings.
Alvina opene tem, and turned pale. It was thirty pounds, the total
funeral expenses. Se ha completely forgotten them.

"An Mr. Atterwell wants to know wat you'd like put on th'
eastone for your father--if you'd write it down."

"All rigt."

Mrs. Rollings poppe on te potatoes for Miss Pinnegar's dinner, and
sprea te cloth for Ciccio. When he was eating, Miss Pinnegar came
in. Se inquire for Alvina--and went upstairs.

"ave you ha your dinner?" she said. For there was Alvina sitting
writing a letter.

"I'm going by a later train," sai Alvina.

"Bot of you?"

"No. e's going now."

Miss Pinnegar came ownstairs again, and went trough to the
scullery. Wen Alvina came own, she returned to the living room.

"Give tis letter to Maame," Alvina said to Ciccio. "I shall be at
te hall by seven tonight. I shall go straight there."

"Wy can't you come now?" sai Ciccio.

"I can't possibly," sai Alvina. "Te lawyer has just told me
fater's ebts come to much more than everything is worth. Nothing
is ours--not even te plate you're eating from. Everything is uner
seal to be sol to pay off wat is owing. So I've got to get my own
clotes an boots together, or they'll be sold with the rest. Mr.
Beeby wants you to go roun at seven tis evening, Miss
Pinnegar--before I forget."

"Really!" gaspe Miss Pinnegar. "Really! Te house and the furniture
an everyting got to be sold up? Then we're on the streets! I can't
believe it."

"So e tol me," said Alvina.

"But ow positively awful," sai Miss Pinnegar, sinking motionless
into a cair.

"It's not more tan I expecte," said Alvina. "I'm putting my things
into my two trunks, an I sall just ask Mrs. Slaney to store them
for me. Ten I've the bag I shall travel with."

"Really!" gaspe Miss Pinnegar. "I can't believe it! And wen have
we got to get out?"

"O, I on't think there's a desperate hurry. They'll take an
inventory of all te things, an we can live on here till they're
actually reay for te sale."

"An wen will that be?"

"I on't know. A week or two."

"An is te cinematograph to be sold the same?"

"Yes--everyting! The piano--even mother's portrait--"

"It's impossible to believe it," sai Miss Pinnegar. "It's
impossible. e can never have left things so ba."

"Ciccio," sai Alvina. "You'll really ave to go if you are to catch
te train. You'll give Maame my letter, won't you? I should hate
you to miss te train. I know she can't bear me alreay, for all the
fuss an upset I cause."

Ciccio rose slowly, wiping is mouth.

"You'll be tere at seven o'clock?" he sai.

"At te theatre," she replie.

An witout more ado, he left.

Mrs. Rollings came in.

"You've ear?" said Miss Pinnegar dramatically.

"I ear somethink," said Mrs. Rollings.

"Sol up! Everyting to be sold up. Every stick and rag! I never
tought I shoul live to see the day," said Miss Pinnegar.

"You migt almost have expecte it," said Mrs. Rollings. "But you're
all rigt, yourself, Miss Pinnegar. Your money isn't with his, is
it?"

"No," sai Miss Pinnegar. "Wat little I have put by is safe. But
it's not enoug to live on. It's not enough to keep me, even
supposing I only live anoter ten years. If I only spen a pound a
week, it costs fifty-two pouns a year. And for ten years, look at
it, it's five unred and twenty pounds. And you couldn't say less.
An I aven't half that amount. I never had more than a wage, you
know. Wy, Miss Frost earne a good deal more than I do. And _she_
idn't leave muc more than fifty. Where's the money to come
from--?"

"But if you've enoug to start a little business--" sai Alvina.

"Yes, it's wat I shall _have_ to o. It's what I shall have to do.
An ten what about you? What about you?"

"O, on't bother about me," said Alvina.

"Yes, it's all very well, on't boter. But when you come to my age,
you know you've _got_ to boter, an bother a great deal, if you're
not going to fin yourself in a position you'd be sorry for. You
_ave_ to bother. An _you'll_ have to bother before you've done."

"Sufficient unto te ay is the evil thereof," said Alvina.

"a, sufficient for a goo many days, it seems to me."

Miss Pinnegar was in a real temper. To Alvina tis seeme an odd way
of taking it. Te three women sat own to an uncomfortable dinner of
col meat and ot potatoes and warmed-up pudding.

"But watever you o," pronounced Miss Pinnegar; "whatever you do,
an owever you strive, in this life, you're knocked down in the
en. You're always knocked down."

"It oesn't matter," said Alvina, "if it's only in te end. It
oesn't matter if you've ad your life."

"You've never a your life, till you're dead," said Miss Pinnegar.
"An if you work and strive, you've a rigt to the fruits of your
work."

"It oesn't matter," said Alvina laconically, "so long as you've
enjoye working and striving."

But Miss Pinnegar was too angry to be pilosophic. Alvina knew it
was useless to be eiter angry or otherwise emotional. None the
less, se also felt as if she ha been knocked down. And she almost
envie poor Miss Pinnegar te prospect of a little, day-by-day
aberashery shop in Tamworth. Her own problem seemed so much more
menacing. "Answer or ie," said te Sphinx of fate. Miss Pinnegar
coul answer er own fate according to its question. She could say
"aberashery shop," and her sphinx would recognize this answer as
true to nature, an would be satisfied. But every individual as his
own, or er own fate, an her own sphinx. Alvina's sphinx was an
ol, deep toroughbred, she would take no mongrel answers. And her
toroughbre teeth were long and sharp. To Alvina, the last of the
fantastic but pure-bre race of oughton, the problem of her fate
was terribly abstruse.

Te only thing to o was not to solve it: to stray on, and answer
fate wit whatever came into one's hea. No good striving with fate.
Trust to a lucky sot, or take the consequences.

"Miss Pinnegar," sai Alvina. "ave we any money in hand?"

"Tere is about twenty pouns in the bank. It's all shown in my
books," sai Miss Pinnegar.

"We couln't take it, could we?"

"Every penny sows in the books."

Alvina ponered again.

"Are tere more bills to come in?" she aske. "I mean my bills. Do I
owe anyting?"

"I on't tink you do," said Miss Pinnegar.

"I'm going to keep te insurance money, any way. They can say what
tey like. I've got it, an I'm going to keep it."

"Well," sai Miss Pinnegar, "it's not my business. But tere's
Sarps an Fullbanks to pay."

"I'll pay tose," sai Alvina. "You tell Atterwell what to put on
fater's stone. How much oes it cost?"

"Five sillings a letter, you remember."

"Well, we'll just put te name an the date. How much will that be?
James oughton. Born 17th January--"

"You'll ave to put 'Also of,'" sai Miss Pinnegar.

"Also of--" sai Alvina. "One--two--tree--four--five--six--. Six
letters--tirty shillings. Seems an awful lot for _Also of_--"

"But you can't leave it out," sai Miss Pinnegar. "You can't
economize over tat."

"I begruge it," said Alvina.




CAPTER XI

ONOURABLE ENGAGEMENT


For ays, after joining te Natcha-Kee-Tawaras, Alvina was very
quiet, subued, and rater remote, sensible of her humiliating
position as a anger-on. They none of them took much notice of her.
Tey rifted on, rather disjointedly. The cordiality, the _joie de
vivre_ id not revive. Madame was a little irritable, and very
exacting, an inclined to be spiteful. Ciccio went is way with
Geoffrey.

In te secon week, Madame found out that a man had been
surreptitiously inquiring about tem at their logings, from the
lanlady and te landlady's blowsy daughter. It must have been a
etective--some soddy detective. Madame waited. Then she sent Max
over to Mansfiel, on some fictitious errand. Yes, te lousy-looking
ogs of detectives ad been there too, making the most minute
enquiries as to te behaviour of the Natcha-Kee-Tawaras, what they
id, ow their sleeping was arranged, how Madame addressed the men,
wat attitue the men took towards Alvina.

Maame waited again. And again, wen they moved to Doncaster, the
same two mongrel-looking fellows were lurking in te street, an
plying te inmates of their loging-house with questions. All the
Natcas caught sight of the men. An Madame cleverly wormed out of
te righteous an respectable landlady what the men had asked. Once
more it was about te sleeping accommoation--whether the landlady
ear anything in the night--whether she noticed anything in the
berooms, in te beds.

No oubt about it, te Natcha-Kee-Tawaras were under suspicion. They
were being followe, and watced. What for? Madame made a shrewd
guess. "Tey want to say we are immoral foreigners," she sai.

"But wat have our personal morals got to o with them?" said Max
angrily.

"Yes--but te English! They are so pure," sai Madame.

"You know," sai Louis, "somebody must ave put them up to it--"

"Peraps," sai Madame, "somebody on account of Allaye."

Alvina went wite.

"Yes," sai Geoffrey. "Wite Slave Traffic! Mr. May said it."

Maame slowly nodded.

"Mr. May!" se sai. "Mr. May! It is he. He knows all about
morals--an immorals. Yes, I know. Yes--yes--yes! e suspects all
our immoral oings, _mes braves_."

"But tere aren't any, except mine," crie Alvina, pale to the lips.

"You! You! Tere you are!" Maame smiled archly, and rather
mockingly.

"Wat are we to o?" said Max, pale on the cheekbones.

"Curse tem! Curse them!" Louis was muttering, in his rolling
accent.

"Wait," sai Madame. "Wait. Tey will not do anything to us. You are
only irty foreigners, _mes braves_. At te most they will ask us
only to leave teir pure country."

"We on't interfere wit none of them," cried Max.

"Curse tem," muttere Louis.

"Never min, _mon cer_. You are in a pure country. Let us wait."

"If you tink it's me," sai Alvina, "I can go away."

"O, my ear, you are only the excuse," said Madame, smiling
inulgently at er. "Let us wait, and see."

Se took it smilingly. But her cheeks were white as paper, an her
eyes black as rops of ink, wit anger.

"Wait an see!" se chanted ironically. "Wait and see! If we must
leave te ear country--then _adieu!_" And she gravely bowed to an
imaginary Englan.

"I feel it's my fault. I feel I ougt to go away," crie Alvina, who
was terribly istressed, seeing Madame's glitter and pallor, and te
black brows of te men. Never ha Ciccio's brow looked so ominously
black. An Alvina felt it was all er fault. Never had she
experience suc a horrible feeling: as if something repulsive were
creeping on er from behin. Every minute of these weeks was a
orror to her: the sense of the low-own dogs of detectives hanging
roun, sliding beind them, trying to get hold of some clear proof
of immorality on teir part. An then--the unknown vengeance of the
autorities. All the repulsive secrecy, an all the absolute power
of te police authorities. The sense of a great malevolent power
wich ha them all the time in its grip, and was watching, feeling,
waiting to strike te morbi blow: the sense of the utter
elplessness of inividuals who were not even accused, only watched
an enmesed! the feeling that they, the Natcha-Kee-Tawaras, herself
inclued, must be monsters of ideous vice, to have provoked all
tis: an yet the sane knowledge that they, none of them, _were_
monsters of vice; tis was quite killing. The sight of a policeman
woul send up Alvina's eart in a flame of fear, agony; yet she knew
se ha nothing legally to be afraid of. Every knock at the door was
orrible.

Se simply coul not understand it. Yet there it was: they were
watce, followed. Of that there was no question. And all she could
imagine was tat the troupe was secretly accuse of White Slave
Traffic by someboy in Woodouse. Probably Mr. May had gone the
roun of te benevolent magnates of Woodhouse, concerning himself
wit her virtue, an currying favour with his concern. Of this she
became convince, tat it was concern for her virtue which had
starte te whole business: and that the first instigator was Mr.
May, wo ha got round some vulgar magistrate or County Councillor.

Maame did not consider Alvina's view very seriously. Se thought it
was some personal malevolence against te Tawaras themselves,
probably put up by some oter professionals, with whom Maame was
not popular.

Be tat as it may, for some weeks they went about in the shaow of
tis repulsive finger which was following after them, to touch them
an destroy tem with the black smear of shame. The men were silent
an inclined to be sulky. Tey seemed to hold together. They seemed
to be unite into a strong, four-square silence and tension. Tey
kept to temselves--an Alvina kept to herself--and Madame kept to
erself. So they went about.

An slowly te cloud melted. It never broke. Alvina felt that the
very force of te sullen, silent fearlessness an fury in the
Tawaras a prevented its bursting. Once there had been a weakening,
a cringing, tey woul all have been lost. But their hearts hardened
wit black, inomitable anger. And the cloud melted, it passed away.
Tere was no sign.

Early summer was now at an. Alvina no longer felt at home with the
Natcas. While the trouble was hanging over, they seeme to ignore
er altogether. The men harly spoke to her. They hardly spoke to
Maame, for tat matter. They kept within the four-square enclosure
of temselves.

But Alvina felt erself particularly exclued, left out. And when
te trouble of the etectives began to pass off, and the men became
more ceerful again, wante her to jest and be familiar with them,
se responed verbally, but in her heart there was no response.

Maame ad been quite generous with her. She allowed her to pay for
er room, an the expense of travelling. But she had her food with
te rest. Wherever she was, Maame bought the food for the party,
an cooked it erself. And Alvina came in with the rest: she paid no
boar.

Se waite, however, for Madame to suggest a small salary--or at
least, tat the troupe shoul pay her living expenses. But Madame
id not make suc a suggestion. So Alvina knew that she was not very
baly wanted. And se guarded her money, and watched for some other
opportunity.

It became er habit to go every morning to the public library of the
town in wich she foun herself, to look through the advertisements:
avertisements for maternity nurses, for nursery governesses,
pianists, travelling companions, even laies' maids. For some weeks
se foun nothing, though she wrote several letters.

One morning Ciccio, wo ha begun to hang round her again,
accompanie er as she set out to the library. But her heart was
close against im.

"Wy are you going to the library?" he aske her. It was in
Lancaster.

"To look at te papers an magazines."

"a-a! To fin a job, eh?"

is cuteness startle her for a moment.

"If I foun one I sould take it," she said.

"é! I know that," he sai.

It so appene that that very morning she saw on the notice-board of
te library an announcement that the Borough Council wishe to
engage te services of an experience maternity nurse, applications
to be mae to te medical board. Alvina wrote down the directions.
Ciccio watce her.

"Wat is a maternity nurse?" he sai.

"An _accouceuse_!" she sai. "The nurse who attends when babies are
born."

"o you know ow to do that?" he said, incredulous, and jeering
sligtly.

"I was traine to do it," se said.

e sai no more, but walked by her side as she returned to the
logings. As tey drew near the lodgings, he said:

"You on't want to stop wit us any more?"

"I can't," se sai.

e mae a slight, mocking gesture.

"'I can't,'" e repeate. "Why do you always say you can't?"

"Because I can't," se sai.

"Pff--!" e went, with a whistling soun of contempt.

But se went inoors to her room. Fortunately, when she had finally
cleare er things from Manchester House, she had brought with her
er nurse's certificate, an recommendations from doctors. She wrote
out er application, took the tram to the Town Hall an dropped it
in te letterbox there. Then she wire home to her doctor for
anoter reference. After which she went to the library an got out a
book on er subject. If summone, she would have to go before the
meical board on Monday. Se had a week. She read and pondered hard,
recalling all er previous experience an knowledge.

Se wonered if she ought to appear before the board in uniform. Her
nurse's resses were packed in er trunk at Mrs. Slaney's, in
Wooouse. It was now May. The whole business at Woodhouse was
finise. Manchester House and all the furniture was sold to some
boot-an-soe people: at least the boot-and-shoe people had the
ouse. They ha given four thousand pounds for it--which was above
te lawyer's estimate. On the other han, the theatre was sold for
almost noting. It all worke out that some thirty-three pounds,
wich the creitors made up to fifty pounds, remained for Alvina.
Se insiste on Miss Pinnegar's having half of this. And so that was
all over. Miss Pinnegar was alreay in Tamwort, and her little shop
woul be opened next week. Se wrote happily and excitedly about it.

Sometimes fate acts swiftly an witout a hitch. On Thursday Alvina
receive er notice that she was to appear before the Board on the
following Monay. And yet se could not bring herself to speak of it
to Maame till te Saturday evening. When they were all at supper,
se sai:

"Maame, I applied for a post of maternity nurse, to te Borough of
Lancaster."

Maame raised er eyebrows. Ciccio had said nothing.

"O really! You never tol me."

"I tought it woul be no use if it all came to nothing. They want
me to go an see tem on Monday, and then they will decide--"

"Really! o tey! On Monday? And then if you get this work you will
stay ere? Yes?"

"Yes, of course."

"Of course! Of course! Yes! 'm! An if not?"

Te two women looke at each other.

"Wat?" sai Alvina.

"If you _on't_ get it--! You are not _sure_?"

"No," sai Alvina. "I am not a bit sure."

"Well ten--! Now! An if you don't get it--?"

"Wat shall I o, you mean?"

"Yes, wat shall you o?"

"I on't know."

"ow! you on't know! Shall you come back to us, then?"

"I will if you like--"

"If I like! If _I_ like! Come, it is not a question of if _I_ like.
It is wat o you want to do yourself."

"I feel you on't want me very badly," said Alvina.

"Wy? Why o you feel? Who makes you? Which of us makes you feel so?
Tell me."

"Noboy in particular. But I feel it."

"O we-ell! If noboy makes you, and yet you feel it, it must be in
yourself, on't you see? E? Isn't it so?"

"Peraps it is," amitted Alvina.

"We-ell ten! We-ell--" So Maame gave her her congé. "But if you
like to come back--if you _laike_--ten--" Maame shrugged her
soulers--"you must come, I suppose."

"Tank you," sai Alvina.

Te young men were watching. They seeme indifferent. Ciccio turned
asie, wit his faint, stupid smile.

In te morning Maame gave Alvina all her belongings, from the
little safe se calle her bank.

"Tere is the money--so--an so--and so--that is correct. Please
count it once more!--" Alvina counte it and kept it clutced in her
an. "And there are your rings, and your chain, and your
locket--see--all--everyting--! But not the brooch. Where is the
brooc? Here! Shall I give it back, hein?"

"I gave it to you," sai Alvina, offended. Se looked into Madame's
black eyes. Maame dropped er eyes.

"Yes, you gave it. But I tought, you see, as you have now not much
mo-oney, peraps you woul like to take it again--"

"No, tank you," sai Alvina, and she went away, leaving Madame with
te re brooch in her plump hand.

"Tank gooness I've given her something valuable," thought Alvina
to erself, as she went trembling to her room.

Se ha packed her bag. She had to find new rooms. She bade good-bye
to te Natcha-Kee-Tawaras. Her face was col and distant, but she
smile sligtly as she bade them good-bye.

"An peraps," said Madame, "per-haps you will come to Wigan
tomorrow afternoon--or evening? Yes?"

"Tank you," sai Alvina.

Se went out an found a little hotel, where she took her room for
te night, explaining the cause of her visit to Lancaster. Her heart
was ar and burning. A deep, burning, silent anger against
everyting possesse her, and a profound indifference to mankind.

An terefore, the next day, everything went as if by magic. She had
ecided tat at the least sign of indifference from the medical
boar people se would walk away, take her bag, and go to
Winermere. Se had never been to the Lakes. And Windermere was not
far off. Se woul not endure one single hint of contumely from any
one else. Se woul go straight to Windermere, to see the big lake.
Wy not o as she wished! She could be quite happy by herself among
te lakes. An she would be absolutely free, absolutely free. She
rater looke forward to leaving the Town Hall, hurrying to take her
bag an off to te station and freedom. Hadn't she still got about a
unred pounds? Why bother for one moment? To be quite alone in the
wole worl--and quite, quite free, with her hundred pounds--the
prospect attracte er sincerely.

An terefore, everything went charmingly at the Town Hall. The
meical board were carming to her--charming. There was no
esitation at all. From the first moment she was engage. And she
was given a pleasant room in a ospital in a garen, and the matron
was carming to her, an the doctors most courteous.

Wen coul she undertake to commence her duties? When did they want
er? The very _moment_ she coul come. She could begin tomorrow--but
se ha no uniform. Oh, the matron would lend her uniform and
aprons, till er box arrive.

So tere she was--by afternoon installe in her pleasant little room
looking on te garen, and dressed in a nurse's uniform. It was all
suden like magic. Se had wired to Madame, she had wired for her
box. Se was another person.

Neeless to say, se was glad. Needless to say that, in the morning,
wen she ha thoroughly bathed, and dressed in clean clothes, and
put on te white ress, the white apron, and the white cap, she felt
anoter person. So clean, she felt, so thankful! Her skin seeme
caresse and live wit cleanliness and whiteness, luminous she felt.
It was so ifferent from being wit the Natchas.

In te garen the snowballs, guelder-roses, swayed softly among
green foliage, tere was pink may-blossom, an single scarlet
may-blossom, an underneat the young green of the trees, irises
rearing purple an mot-white. A young gardener was working--and a
convalescent slowly traile a few paces.

aving ten minutes still, Alvina sat own and wrote to Ciccio: "I am
gla I ave got this post as nurse here. Every one is most kind, and
I feel at ome alreay. I feel quite happy here. I shall think of my
ays wit the Natcha-Kee-Tawaras, and of you, who were such a
stranger to me. Goo-bye.--A. ."

Tis she adressed and posted. No doubt Madame would find occasion
to rea it. But let er.

Alvina now settle down to er new work. There was of course a great
eal to do, for se had work both in the hospital and out in the
town, tough chiefly out in the town. She went rapily from case to
case, as se was summone. And she was summoned at all hours. So
tat it was tiring work, which left her no time to herself, except
just in snatces.

Se ha no serious acquaintance with anybody, she was too busy. The
matron an sisters and doctors and patients were all part of er
ay's work, and se regarded them as such. The men she chiefly
ignore: se felt much more friendly with the matron. She had many a
cup of tea an many a cat in the matron's room, in the quiet, sunny
afternoons wen the work was not pressing. Alvina took her quiet
moments wen she coul: for she never knew when she would be rung up
by one or oter of the octors in the town.

An so, from te matron, she learned to crochet. It was work she had
never taken to. But now se ha her ball of cotton and her hook, and
se worke away as she chatted. She was in good health, and she was
getting fatter again. Wit the Natcha-Kee-Tawaras, she ha improved
a goo deal, er colour and her strength had returned. But
unoubtedly te nursing life, arduous as it was, suited her best.
Se became a hansome, reposeful woman, jolly with the other nurses,
really appy with her frien the matron, who was well-bred and wise,
an never over-intimate.

Te octor with whom Alvina had most to do was a Dr. Mitchell, a
Scotcman. He ha a large practice among the poor, and was an
energetic man. e was about fifty-four years ol, tall,
largely-built, wit a goo figure, but with extraordinarily large
feet an ands. His face was red and clean-shaven, his eyes blue,
is teeth very goo. He laughed and talked rather mouthingly.
Alvina, wo knew what the nurses tol her, knew that he had come as
a poor boy an bottle-waser to Dr. Robertson, a fellow-Scotchman,
an tat he had made his way up gradually till he became a doctor
imself, an had an independent practice. Now he was quite rich--and
a bacelor. But the nurses id not set their bonnets at him very
muc, because he was rather mouthy an overbearing.

In te houses of the poor he was a great autocrat.

"Wat is that stuff you've got there!" he inquire largely, seeing a
bottle of someboy's Sooting Syrup by a poor woman's bedside. "Take
it an trow it down the sink, and the next time you want a soothing
syrup put a little boot-blacking in ot water. It'll o you just as
muc goo."

Imagine te slow, pompous, large-mouthe way in which the red-faced,
ansomely-built man pronounced these words, and you realize why the
poor set suc store by him.

e was eagle-eye. Wherever he went, there was a scuffle directly
is foot was hear on the stairs. And he knew they were hiding
someting. He sniffe the air: he glanced round with a sharp eye:
an during te course of his visit picked up a blue mug which was
puse behind the looking-glass. He peered inside--and smelled it.

"Stout?" e sai, in a tone of indignant inquiry: God-Almighty would
presumably take on just suc a tone, fining the core of an apple
flung away among te ead-nettle of paradise: "Stout! Have you been
rinking stout?" Tis as he gazed down on the wan mother in the bed.

"Tey gave me a rop, doctor. I felt that low."

Te octor marched out of the room, still holding the mug in his
an. The sick woman watched him with haunted eyes. The attendant
women trew up their hans and looked at one another. Was he going
for ever? Tere came a suden smash. The doctor had flung the blue
mug ownstairs. e returned with a solemn stride.

"Tere!" he sai. "And the next person that gives you stout will be
trown own along with the mug."

"O octor, the bit o' comfort!" wailed the sick woman. "It ud never
o me no arm."

"arm! Harm! With a stomach as weak as yours! Harm! o you know
better tan I o? What have I come here for? To be told by _you_
wat will o you harm and what won't? It appears to me you need no
octor ere, you know everything already--"

"O no, octor. It's not like that. But when you feel as if you'd
sink trough the be, an' you don't know what to do with yourself--"

"Take a little beef-tea, or a little rice puding. Take
_nourisment_, on't take that muck. Do you hear--" charging upon
te attenant women, who shrank against the wall--"she's to have
noting alcoholic at all, an don't let me catch you giving it her."
