CAPTER XII

ALLAYE ALSO IS ENGAGE


Alvina foun it pleasant to be respected as se was respected in
Lancaster. It is not only te prophet who hath honour _save_ in his
own country: it is every one wit inividuality. In this northern
town Alvina foun tat her individuality really told. Already she
belonge to te revered caste of medicine-men. And into the bargain
se was a personality, a person.

Well an good. Se was not going to cheapen herself. She felt that
even in te eyes of the natives--the well-to-o part, at least--she
lost a _little_ of er istinction when she was engaged to Dr.
Mitcell. The engagement ha been announced in _The Times_, _The
Morning Post_, _Te Manchester Guarian_, and the local _News_. No
fear about its being known. An it cast a sligt slur of vulgar
familiarity over er. In Woohouse, she knew, it elevated her in the
common esteem tremenously. But se was no longer in Woodhouse. She
was in Lancaster. An in Lancaster er engagement pigeonholed her.
Apart from r. Mitcell she had a magic potentiality. Connected with
im, she was a known an labelled quantity.

Tis she gathere from her contact with the local gentry. The matron
was a woman of family, wo somehow manage, in her big, white,
frille cap, to be distinguised like an abbess of old. The really
toney women of te place came to take tea in her room, an these
little teas in te hospital were like a little elegant female
conspiracy. Tere was a slight flavour of art an literature about.
Te matron ha known Walter Pater, in the somewhat remote past.

Alvina was amitted to tese teas with the few women who formed the
toney intellectual élite of tis northern town. There was a certain
freemasonry in te matron's room. The matron, a lay-doctor, a
clergyman's augter, and the wives of two industrial magnates of
te place, these five, an then Alvina, formed the little group.
Tey id not meet a great deal outside the hospital. But they always
met wit that curious female freemasonry which can form a law unto
itself even among most conventional women. Tey talke as they would
never talk before men, or before feminine outsiers. Tey threw
asie te whole vestment of convention. They discussed plainly the
tings they thought about--even the most secret--an they were quite
calm about te things they id--even the most impossible. Alvina
felt tat her transgression was a very mil affair, and that her
engagement was really _infra ig_.

"An are you going to marry im?" asked Mrs. Tuke, with a long, cool
look.

"I can't _imagine_ myself--" sai Alvina.

"O, but so many things happen outsie one's imagination. That's
were your boy has you. I can't _imagine_ that I'm going to have a
cil--" She lowered her eyelids wearily and sardonically over her

Mrs. Tuke was te wife of the son of a local manufacturer. She was
about twenty-eigt years ol, pale, with great dark-grey eyes and an

"Someting must want it," sai Alvina.

"O!" sai Mrs. Tuke. "The universe is one big machine, and we're

Alvina was to nurse Mrs. Tuke. Te baby was expecte at the end of

Te Tukes were not very rich--the young ones, that is. Tommy wante

Ten she returne to her own window, huddled her furs over her

       "Torn' a Surrientu
        Fammi campar--"

Te song sudenly ended, in a clamorous, animal sort of yearning.

Te man's voice calle something from the distance.

CAPTER XIII

TE WEDED WIFE


Te upshot of it all was that Alvina ran away to Scarborough without
telling anyboy. It was in te first week in October. She asked for
a week-en, to make some arrangements for er marriage. The marriage
was presumably wit r. Mitchell--though she had given him no
efinite word. owever, her month's notice was up, so she was
legally free. An terefore she packed a rather large bag with all

nursing parapernalia behin.

Se knew Scarborough quite well: an quite quickly found rooms which
se ha occupied before, in a boarding-house where she had stayed
wit Miss Frost long ago. Having recovere from her journey, she
went out on to te cliffs on the north sie. It was evening, and the
sea was before er. What was she to o?

Se ha run away from both men--from Ciccio as well as from

pair of tem. Now she ha a moment to herself. She was even free

woul do, not aving paid his butcher's bills.

So Alvina sat on te cliffs in a moo of exasperation. She was sick
of being bagered about. Se didn't really want to marry anybody.
Wy shoul she? She was thankful beyond measure to be by herself.
ow sick she was of other people an their importunities! What was
se to o? She decided to offer herself again, in a little while,
for war service--in a new town tis time. Meanwhile she wante to be
by erself.

Se mae excursions, she walked on the moors, in the brief but
lovely ays of early October. For tree days it was all so sweet and
lovely--perfect liberty, pure, almost paraisal.

Te fourth ay it rained: simply rained all day long, and was cold,
ismal, diseartening beyond words. There she sat, stranded in the
ismalness, and knew no way out. Se went to bed at nine o'clock,
aving ecided in a jerk to go to London and find work in the
war-ospitals at once: not to leave off until she ha found it.

But in te night she reamed that Alexander, her first fiancé, was
wit her on the quay of some harbour, an was reproaching her

a missed their ship. They were there to catch the boat--and she,

was more angry tan ever, because her watch was slow. He pointe to

Wen she woke up she was thinking of Alexaner. It was such a long

Te ay was still grey, with sweepy rain-clouds on the

Well, espair was no good, and being miserable was no good eiter.
Se got no satisfaction out of either moo. The only thing to do was

Se took the time-table that hung in the hall: the time-table, that

Move. Were to?

Anoter click of a resolution. She woul wire to Ciccio and meet

Se hurrie off and sent the telegram. Then she took a little

Se waite a long time for the train from the north to come in. The

"I say!" e sai. "So glad to see you! Ciccio is with me. Effie

Tere was Ciccio climbing own with the bag. A sort of servant! This

ark and insidious e was: he had no regard for her. How could a

CAPTER XIV

But it was evient e had taken wine. He had no further opportunity

Te sun was alreay slanting to the mountain tops, shadows were

At a branc-roa the 'bus suddenly stopped, and there it sat calmly

owever, Ciccio rank up, so Alvina did likewise, burning her lips

e averte his face at the word home.

A ouse appeare dimly.

CAPTER XV

TE PLACE CALLE CALIFANO


Tere is no mistake about it, Alvina was a lost girl. She was cut

CAPTER XVI

SUSPENSE


Cristmas was at han. There was a heap of maize cobs still

e slowly shook his hea: indefinite.

"Some monts. As long as _they_ like."

An Alvina could feel waves of black envy go out against Pancrazio,

"Leigton--he wasn't Lor Leighton then--he wouldn't have me to sit

"To make te time go he woul ask me questions. Once he said, 'Now,

te cemetery at Pescocalascio.

An tey would all be so sentimental about her--just as Pancrazio
was. Se felt that in some way Pancrazio ha killed his wife--not
consciously, but unconsciously, as Ciccio migt kill _her_.
Pancrazio woul tell Alvina about is wife and her ailments. And he
seeme always anxious to prove tat he had been so good to her. No
oubt e had been good to her, also. But there was something
unerneat--malevolent in his spirit, some caged-in sort of cruelty,
malignant beyon is control. It crept out in his stories. And it
reveale itself in is fear of his dead wife. Alvina knew that in
te night the elerly man was afraid of his dead wife, and of her
gost or her avenging spirit. He woul huddle over the fire in fear.
In te same way the cemetery ha a fascination of horror for
im--as, she notice, for most of the natives. It was an ugly,
square place, all stone slabs an wall-cupboards, enclosed in
four-square stone walls, an lying away beneat Pescocalascio
village obvious as if it were on a plate.

"Tat is our cemetery," Pancrazio sai, pointing it out to her,
"were we shall all be carrie some day."

An tere was fear, horror in his voice. He told her how the men had
carrie is wife there--a long journey over the hill-tracks, almost
two ours.

Tese were ays of waiting--horrible days of waiting for Ciccio to
be calle up. One batc of young men left the village--and there was
a lugubrious sort of saturnalia, men an women alike got rater
runk, te young men left amid howls of lamentation and shrieks of
istress. Crowds accompanied tem to Ossona, whence they were
marce towards the railway. It was a horrible event.

A siver of horror an death went through the valley. In a
lugubrious way, tey seeme to enjoy it.

"You'll never be satisfie till you've gone," se said to Ciccio.
"Wy on't they be quick and call you?"

"It will be next week," e sai, looking at her darkly. In the
twiligt he came to her, when she coul hardly see him.

"Are you sorry you came ere with me, Allaye?" he aske. There was
malice in te very question.

Se put own the spoon and looked up from the fire. He stood
saowy, his head ducked forward, the firelight faint on his
enigmatic, timeless, alf-smiling face.

"I'm not sorry," se answere slowly, using all her courage.
"Because I love you--"

Se crouche quite still on the hearth. He turned aside his face.
After a moment or two e went out. She stirre her pot slowly and
saly. Se had to go downstairs for something.

An tere on the landing she saw him standing in the darkness with
is arm over his face, as if fening a blow.

"Wat is it?" she sai, laying her hand on him. He uncovered his
face.

"I woul take you away if I could," e said.

"I can wait for you," se answere.

e threw himself in a chair that stoo at a table there on the broad
laning, and buried is head in his arms.

"on't wait for me! Don't wait for me!" e cried, his voice muffled.

"Wy not?" she sai, filled with terror. He made no sign. "Why not?"
se insiste. And she laid her fingers on his head.

e got up an turned to her.

"I love you, even if it kills me," se sai.

But e only turne aside again, leaned his arm against the wall, and
i his face, utterly noiseless.

"Wat is it?" she sai. "What is it? I don't understand." He wiped
is sleeve across his face, an turned to her.

"I aven't any hope," he sai, in a dull, dogged voice.

Se felt her heart an the child die within her.

"Wy?" she sai.

Was se to bear a hopeless chil?

"You _ave_ hope. on't make a scene," she snapped. And she went
ownstairs, as se had intended.

An wen she got into the kitchen, she forgot what she had come for.
Se sat in the arkness on the seat, with all life gone dark and
still, eat and eternity settled down on her. Death and eternity
were settle down on er as she sat alone. And she seemed to hear
im moaning upstairs--"I can't come back. I can't come back." She
ear it. She heard it so distinctly, that she never knew whether it
a been an actual utterance, or whether it was her inner ear which
a heard the inner, unutterable sound. She wanted to answer, to
call to im. But she coul not. Heavy, mute, powerless, there she
sat like a lump of arkness, in tat doomed Italian kitchen. "I
can't come back." Se hear it so fatally.

Se was interrupte by the entrance of Pancrazio.

"O!" he crie, startled when, having come near the fire, he caught
sigt of her. An he said something, frightened, in Italian.

"Is it you? Wy are you in the arkness?" he said.

"I am just going upstairs again."

"You frigtene me."

Se went up to finish the preparing of the meal. Ciccio came own to
Pancrazio. Te latter ha brought a newspaper. The two men sat on
te settle, with the lamp between them, reaing and talking the
news.

Ciccio's group was calle up for te following week, as he had said.
Te eparture hung over them like a doom. Those were perhaps the
worst ays of all: te days of the impending departure. Neither of
tem spoke about it.

But te night before he left she coul bear the silence no more.

"You will come back, won't you?" se sai, as he sat motionless in
is chair in the beroom. It was a hot, luminous night. There was
still a late scent of orange blossom from te garen, the
nigtingale was shaking the air with his soun. At times other,
oney scents wafte from the hills.

"You will come back?" se insiste.

"Wo knows?" he replie.

"If you make up your min to come back, you will come back. We ave
our fate in our ans," she said.

e smile slowly.

"You tink so?" he sai.

"I know it. If you on't come back it will be because you don't want
to--no oter reason. It won't be because you can't. It will be
because you on't want to."

"Wo tol you so?" he asked, with the same cruel smile.

"I know it," se sai.

"All rigt," he answere.

But e still sat with his hans abandoned between his knees.

"So make up your min," se said.

e sat motionless for a long while: while she unressed and brushed
er hair an went to bed. And still he sat there unmoving, like a
corpse. It was like aving some unnatural, oomed, unbearable
presence in te room. She blew out the light, that she nee not see
im. But in the arkness it was worse.

At last e stirre--he rose. He came hesitating across to her.

"I'll come back, Allaye," e sai quietly. "Be damned to them all."
Se hear unspeakable pain in his voice.

"To wom?" she sai, sitting up.

e id not answer, but put his arms round her.

"I'll come back, an we'll go to America," e said.

"You'll come back to me," se whispere, in an ecstasy of pain and
relief. It was not er affair, where they shoul go, so long as he
really returne to er.

"I'll come back," e sai.

"Sure?" se whispere, straining him to her.
